The Obligation of Creedal Knowledge: Summary vs Detailed Evidence
The commentator states the obligation of creedal knowledge and distinguishes two types of evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between summary and detailed evidence in creed, and that knowing summary evidence is a personal obligation while detailed evidence is a communal obligation.

Core Idea
Knowing creed with summary evidence is fard ‘ayn; detailed evidence is fard kifaya.

This lesson clarifies the obligations concerning creedal knowledge. The commentator explains that every Muslim must know the basics of creed with at least simple evidence, but mastering the detailed reasoning is a communal duty. He also refutes the view that makes detailed evidence obligatory for all.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the Quranic verse used as evidence.
2
Identify the two obligations
Find where the commentator states what is fard 'ayn and what is fard kifaya. Note the basis from the verse.
3
Understand the rebuttal
Read the argument against those who made detailed evidence fard 'ayn. Why is their view rejected?
The commentator warns against narrowing God's mercy.
4
Reflect on the community aspect
Ask yourself why detailed evidence is communal. How does this protect the community from doubts?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مُحَتَّمٌ) أي: حتَّمه الشارعُ وأوجبه ، ولم يرخُص في تركه؛ لقوله تعالى : فاعلم أنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللهُ [محمد : ۱۹]، فيجب على كل مكلَّف من ذكر وأنثى وجوباً عينيا معرفةُ كلِّ عقيدة بدليل ولو إجماليًّا، وأما معرفتها بالدليل التفصيلي، ففرضُ كفاية ) ، فيجب على أهل كلِّ قطر أي ناحية يشقُ الوصول منها إلى غيرها أن يكونَ فيهم من يعرفها بالدليل التفصيلي ؛ لأنه ربما طرأت شبهة فيدفعها، وبعضهم أوجبَ الدليل التفصيلي وجوباً عينيا، وردُّوه بأنهم ضيقوا رحمة الله الواسعة)، وجعلوا الجنة مختصة بطائفة يسيرة. فالحق أن الواجب وجوباً عينيا إنما هو الدليل الإجمالي، وهو المعجوز عن تقريره وحلّ شبهه، وأما الدليل التفصيلي فهو المقدورُ على تقريره وحل شبهه ؛ فإذا قيل لك: ما الدليل على وجوده تعالى؟ فقلت العالم، ولم تعرف جهة الدلالة، فهو دليل جملي ويقال له : دليل إجمالي، وكذلك إذا عرفت جهة الدلالة ولم تقدر على حلّ الشبه الواردة عليه، وأما إذا عرفت جهة الدلالة وقدرت على حل الشبه، فهو دليل تفصيلي . فإذا قيل لك : ما الدليل على وجوده تعالى؟ فقلت هذا العالم، وعرفت جهةً الدلالة ؛ وهي الحدوث أو الإمكان أو هما ، والثاني شطر أو شرط، وقدرتَ على حل الشبه، فهو دليل تفصيلي
His saying: "(determined)" meaning: the Lawgiver determined it and obligated it, and allowed no concession in leaving it due to His saying: 'Know that there is no god but Allah' [Muhammad 19]. So it is personally obligatory upon every legally responsible person, male or female, to know every creed with evidence, even summary evidence. As for knowing it with detailed evidence, it is a communal obligation. It is obligatory upon the people of every region that there be among them those who know it with detailed evidence, because perhaps a doubt may arise and he may repel it. And some made detailed evidence personally obligatory, and they refuted it saying they narrowed the vast mercy of Allah and made Paradise exclusive to a small group. The truth is that what is personally obligatory is only summary evidence, which is that which one is unable to articulate and resolve its doubts. As for detailed evidence, it is that which one is able to articulate and resolve its doubts. So if you are asked: 'What is the evidence for His existence, exalted is He?' and you say 'the world' and you do not know the aspect of indication, it is summary evidence, and is called summary evidence. Likewise if you know the aspect of indication but are unable to resolve the doubts that arise. But if you know the aspect of indication and are able to resolve the doubts, it is detailed evidence. So if you are asked: 'What is the evidence for His existence?' and you say 'this world' and you know the aspect of indication, which is origination or contingency or both, and the second is a part or a condition, and you are able to resolve the doubts, it is detailed evidence.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage defines the obligation of learning creedal matters and distinguishes between two types of evidence. The commentator explains that knowing every creed with at least summary evidence is fard 'ayn (personal obligation) based on the verse 'Know that there is no god but Allah'. Detailed evidence is fard kifaya (communal obligation) – the community must have some people who can articulate it to repel doubts. Then he rejects the view of those who made detailed evidence fard 'ayn, arguing they narrow God's mercy. The true ruling is that summary evidence is fard 'ayn, detailed is fard kifaya. He then defines both: summary evidence is acknowledging the world as proof without knowing the logical connection (jiha al-dalalah) or being unable to answer doubts; detailed evidence includes knowing the connection and being able to resolve doubts. He gives the example of attributing origination or contingency to the world, with some nuance about which condition is required.

Key Terms
فرض عين
فرض كفاية
دليل إجمالي
دليل تفصيلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that detailed evidence is personally obligatory for every Muslim.
Correction: The commentator clarifies it is only communal (fard kifaya), and making it personal narrows God's mercy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for knowing creed with detailed evidence?
A) Fard 'ayn for all
B) Fard kifaya
C) Sunnah mu'akkada
D) Permissible

Key Takeaway
Knowing creed with summary evidence is a personal duty; detailed evidence is a communal duty to protect against doubts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you satisfied with merely knowing the basics of your creed, or do you feel the weight of the responsibility to delve deeper so you can defend it? What will you do to fulfill the communal duty if no one else in your community is doing it?
?
The commentator mentions that some people made detailed evidence personally obligatory and were refuted for narrowing God's mercy. Examine your own heart: have you ever judged others who do not have detailed knowledge as less worthy of Paradise? What does this passage teach you about God's mercy?
?
If you are honest, do you see learning detailed creedal evidence as a burden or a gift? What would change if you saw it as a protection against doubts that may arise in your own life?

Defining Summary and Detailed Evidence
The commentator defines summary and detailed evidence and provides an example using the world as proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of summary and detailed evidence, including the role of the aspect of indication and the ability to resolve doubts.

Core Idea
Summary evidence is knowing the world is proof; detailed evidence includes knowing why and defending it.

This lesson dives into the technical definitions. The commentator distinguishes two levels of evidence: one that simply points to the Creator, and another that fully articulates the logical connection and answers all objections. A concrete example is given using the world as proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the passage that defines summary and detailed evidence. Note the two criteria: knowing the aspect of indication and ability to resolve doubts.
2
Understand summary evidence
Summary evidence is when you either do not know the aspect of indication or cannot resolve doubts. The example: saying 'the world' without explanation.
3
Understand detailed evidence
Detailed evidence requires both knowing the aspect and being able to resolve doubts. The example: knowing the world's contingency or origination and defending it.
4
Examine the example
Trace the example given: when asked for evidence, you say 'the world' and specify the aspect (origination or contingency) and can resolve doubts. This is detailed evidence.
The commentator notes a nuance about which condition (shart or shatr) but the main point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مُحَتَّمٌ) أي: حتَّمه الشارعُ وأوجبه ، ولم يرخُص في تركه؛ لقوله تعالى : فاعلم أنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللهُ [محمد : ۱۹]، فيجب على كل مكلَّف من ذكر وأنثى وجوباً عينيا معرفةُ كلِّ عقيدة بدليل ولو إجماليًّا، وأما معرفتها بالدليل التفصيلي، ففرضُ كفاية ) ، فيجب على أهل كلِّ قطر أي ناحية يشقُ الوصول منها إلى غيرها أن يكونَ فيهم من يعرفها بالدليل التفصيلي ؛ لأنه ربما طرأت شبهة فيدفعها، وبعضهم أوجبَ الدليل التفصيلي وجوباً عينيا، وردُّوه بأنهم ضيقوا رحمة الله الواسعة)، وجعلوا الجنة مختصة بطائفة يسيرة. فالحق أن الواجب وجوباً عينيا إنما هو الدليل الإجمالي، وهو المعجوز عن تقريره وحلّ شبهه، وأما الدليل التفصيلي فهو المقدورُ على تقريره وحل شبهه ؛ فإذا قيل لك: ما الدليل على وجوده تعالى؟ فقلت العالم، ولم تعرف جهة الدلالة، فهو دليل جملي ويقال له : دليل إجمالي، وكذلك إذا عرفت جهة الدلالة ولم تقدر على حلّ الشبه الواردة عليه، وأما إذا عرفت جهة الدلالة وقدرت على حل الشبه، فهو دليل تفصيلي . فإذا قيل لك : ما الدليل على وجوده تعالى؟ فقلت هذا العالم، وعرفت جهةً الدلالة ؛ وهي الحدوث أو الإمكان أو هما ، والثاني شطر أو شرط، وقدرتَ على حل الشبه، فهو دليل تفصيلي
His saying: "(determined)" meaning: the Lawgiver determined it and obligated it, and allowed no concession in leaving it due to His saying: 'Know that there is no god but Allah' [Muhammad 19]. So it is personally obligatory upon every legally responsible person, male or female, to know every creed with evidence, even summary evidence. As for knowing it with detailed evidence, it is a communal obligation. It is obligatory upon the people of every region that there be among them those who know it with detailed evidence, because perhaps a doubt may arise and he may repel it. And some made detailed evidence personally obligatory, and they refuted it saying they narrowed the vast mercy of Allah and made Paradise exclusive to a small group. The truth is that what is personally obligatory is only summary evidence, which is that which one is unable to articulate and resolve its doubts. As for detailed evidence, it is that which one is able to articulate and resolve its doubts. So if you are asked: 'What is the evidence for His existence, exalted is He?' and you say 'the world' and you do not know the aspect of indication, it is summary evidence, and is called summary evidence. Likewise if you know the aspect of indication but are unable to resolve the doubts that arise. But if you know the aspect of indication and are able to resolve the doubts, it is detailed evidence. So if you are asked: 'What is the evidence for His existence?' and you say 'this world' and you know the aspect of indication, which is origination or contingency or both, and the second is a part or a condition, and you are able to resolve the doubts, it is detailed evidence.

Commentary
This passage defines the obligation of learning creedal matters and distinguishes between two types of evidence. The commentator explains that knowing every creed with at least summary evidence is fard 'ayn (personal obligation) based on the verse 'Know that there is no god but Allah'. Detailed evidence is fard kifaya (communal obligation) – the community must have some people who can articulate it to repel doubts. Then he rejects the view of those who made detailed evidence fard 'ayn, arguing they narrow God's mercy. The true ruling is that summary evidence is fard 'ayn, detailed is fard kifaya. He then defines both: summary evidence is acknowledging the world as proof without knowing the logical connection (jiha al-dalalah) or being unable to answer doubts; detailed evidence includes knowing the connection and being able to resolve doubts. He gives the example of attributing origination or contingency to the world, with some nuance about which condition is required.

?

?

?

General vs. Detailed Evidence in Kalam
Defines daleel ijmaali and tafseeli and gives examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between general (ijmaali) and detailed (tafseeli) evidence in Islamic theology, and why only general evidence is personally obligatory for every believer.

Core Idea
Knowing the evidence matters less than being able to defend it.

How do you prove God's existence? Is it enough to say 'the world' or must you elaborate? Theologians distinguish between two levels of evidence: general and detailed. In this passage, the author clarifies which one is actually obligatory and how to identify each.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Focus on the definitions and examples.
2
Identify the two types of evidence
Find where the author defines general evidence (daleel ijmaali) and detailed evidence (daleel tafseeli). Notice the key difference: ability to resolve doubts.
3
Study the example of proving God's existence
The author gives a concrete example: responding to 'what is the evidence?' with 'the world'. Trace how the answer shifts from general to detailed based on knowledge and ability.
4
Reflect on the misconception
The passage begins by refuting the claim that detailed evidence is obligatory for everyone. Consider why only general evidence is required for all.
This corrects a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل التفصيلي وجوباً عينيا، وردُّوه بأنهم ضيقوا رحمة الله الواسعة، وجعلوا الجنة مختصة بطائفة يسيرة. فالحق أن الواجب وجوباً عينيا إنما هو الدليل الإجمالي، وهو المعجوز عن تقريره وحل شبهه، وأما الدليل التفصيلي فهو المقدور على تقريره وحل شبهه؛ فإذا قيل لك: ما الدليل على وجوده تعالى؟ فقلت العالم، ولم تعرف جهة الدلالة، فهو دليل جملي ويقال له: دليل إجمالي، وكذلك إذا عرفت جهة الدلالة ولم تقدر على حل الشبه الواردة عليه، وأما إذا عرفت جهة الدلالة وقدرت على حل الشبه، فهو دليل تفصيلي. فإذا قيل لك: ما الدليل على وجوده تعالى؟ فقلت هذا العالم، وعرفت جهة الدلالة؛ وهي الحدوث أو الإمكان أو هما، والثاني شطر أو شرط، وقدرت على حل الشبه، فهو دليل تفصيلي؛ فتقول في تقريره على الأول: العالم حادث، وكل حادث لا بد له من محدث، وعلى الثاني: العالم ممكن، وكل ممكن لا بد له من صانع، وعلى الثالث والرابع: العالم حادث ممكن، وكل حادث ممكن لا بد له من محدث. ويقوم مقام ذلك ما لو عرف العقائد بالكشف.
The detailed evidence (ad-daleel at-tafseeli) is personally obligatory, and they refuted it by saying that they narrowed the vast mercy of God, and made paradise exclusive to a small group. The truth is that what is obligatory as a personal duty is only the general evidence (ad-daleel al-ijmaali), which is the one incapable of presenting it and resolving its doubts. As for the detailed evidence, it is the one capable of presenting it and resolving its doubts. So if it is said to you: 'What is the evidence for His existence, exalted be He?' and you say 'the world', and you do not know the direction of indication, then it is general evidence (daleel jumli) and it is called general evidence (daleel ijmaali). Similarly, if you know the direction of indication but are not able to resolve the doubts that arise upon it. And as for if you know the direction of indication and are able to resolve the doubts, then it is detailed evidence. So if it is said to you: 'What is the evidence for His existence?' and you say 'this world', and you know the direction of indication – which is temporal origination (huduth) or contingency (imkan) or both, the second being a part or condition – and you are able to resolve the doubts, then it is detailed evidence. So you say in its exposition according to the first: The world is temporally originated, and every temporally originated thing necessarily has an originator. According to the second: The world is contingent, and every contingent thing necessarily has a maker. According to the third and fourth: The world is temporally originated and contingent, and every temporally originated contingent thing necessarily has an originator. And that is stood in place of if one knows the beliefs by unveiling (kashf).
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between two types of evidence in kalam: general (ijmaali) and detailed (tafseeli). It begins by noting a claim that detailed evidence is personally obligatory, but then refutes it, stating that the truly obligatory personal duty is to have the general evidence. The general evidence is what one cannot elaborate or defend, while the detailed evidence is what one can elaborate and defend. The author then gives examples: if asked for evidence of God's existence, simply saying 'the world' without knowing how it indicates God is general evidence; if one knows the indication but cannot resolve doubts, it is still general; but if one knows the indication and can resolve doubts, it is detailed evidence. He further clarifies the detailed evidence by showing the logic: the world is either temporally originated or contingent, and each requires a Creator. He also mentions that knowing beliefs by unveiling (kashf) stands in place of this detailed evidence.

Key Terms
دليل إجمالي
دليل تفصيلي
وجوب عيني
حدوث
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Detailed evidence is personally obligatory for all Muslims.
Correction: Only general evidence is obligatory for everyone; detailed evidence is only required for those capable of presenting and defending it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes detailed evidence (daleel tafseeli) from general evidence (daleel ijmaali)?
A) Detailed evidence can be presented and defended against doubts.
B) Detailed evidence is known by unveiling.
C) Detailed evidence does not require knowing the direction of indication.
D) Detailed evidence is only for scholars.

Key Takeaway
General evidence is what every Muslim must have, while detailed evidence involves the ability to present and defend the proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have only general evidence for your faith, or have you taken the time to learn the detailed arguments and prepare to face doubts? What is stopping you from moving from general to detailed evidence?
?
When someone challenges your belief in God's existence, can you articulate the proof from the world's temporal origination and resolve their objections? If not, what specific doubt will you work on resolving this week?
?
The passage warns against limiting God's mercy by imposing detailed evidence as obligatory on all. Are you guilty of requiring others to meet a standard you yourself have not achieved? Or are you complacent with general evidence when you could grow in knowledge?

The Obligation to Know the Three Categories and the Meaning of Taklif
The author explains the obligation to know the three categories, defines who is obligated, and defines taklif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand who is obligated to know the three rational judgments regarding God and His messengers, and the precise meaning of taklif (obligation) in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Knowing the necessary, possible, and impossible about God and His messengers is a personal obligation for every accountable being.

This foundational passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid sets the stage for the entire study of Islamic theology. It establishes that knowledge of the three rational categories is obligatory for every person subject to divine law, and then defines what taklif (obligation) itself means. Understanding this passage is crucial for grasping the scope and nature of theological responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the grammar note about the 'fa' fasiha'.
2
Identify the obligation and its scope
Find the statement that declares the obligation and note who is included and excluded. Notice the evidence from the Quran for excluding angels.
The passage specifies that angels are not obligated because their knowledge is innate.
3
Analyze the two definitions of taklif
Read the definitions of taklif: one as 'imposing hardship' and the other as 'requesting hardship'. Understand which categories of legal rulings each covers.
The preponderant definition restricts taklif to obligatory and prohibited actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإلهيات
۹۱
كلف
[ القسم الأول: الإلهيات]
[ معرفةُ الواجب والجائز والمستحيل في حق الله ورسله واجبةٌ]
- فَكُلُّ مَنْ كُلِّفَ شَرْعاً وَجَبَا عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يَعْرِفَ : مَا قَدْ وَجَبَا قوله : (فَكُلُّ مَنْ كُلّف ... إلى آخره : الفاء فاء الفصيحة؛ لأنها أفصحت عن شرط مقدر (۱) ، والتقدير : إذا أردت بيان علم أصول الدين، فأقول لك: كلُّ من ... إلى آخره؛ أي: كلُّ فردٍ من المكلَّفين من الإنس والجن، ذكراً كان أو أنثى، ولو من العوام والعبيد والنساء والخدم، حتى يأجوج ومأجوج، دون الملائكة ولو قلنا بأنهم مكلفون؛ لأن الخلاف في تكليفهم إنما هو بالنسبة لغير معرفة الله تعالى؛ فإنها جبلية لهم، فليس فيهم من يجهل صفاتِهِ تعالى كما في الإنس والجن ؛ ولذلك قال الله تعالى : وشَهِدَ اللهُ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ وَالْمَلَكَةُ ﴾ [آل عمران : ۱۸] ثم قال: ﴿وَأُولُوا الْعِلْمِ [آل عمران: ۱۸] ، فلم يطلق الأمر كما أطلقه في الملائكة.
ثم إن التكليف إلزام ما فيه كُلفة ؛ وقيل : طلب ما فيه كلفة؛ فعلى الأول - وهو الراجح - يكون قاصراً على الوجوب والحرمة دون الندب والكراهة والإباحة؛ إذ لا إلزام فيها، وعلى الثاني يشمل ما عدا الإباحة؛ إذ لا طلب فيها، فالإباحة ليست تكليفاً عليهما .
(1) وهي أيضاً : الفاء التي يحذف فيها المعطوف عليه مع كونه سيباً للمعطوف من غير تقدير حرف الشرط ، وقيل : سُمِّيت فصيحة لأنها تفصح عن المحذوف، وتفيد بيان سببيته، كما أفاده العلامة الدقر في معجم القواعد العربية».
Divine Matters 91 [Section One: Divine Matters] [Knowledge of the necessary, possible, and impossible with regard to God and His messengers is obligatory] - So every person who is obligated by law, it is necessary upon him to know that which has been made obligatory. His saying: (فَكُلُّ مَنْ كُلّف ... etc.): The fa' is the fa' of clarification because it clarifies an implied condition. And the estimation is: If you want to explain the science of the foundations of religion, then I say to you: Everyone who... etc.; meaning: every individual among the obligated from humans and jinn, male or female, even from the common people, slaves, women, servants, even Gog and Magog, excluding the angels even if we say they are obligated; because the disagreement regarding their obligation is only with respect to other than knowledge of God Almighty; for it is innate to them, so there is none among them who is ignorant of His attributes as is the case with humans and jinn. Therefore God Almighty said: 'And God bears witness that there is no god but Him, and the angels' [Al-Imran:18], then He said: 'and those of knowledge' [Al-Imran:18], so He did not release the command as He released it regarding the angels. Then indeed taklif (obligation) is imposing something that has hardship; and it is said: requesting something that has hardship. According to the first - which is the preponderant - it is confined to obligation and prohibition, excluding recommendation and dislike and permission, because there is no imposition in them. According to the second, it includes everything except permission, because there is no request in it. So permission is not a taklif according to both. (1) And it is also the fa' by which the conjoined item is omitted while being the cause for the conjoined item without estimating a conditional particle, and it is said it is called the clarifying fa' because it clarifies the omitted part and indicates the explanation of its causality, as expressed by al-Allamah al-Duqar in Mu'jam al-Qawa'id al-Arabiyyah.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage begins with a heading establishing the first section on Divine Matters. It then states that knowledge of what is wajib (necessary), ja'iz (possible), and mustahil (impossible) regarding God and His messengers is obligatory. The author then explains the phrase "فكل من كلف شرعا وجب عليه أن يعرف ما قد وجبا" (So every person who is obligated by law, it is necessary upon him to know that which has been made obligatory). He analyzes the grammar of the sentence, noting that the 'fa'' is the 'fa' fasiha' (clarifying fa') which reveals an implied condition: 'If you want to explain the science of the foundations of religion, then I say to you...' Then he specifies who is included in 'كل من كلف': every individual from humans and jinn, male or female, even common people, slaves, women, servants, and even Gog and Magog. Angels are excluded because their knowledge of God is innate; they do not need to learn it. He cites Quran 3:18 to support this distinction. Then the author defines taklif (obligation) itself: it is either 'إلزام ما فيه كلفة' (imposing that which has hardship) or 'طلب ما فيه كلفة' (requesting that which has hardship). The first definition, which is preponderant, restricts taklif to wajib (obligatory) and haram (prohibited) since they involve imposition, excluding mandub (recommended), makruh (disliked), and mubah (permissible). The second definition expands taklif to include everything except mubah, since mubah involves no request. Therefore, mubah is not considered taklif by either definition. A footnote further explains the grammatical term 'fa' fasiha' from al-Duqar's dictionary.

Key Terms
التكليف (taklif: obligation/imposition)
الواجب (wajib: necessary/obligatory)
الجائز (ja'iz: possible/permissible)
المستحيل (mustahil: impossible)
الفاء الفصيحة (fa' fasiha: clarifying fa')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that taklif only applies to adult humans.
Correction: The passage clarifies that taklif applies to all humans and jinn, male and female, including common people, slaves, women, servants, and even Gog and Magog, but excludes angels.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who is excluded from the obligation to know the three categories regarding God and His messengers?
A) Humans and jinn
B) Angels
C) Gog and Magog
D) Common people

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of the three rational judgments is obligatory for every accountable being, and taklif is defined as imposing a legal command with hardship.

?
The angels have innate knowledge of Allah, but you must acquire it. Are you actively seeking that knowledge, or are you content with ignorance? What will you change starting now?
?

The Condition of Sound Senses for Moral Responsibility
Excludes those lacking sound senses from obligation of reflection and taklif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sound senses are a necessary condition for the obligation of reflection and moral responsibility in kalam.

Core Idea
Sound senses are prerequisites for the duty of knowing Allah.

In this brief but important passage, Sanusi clarifies a boundary of moral obligation. Not everyone is required to engage in theological reflection; those deprived of sound senses are exempt. This principle reflects the Islamic legal maxim that obligation is contingent upon capability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is concise yet packs a significant ruling.
2
Identify the condition and its exception
The passage begins by stating that 'by sound senses' others are excluded. Understand that the obligation of nazar and taklif is contingent upon possessing sound senses. The exception is explicit: a blind and deaf person is excused.
3
Grasp the reasoning behind the exemption
Ask why such a person is exempt. The commentary explains that reflection requires the use of senses. Without them, the person cannot fulfil the obligation, so it is lifted.
This is based on the principle that obligation requires ability.
4
Appreciate the scholarly source
Sanusi attributes this ruling to some Shafi'i imams and affirms it as correct. Recognize that this shows how theological rulings are derived from legal principles.
The author's own commentary confirms this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخرج بسليم الحواس: غيرُه، ولهذا قال بعضُ أئمة الشافعية: لو خلق الله إنساناً أعمى أصم، سقط عنه وجوب النظر والتكليف، وهو صحيح كما في شرح المصنف.
And by 'possessor of sound senses' is excluded others. Hence, some Shafi‘i imams said: If God created a blind and deaf person, the obligation of reflection (nazar) and moral responsibility (taklif) falls from him, and this is correct, as stated in the commentary of the author.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage clarifies the condition of sound senses (salim al-hawas) for moral responsibility. The author notes that those lacking sound senses are excluded from the obligation. As evidence, he cites some Shafi‘i imams: if a person is created blind and deaf, the obligation of reflection (nazar) and taklif (moral burden) falls from him. He affirms this ruling as correct, referencing the author's own commentary. This shows that physical and mental capability are necessary for the duty of knowing God through reflection.

Sound senses are a necessary condition for the obligation of reflection and moral responsibility; those without them are excused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Ash'ari Position: Obligation of Knowledge by Revelation
Commentary explains the word 'shar'an' and states Ash'ari doctrine on obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that knowledge of God is obligatory by revelation, and the grammatical analysis of the word 'shar'an' in the poem.

Core Idea
Revealed law, not human reason, is the source of religious obligation in Ash'ari theology.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawḥīd examines the word 'shar'an' and clarifies a fundamental Ash'ari principle: knowledge of God is made obligatory by revelation, not by reason alone. The commentator first resolves a grammatical question about the word's case, then states the doctrinal conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the grammatical discussion at the beginning and the doctrinal statement at the end.
2
Identify the two grammatical analyses
Look for the two opinions on the case of 'shar'an': one as a specification (tamyīz) and the other as removal of preposition (naz' al-khāfiḍ). Understand why the latter is considered samā'ī and the answer given.
The preferred view is the first, due to frequency in authors' speech.
3
Determine the attachment of 'shar'an'
See that 'shar'an' is attached to 'wajaba' (it is obligatory) not to 'kullifa' (is obliged). The reason is to emphasize that the obligation itself comes from revelation.
This distinction is key to the Ash'ari position.
4
Grasp the Ash'ari doctrine
The passage concludes that knowledge of God and all rulings are obligated by revelation, with no binding rule before revelation. This contrasts with rationalist views.
This is the core takeaway of the lesson.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this position affects the understanding of moral and religious duties prior to receiving revelation.
Think about the difference from the Mu'tazili view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (شَرْعاً) الأولى أنه منصوب على التمييز وإن ذكر الشيخ عبد السلام أنه منصوب على نزع الخافض؛ لأنَّه سماعي؛ لكن أجيب عنه بأنه كثر في كلام المؤلفين حتى صار كالقياسي، وعلى كل فهو متعلق بقوله : (وجبا)، وقيل: متعلق بـ ( كُلّف) لكن الأظهر الأول؛ لأن المقصود أن المعرفة وجبت بالشرع لا بالعقل وليس المقصود تقييد التكليف بالشرع، وهذا مذهب الأشاعرة وجمع من غيرهم، فمعرفة الله وجبت عندهم بالشرع (١) ، وكذلك سائر الأحكام؛ إذ لا حكم قبل الشرع لا أصلياً ولا فرعياً.
His saying: 'shar'an' – the preferred view is that it is accusative as a specification (tamyīz), although Shaykh 'Abd al-Salām mentioned that it is accusative by removal of the preposition (naz' al-khāfiḍ) because it is samā'ī (based on transmitted usage); but this was answered by saying that it has become frequent in the speech of authors until it became like qiyāsī (analogical). In any case, it is related to his saying 'wajabā' (it is obligatory). And it is said: it is related to 'kullifa' (is obliged). But the first is more apparent because the intent is that knowledge is obligatory by revelation (shar'), not by reason ('aql), and the intent is not to restrict obligation to revelation. This is the madhhab of the Ash'aris and a group of others: knowledge of God is obligatory by revelation for them, and likewise all rulings (aḥkām); for there is no ruling before revelation, neither primary nor secondary.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and doctrinal explanation of the word 'shar'an' (by revelation/Sharī'a) in the poem Jawharat al-Tawḥīd. The commentator first discusses the grammatical analysis: the preferred view is that 'shar'an' is a specification (tamyīz), though Shaykh 'Abd al-Salām considered it accusative by removal of a preposition. The objection that this is samā'ī (based on transmitted usage) is answered by saying that it has become so frequent in authors' speech that it is treated like a rule (qiyāsī). The commentator then clarifies that the word 'shar'an' is attached to 'wajabā' (it is obligatory), not to 'kullifa' (is obliged), because the intended meaning is that knowledge (ma'rifa) of God is made obligatory by revelation, not by unaided reason. This is the Ash'arī position: all obligations, including knowledge of God, depend on revelation, and there is no binding rule (ḥukm) before revelation arrives, neither in original matters (uṣūl) nor in derived branches (furū').

Key Terms
شَرْعاً
التمييز
نزع الخافض
سماعي
قياسي
وجوب المعرفة
الأشاعرة
حكم قبل الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'shar'an' here means a particular legal ruling rather than the source of obligation.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'shar'an' refers to the basis of obligation itself—revelation—not a specific ruling.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position explained in this passage, how is knowledge of God obligated?
A) By reason alone
B) By revelation only
C) By both reason and revelation equally
D) By community consensus

Key Takeaway
In Ash'ari theology, no religious obligation exists prior to revelation; knowledge of God itself becomes obligatory only through the revealed law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all obligations come only through revelation, do you truly rely on the Qur'an and Sunnah to determine your duties, or do you sometimes follow your own opinions and desires?
?
Before you knew the revealed proofs, did you feel no obligation to know God? Now that revelation has reached you, has it stirred your heart to seek Him?
?
When you face a moral choice, do you pause to ask 'What has Allah revealed about this?' or do you default to your own reasoning? How can you train yourself to submit more fully?

Mu'tazili View on Rational Good and Evil
Mu'tazili position that all rulings are known by reason, and their definition of husn and qubh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilī position that reason alone can determine moral rulings, and how they define good and evil.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila make reason the ultimate moral judge; revelation only confirms it.

This passage outlines the core Mu'tazilī thesis on moral epistemology. Before we examine the Sunni response, we need to see how the Mu'tazila ground all moral obligations in reason alone, defining good and evil as what reason deems so. They do not reject revelation but place it as a secondary support.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the citation from Jam' al-Jawāmi'.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage begins with the Mu'tazila's core thesis: 'all rulings are established by reason'. Understand what 'rulings' means here (moral obligations, prohibitions, etc.).
3
Understand the role of revelation
They do not reject revelation; they see it as a strengthener and confirmer for reason. This clarifies a common misconception.
4
Note the definitions of good and evil
Good is what reason deems good; evil is what reason deems evil. This is the basis of rational good and evil.
5
See how obligation follows
When reason apprehends an act as good in a specific sense (blame for leaving, praise for doing), it rules it obligatory. The phrase 'and so on' implies other categories follow similarly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن الأحكام كلها ثبتت بالعقل، ولذلك قال في جمع الجوامع : وحكمت المعتزلة العقل أي : جعلته حاكماً؛ أي: مدركاً للأحكام وإن لم يرد الشرع، ويقولون : إن الشرع جاة مقوّياً ومؤكّداً للعقل، فلا ينفون الشرع أصلاً، وإلا لكفروا قطعاً، ويبنون كلامَهُمْ على التحسين والتقبيح العقليين؛ فالحسن عندهم : ما حسنه العقل، والقبيحُ : ما قبحه العقل؛ فإذا أدرك أن هذا الفعل حسن؛ بحيث يُدم على تركه ويُمدحُ على فعله حكم بوجوبه، وهكذا
And the Mu'tazila went to the view that all rulings are established by reason ('aql). Therefore it was said in Jam' al-Jawāmi': 'And the Mu'tazila made reason a judge (ḥākiman)', i.e. they made it a judge (ḥākiman), i.e. an apprehender (mudrikan) of rulings even if revelation (shar') has not come. And they say that revelation comes as a strengthener and confirmer for reason, so they do not deny revelation entirely, otherwise they would undoubtedly disbelieve. And they base their discourse on rational good and evil (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ al-'aqliyyayn); so 'good' (al-ḥasan) for them is what reason deems good, and 'evil' (al-qabīḥ) is what reason deems evil. So when it (reason) apprehends that this act is good – such that one is deserving of blame for abandoning it and praise for performing it – it rules it obligatory, and so on.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilī position on moral epistemology. It begins with their core thesis: all rulings (aḥkām) – including obligation, prohibition, etc. – are established by reason, not by revelation. The commentator cites the work Jam' al-Jawāmi' which states that the Mu'tazila made reason a 'judge' (ḥākim), meaning it is an 'apprehender' (mudrik) of rulings independently of revelation. However, they do not reject revelation entirely; they see it as a support (muqawwī) and confirmer (mu'akkid) for reason. Denying revelation would be kufr. The Mu'tazilī system is built upon the doctrine of rational good and evil (taḥsīn and taqbīḥ 'aqliyyān). They define 'good' (ḥasan) as that which reason deems good, and 'evil' (qabīḥ) as that which reason deems evil. When reason apprehends that an act is good in the sense that one would be blameworthy for omitting it and praiseworthy for performing it, reason then rules that act as obligatory (wājib). The phrase 'and so on' indicates that other moral categories (recommended, prohibited, etc.) follow the same principle.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الأحكام
حكمت العقل
مدرك
التحسين والتقبيح العقليين
الحسن
القبيح
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila reject revelation entirely.
Correction: This passage clarifies they see it as a confirmer of reason, not a replacement.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila as described in this passage, how does reason determine that an act is obligatory?
A) By seeing that the act is beneficial
B) By apprehending that the act is good such that one deserves praise for doing it and blame for leaving it
C) By receiving a direct command from God
D) By consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila hold that reason independently discerns good and evil, and that revelation serves only to strengthen reason's verdict.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever find yourself relying on your own reason to decide what is right or wrong, even when it contradicts what you know from revelation? How would the Mu'tazila's position challenge or affirm your approach?

?
The Mu'tazila claim that revelation only confirms reason. Do you truly believe that your reason is sufficient to guide you morally without divine guidance? If not, how does your dependence on revelation shape your humility before Allah?

Good, Evil, and Obligation: Sunni vs Maturidi
Contrasts Ash'ari and Maturidi definitions of good/evil and obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the Ash'ari and Maturidi positions on moral definitions and the role of reason in the obligation to know God.

Core Idea
For Ash'aris, good and evil depend on revelation; for Maturidis, reason independently knows the obligation.

In this passage, the author presents two key theological positions within Sunni Islam: the Ash'ari (Ahl al-Sunna) view that good and evil are defined solely by revelation, and the Maturidi view that reason independently recognizes the obligation to know God. Understanding this contrast is essential for grasping how different schools approach moral epistemology and the role of human intellect in faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the two distinct positions presented.
The passage uses 'And as for' to shift between views.
2
Understand the Ash'ari definition
Focus on the first sentence: 'good is what revelation deems good, evil is what revelation deems evil.' This is the Ash'ari moral theology.
This means actions have no intrinsic moral value; value comes from divine command.
3
Analyze the Maturidi position
The second part states that reason independently grasps the obligation to know God, even without revelation. But note the caveat 'not based on' — reason discovers, not creates, the obligation.
This distinguishes Maturidis from Mu'tazilis who hold reason creates obligation.
4
Contrast the two views
Compare how each school defines moral categories. For Ash'aris, revelation is the source. For Maturidis, reason plays an independent role for a specific obligation.
Remember that both agree the obligation ultimately comes from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عند أهل السنة، فالحسنُ : ما حسَّنَهُ الشرع، والقبيح : ما قبحه الشرع. ومذهب الماتريدية كما نقله المصنف في شرحه عنهم : أن وجوب المعرفة بالعقل (٤)؛ بمعنى : أنه لو لم يرد به الشرع، لأدركه العقل استقلالاً لوضوحه، لا بناءً
And as for the Ahl al-Sunna, 'good' (al-ḥasan) is what revelation (shar') deems good, and 'evil' (al-qabīḥ) is what revelation deems evil. And the madhhab of the Māturīdiyya, as the author narrated in his commentary from them, is that the obligation of knowledge (wujūb al-ma'rifa) is by reason ('aql); meaning: that if revelation had not come concerning it, reason would have apprehended it independently due to its clarity, not based on [something else].
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the contrasting positions of the Ahl al-Sunna (Ash'aris) and the Māturīdiyya regarding moral epistemology and the obligation to know God. First, the Sunni view: good (ḥasan) and evil (qabīḥ) are defined not by reason but by revelation. What God's law declares good is good, and what it declares evil is evil. This is the Ash'arī position mentioned earlier that rulings depend on revelation. Then the Māturīdī view is given: they hold that the obligation to know God (wujūb al-ma'rifa) is known by reason ('aql). The commentator explains this means that even if revelation had not come about this obligation, reason would have known it independently because its truth is so clear. However, this does not mean reason creates obligation; rather, reason discovers an obligation that God has placed. The phrase 'not based on' (lā binā'an) likely contrasts with the Mu'tazilī view that reason itself imposes obligation. The Māturīdī position is thus a middle ground: reason can know the obligation, but the obligation itself comes from God.

Key Terms
أهل السنة
الحسن
القبيح
الشرع
الماتريدية
وجوب المعرفة
العقل استقلالاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Maturidi view is identical to the Mu'tazili view that reason creates moral obligation.
Correction: The Maturidi position as described here states that reason apprehends the obligation independently, not that it creates it. The obligation still originates from God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Ash'ari (Ahl al-Sunna) definition of 'good' (al-ḥasan)?
A) What reason deems good
B) What revelation deems good
C) What is intrinsically beneficial
D) What the community agrees upon

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school defines good and evil by revelation, while the Maturidi school holds that reason independently knows the obligation to know God, though the obligation itself comes from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you decide what is good or evil, do you instinctively rely more on your own reasoning or on what God has revealed? This lesson shows two Orthodox views—which one challenges your automatic tendency more? Will you commit today to measuring your moral judgments against the Quran and Sunnah first?
?
If, as the Maturidi school says, reason can independently know the obligation to know God, have you truly used your reason to seek that knowledge? What concrete step will you take this week to increase your ma'rifa of Allah—reading, reflection, or asking a scholar?
?
The Ash'ari position makes good and evil entirely dependent on revelation. Is there an area of your life where you secretly believe your own sense of 'good' is better than what Allah has commanded? What would it cost you to submit fully to His definition in that area?

The Mu'tazila View: Reason as the Judge of Good and Evil
The Mu'tazila position on reason as the source of moral knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila doctrine of rational approbation and disapprobation, and why they consider reason an independent judge of moral and legal rulings.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, reason alone determines good and evil; revelation merely confirms it.

The Mu'tazila school placed extraordinary confidence in human reason, arguing that moral values are objectively known by the intellect without needing revelation. This lesson explores their position as presented in the source, focusing on how they define good (ḥasan) and evil (qabīḥ).

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila passage
Read the Arabic passage about the Mu'tazila carefully, then its translation. Notice that they do not reject revelation but treat it as a strengthener of reason.
Pay attention to the analogy of reason as a 'judge'.
2
Identify the key definitions
Find how the Mu'tazila define 'good' (ḥasan) and 'evil' (qabīḥ) based on reason's judgment.
The definition includes both praise/blame and obligation.
3
Understand the logical consequence
The Mu'tazila argue that if reason deems an act good, it becomes obligatory. Reflect on how this leads to independent moral knowledge without revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[الحسنُ والقبيحُ عند المعتزلة وأهل السنة]
وذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن الأحكام كلها ثبتت بالعقل، ولذلك قال في جمع الجوامع : وحكمت المعتزلة العقل) أي : جعلته حاكماً؛ أي: مدركاً للأحكام وإن لم يرد الشرع، ويقولون : إن الشرع جاء مقوّياً ومؤكّداً للعقل، فلا ينفون الشرع أصلاً، وإلا لكفروا قطعاً، ويبنون كلامَهُمْ على التحسين والتقبيح العقليين؛ فالحسن عندهم : ما حسنه العقل، والقبيحُ : ما قبحه العقل؛ فإذا أدرك أن هذا الفعل حسن؛ بحيث يُذَم على تركه ويُمدحُ على فعله حكم بوجوبه، وهكذا (3)
وأما عند أهل السنة، فالحسنُ : ما حسَّنَهُ الشرع، والقبيح : ما قبحه الشرع. ومذهب الماتريدية كما نقله المصنف في شرحه عنهم : أن وجوب المعرفة بالعقل (4)؛ بمعنى : أنه لو لم يرد به الشرع، لأدركه العقل استقلالاً لوضوحه، لا بناءً على التحسين والتقبيح العقليين كما قالت المعتزلة
[Good and evil according to the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna] The Mu'tazila held that all rulings are established by reason, and therefore it is said in Jam' al-Jawami': 'The Mu'tazila made reason a judge' – that is, they made it a ruler, i.e., an apperhender of rulings even if revelation does not come. They say that revelation came as a strengthener and confirmer for reason, so they do not negate revelation entirely, otherwise they would surely be disbelievers. They base their discourse on rational approbation and disapprobation: good in their view is what reason deems good, and evil is what reason deems evil. So if it perceives that an act is good, such that one is blamed for leaving it and praised for doing it, it rules its obligation, and so on. As for Ahl al-Sunna, good is what the Law deems good, and evil is what the Law deems evil. And the Maturidi school, as the author narrated in his commentary from them, is that the obligation of knowledge is by reason – meaning that if revelation had not come concerning it, reason would independently perceive it due to its clarity, not on the basis of rational approbation and disapprobation as the Mu'tazila said.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage defines the concepts of good (ḥasan) and evil (qabīḥ) according to two or three major theological schools. It begins with the Mu'tazila position: they hold that all moral and legal rulings are known by reason independently of revelation. They argue that reason is like a judge (ḥākim) that discerns good and evil even without revelation, though revelation reinforces reason. Their key principle is rational approbation and disapprobation (taḥsīn wa taqbīḥ ʿaqliyyān): good is what reason perceives as blameworthy to omit or praiseworthy to perform, and reason then deems such acts obligatory. The text then presents the Ahl al-Sunna (Ashʿarī) view: good and evil are defined by what the Law (Sharʿ) declares good or evil – reason alone cannot make such judgments. Finally, a third position from the Māturīdī school is noted: they say that the obligation to know God is known by reason, meaning that if no revelation existed, reason could independently deduce it because the evidence for God's existence is clear. However, they do not base this on rational approbation/disapprobation like the Mu'tazila; rather, it is a special case of logical clarity. Thus the passage distinguishes three approaches: Mu'tazila give reason full authority, Ahl al-Sunna give revelation full authority, and Māturīdīs give reason authority only in the specific matter of knowing God (not in all moral judgments).

Key Terms

Correction: The passage clearly states they do not negate revelation; it acts as a strengthener and confirmer of reason.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what defines the goodness (ḥusn) of an act?
A) The act being praised in revelation
B) Reason deeming it good and that one is praised for doing it
C) The act being beneficial
D) The act being customary

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila believe reason independently identifies good and evil, and revelation only confirms it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever trusted your own reasoning about a moral issue over the clear text of revelation? What was the result, and how did it affect your relationship with Allah's guidance?
?
The Mu'tazila say reason alone can obligate you to act. Do you find yourself making moral judgments based solely on your intellect without consulting the Qur'an and Sunnah? What does that reveal about the place of revelation in your heart?
?
If reason and revelation ever seemed to conflict in your mind, would you follow your reason or the revealed text? What does that choice say about your ultimate allegiance and trust in Allah's wisdom?

The Ahl al-Sunna and Maturidi Positions: Revelation and Reason
The Ash'ari and Maturidi positions on the source of moral knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that good and evil are defined by revelation, and the Maturidi exception regarding the obligation to know God.

Core Idea
For Ash'aris, only revelation defines good and evil; Maturidis make a limited exception for knowledge of God.

While the Mu'tazila elevate reason, Ahl al-Sunna (Ash'aris) anchor moral values in divine command. However, the Maturidi school offers a nuanced middle position, allowing reason to independently know the obligation to know God. This lesson unpacks these two positions as presented in the source.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Ash'ari view
Read the part about Ahl al-Sunna. Note that good and evil are defined solely by revelation (Sharʿ).
This is a direct contrast to the Mu'tazila.
2
Examine the Maturidi exception
Read about the Maturidi position: they say the obligation to know God is known by reason independently, due to clarity of evidence.
They do NOT base this on rational approbation/disapprobation.
3
Compare the three views
Contrast the Mu'tazila, Ash'ari, and Maturidi positions. Use the guided breakdown to see how each school treats reason and revelation.

Three Schools on Obligation: Reason vs. Revelation
Summarizes three schools on obligation: Ash'ari, Maturidi, Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three major theological positions on the role of reason and revelation in establishing obligations.

Core Idea
Reason's role in obligation is disputed: all, some, or none.

This passage presents the author's summary of three schools: Ash'ari, Maturidi, and Mu'tazila. The author begins by affirming that reason does not stand alone. Then he outlines each school's stance on whether rulings are established by reason or revelation. Pay close attention to the subtle distinction between Maturidi and Mu'tazila positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the source Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
2
Identify the three schools and their positions
Locate each school mentioned and note what they say about the source of rulings (sharʿ or ʿaql) and any conditions.
3
Compare the Maturidi and Mu'tazila positions
The author highlights a crucial difference. Understand that Maturidis only say knowledge of God is obligatory by reason, while Mu'tazila say all rulings come from reason.
4
Reflect on the Ash'ari condition of reason
Ash'aris say rulings are by revelation but with the condition of reason. Consider what this condition implies about the role of reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحق أن العقل لا يستقل بشيء أصلاً، فتلخص أن المذاهب ثلاثة : - مذهب الأشاعرة: وهو أن الأحكام كلّها تثبت بالشرع، لكن بشرط العقل. والثاني مذهب الماتريدية وهو أن وجوب المعرفة ثبت بالعقل دون سائر الأحكام. والثالث مذهب المعتزلة وهو أن الأحكام كلَّها تثبت بالعقل. وقد علمت الفرق بين قول الماتريدية بوجوب المعرفة بالعقل، وقول المعتزلة بثبوت الأحكام بالعقل.
And the truth is that reason does not stand independently regarding anything at all. So it is summarized that the schools are three: the first is the Ash'ari school – that all rulings are established by revelation, but with the condition of reason. The second is the Maturidi school – that the obligation of knowledge is established by reason, apart from other rulings. The third is the Mu'tazila school – that all rulings are established by reason. And you have known the difference between the Maturidi statement that knowledge is obligatory by reason and the Mu'tazila statement that rulings are established by reason.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage provides a concise summary of three key theological positions regarding the source of moral and religious obligations. It begins by stating the author's own view (al-ḥaqq) that reason does not operate independently in any matter – a clear rejection of the Mu'tazila position. Then it enumerates the three schools. First, the Ash'ari school: all rulings (aḥkām) are established by revelation (sharʿ), but reason is a necessary condition (bisharṭ al-ʿaql) – meaning that reason must be present for obligation to be meaningful, but it does not itself determine the ruling. Second, the Maturidi school: only the obligation to know God (wujūb al-maʿrifa) is established by reason, while all other rulings come from revelation; this is a middle position, as Maturidis hold that rational proofs for God are clear enough for reason to know independently. Third, the Mu'tazila school: all rulings, without exception, are established by reason itself. The passage then emphasizes that the reader should note the crucial difference: Maturidis say knowledge is obligatory by reason (meaning reason can infer the obligation from divine command, not that reason itself imposes it), whereas Mu'tazila say all rulings are established by reason (meaning reason is the independent source of obligation). This distinction prevents confusion between Maturidi and Mu'tazila positions, which might appear similar on the surface but are fundamentally different in their underlying principles.

Misconception: One might think that the Maturidi position is identical to the Mu'tazila because both say 'obligation of knowledge by reason'.
Correction: The passage clarifies that for Maturidis it is based on clarity of evidence, not on rational approbation/disapprobation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The three schools differ fundamentally on whether reason can establish obligations independently, with Ash'ari relying on revelation, Mu'tazila on reason, and Maturidi taking a middle position on knowledge of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila claim that all obligations are known by reason. Have you ever acted on what you thought was rationally obvious, only to later discover it contradicted revelation? What would that mean for your confidence in pure reason?
?
If reason cannot independently establish any obligation (as the author asserts), then your understanding of right and wrong must come entirely from revelation. Are you actively seeking that revelation, or are you letting cultural norms and personal desires dictate your morality?

The Three Schools on Reason and Revelation
Al-Bajuri enumerates three schools on rational obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions of the Ash'aris, Maturidis, and Mu'tazila on the role of reason and revelation in establishing obligations, and the key distinction between the Maturidi and Mu'tazila views.

Core Idea
Three schools, one truth: reason is dependent on revelation.

The relationship between reason and revelation has been a central debate in Islamic theology. Al-Bajuri summarizes the three major positions: Ash'ari, Maturidi, and Mu'tazila. This passage lays out their views and clarifies a subtle but crucial difference between the Maturidi and Mu'tazila positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the author's clear enumeration of the three schools.
The passage begins with the Mu'tazila position and then states the truth before listing the schools.
2
Identify the three positions
List the three schools and their respective claim about where obligations come from. Write them down in your own words.
Pay attention to the condition of reason for Ash'aris versus the direct role for Maturidis and Mu'tazila.
3
Understand the key distinction
Focus on the last part where the author distinguishes between Maturidi and Mu'tazila views on the obligation to know God. Note how the Maturidi view avoids rationalism.
The key phrase is 'not based on rational improvement of itself, but it is subordinate to God's obligation.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على التحسين العقليّ كما قالت المعتزلة. والحق أن العقل لا يستقل بشيء أصلاً، فتلخص أن المذاهب ثلاثة: مذهب الأشاعرة: وهو أن الأحكام كلّها تثبت بالشرع، لكن بشرط العقل. والثاني مذهب الماتريدية وهو أن وجوب المعرفة ثبت بالعقل دون سائر الأحكام. والثالث مذهب المعتزلة وهو أن الأحكام كلَّها تثبت بالعقل. وقد علمت الفرق بين قول الماتريدية بوجوب المعرفة بالعقل، وقول المعتزلة بثبوت الأحكام بالعقل. بناء على وجوب الصلاح والأصلح، فبالجملة: يجعلون الشرع تابعاً للعقل، لا أنهم ينفون استفادة هذه الأحكام من الشرع ويضيفونها للعقل، وإلا لكفروا قطعاً، وأما الماتريدي فمعنى ما نقل عنه: أن إيجاب المعرفة بالله تعالى بمحض اختياره، غير أن هذا الحكم لو لم يرد به شرع أمكن العقل أن يفهمه عن الله تعالى لوضوحه لا بناءً على تحسين ذاته، بل هو تابع لإيجاب الله تعالی، عکس ما قالت المعتزلة، وهو كلام بديع.
On rational improvement as the Mu'tazila said. The truth is that reason is not independent of anything at all. So it is summarized that the schools are three: the school of the Ash'aris: that all judgments are established by revelation, but with the condition of reason. The second is the school of the Maturidis: that the obligation of knowledge is established by reason without the rest of the judgments. The third is the school of the Mu'tazila: that all judgments are established by reason. And you have known the difference between the saying of the Maturidis that knowledge is obligatory by reason, and the saying of the Mu'tazila that judgments are established by reason. Based on the obligation of what is right and most advantageous; in short, they make revelation subordinate to reason, not that they deny deriving these judgments from revelation and attribute them to reason; otherwise they would be disbelievers absolutely. As for the Maturidi, the meaning of what is transmitted from him is that making knowledge of God obligatory is by His sheer choice, yet if no revelation came concerning this judgment, reason could understand it from God due to its clarity, not based on rational improvement of itself, but it is subordinate to God's obligation, contrary to what the Mu'tazila said, and it is excellent speech.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage classifies three Islamic theological schools regarding the relationship between reason (al-ʿaql) and revelation (al-sharʿ) in determining obligations (al-aḥkām). The author begins by stating that the Mu'tazila hold that obligations are based on rational improvement (taḥsīn ʿaqlī). He then declares the truth: reason is not independent of anything. This leads to a summary of three positions. First, the Ash'aris: all judgments are established by revelation, but reason is a necessary condition (e.g., to understand the revelation). Second, the Maturidis: only the obligation to know God is established by reason; other obligations require revelation. Third, the Mu'tazila: all judgments are established by reason. The author then clarifies the key difference: the Maturidis make knowledge of God obligatory by reason (but not other judgments), whereas the Mu'tazila make all judgments inherently knowable by reason. The Mu'tazila base this on the principle of divine justice (wujūb al-ṣalāḥ wa-al-aṣlaḥ), making revelation subordinate to reason. However, they do not deny that judgments can be derived from revelation; they just attribute them ultimately to reason. If they denied revelation entirely, they would be disbelievers. For the Maturidis, the obligation to know God arises from God's free will, but reason can understand it even without revelation because it is self-evident—not because reason judges it good inherently, but because God has made it so. This is opposite to the Mu'tazila position.

Key Terms
العقل
الشرع
الأحكام
المعرفة
الأشاعرة
الماتريدية
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Maturidis deny any role for revelation beyond knowledge of God.
Correction: Maturidis hold that only the obligation to know God is knowable by reason; all other obligations require revelation. Reason is not independent but subordinate to God's will.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which school holds that all judgments are established by reason?
A) Ash'aris
B) Maturidis
C) Mu'tazila
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The three schools agree that reason has a role, but they differ on the extent: Ash'aris make reason a condition, Maturidis make it sufficient only for knowledge of God, and Mu'tazila make it sufficient for all judgments.

Refutation of the Mu'tazila Circularity Argument
Al-Bajuri cites and answers the Mu'tazila circularity argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila objection of circularity against the Ash'ari view on revelation and how al-Yāfiʿī refutes it by distinguishing revelation's role in commanding reasoning.

Core Idea
Revelation commands reasoning, not knowledge directly — no circularity.

The Mu'tazila argued that if obligation is by revelation, then to know God through revelation would require prior knowledge of God, leading to circularity. In this passage, al-Bajuri cites al-Yāfiʿī's refutation, which clarifies that revelation commands reasoning, which then leads to knowledge. This distinction breaks the alleged circularity and preserves the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila objection
Read the first line to understand what the Mu'tazila claimed about circularity (al-dawr) if obligation is by revelation. What would the circularity be?
Circularity: to know God via revelation, one needs to know God to authenticate revelation.
2
Understand al-Yāfiʿī's clarification
Al-Yāfiʿī distinguishes between the way to knowledge (al-naẓar) and the source of obligation (al-samʿ). Revelation makes reasoning obligatory, but reasoning itself is what leads to knowledge.
The key: revelation commands naẓar, not direct knowledge.
3
Follow the logical consequence
Notice that if one disobeys the command to reason, knowledge does not follow. Thus, revelation does not necessitate knowledge, breaking the circularity.
4
Conclude the refutation
Both knowledge and reasoning are obligatory by revelation, but they are distinct. No circularity exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد حاجت المعتزلة أهل السنة بلزوم الدور إن كان التكليف شرعيًّا، وقد ردَّ هذا الحافظ اليافعي في كتابه مرهم العلل المعضلة في الرد على أئمة المعتزلة (ص ٤٦) حيث قال: (وأما ما ذكره من أن الطريق إلى معرفة الله تعالى السمع عندنا، فليس بصحيح، بل الطريق إليها عندنا وعندهم النظر، لكن عندنا يجب النظر فيها بالسمع، وعندهم بالعقل، فالسمعُ عندنا طريق إلى معرفة وجوب النظر الموصل إلى المعرفة، لا إلى المعرفة نفسها كما زعم؛ لأن الأمر بها موجب للنظر المعرّف، وقد يخلف النظر بخلف امتثال الأمر، فتخلف المعرفة لخلف المعرف، ولا يلزم وجوده وجودها؛ أعني: لا يلزم من وجود الأمر - الذي هو السمع - وجود المأمور به الذي هو المعرفة). وكما أن المعرفة واجبة بالشرع، فكذلك النظر واجب بالشرع ولا فرق بينهما كما بينه ابن الناظم.
And the Mu'tazila have argued against the Ahl al-Sunna regarding the necessity of circularity (al-dawr) if obligation is by revelation. And al-Hāfiẓ al-Yāfiʿī refuted this in his book Marham al-ʿIlal al-Muʿḍilla fī al-Radd ʿalā Aʾimmat al-Muʿtazila (p. 46) where he said: 'As for what he mentioned that the way to knowledge of God is revelation (al-samʿ) according to us, it is not correct. Rather, the way to it according to us and according to them is reasoning (al-naẓar), but according to us reasoning is obligatory by revelation, and according to them by reason. So revelation according to us is a way to know the obligation of reasoning that leads to knowledge, not to knowledge itself as he claimed; because the command to it necessitates reasoning that makes known, and reasoning may be opposed by opposing the command, so knowledge is opposed by opposing the means of knowledge, and its existence does not necessitate its existence; I mean: the existence of the command—which is revelation—does not necessitate the existence of the commanded thing, which is knowledge.' And just as knowledge is obligatory by revelation, so too reasoning is obligatory by revelation, and there is no difference between them as Ibn al-Nāẓim explained.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari position that obligation (taklīf) is established by revelation. The Mu'tazila argued that this leads to circularity (al-dawr): to know God through revelation, one would already need to know God to verify the revelation. The author then cites al-Yāfiʿī's refutation. Al-Yāfiʿī clarifies that the way to knowledge of God is reasoning (al-naẓar) for both parties. For the Ash'aris, reasoning is obligatory by revelation, i.e., revelation commands one to reason. This command itself is the means to know the obligation to reason, not to know God directly. It is possible to receive the command but fail to reason (due to disobedience), so knowledge of God is not entailed by the mere existence of revelation. Thus, no circularity occurs. The passage concludes that both knowledge (maʿrifa) and reasoning (naẓar) are obligatory by revelation, and there is no essential difference between them.

Key Terms
الدور
التكليف
النظر
السمع
المعرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Revelation directly gives knowledge of God without reasoning.
Correction: Revelation commands reasoning, which then leads to knowledge. Knowledge is not directly entailed by revelation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Yāfiʿī's response, what does revelation command?
A) Knowledge of God directly
B) Reasoning that leads to knowledge
C) Immediate acceptance of dogma
D) Blind faith in tradition

Key Takeaway
Revelation commands reasoning, not direct knowledge; therefore, no circularity exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face uncertainty about your faith, do you actively engage in reasoning (naẓar) as commanded by revelation, or do you passively rely on what you've been told? This lesson says revelation obligates you to reason—are you fulfilling that obligation?
?
The command to know God does not guarantee that you will know Him if you disobey. Have you been careless in using your God-given intellect to seek Him? What concrete step will you take today to begin that reasoning?
?
If you claim to believe in revelation, does your life reflect that you treat it as a command to seek understanding? Or have you settled for blind imitation, ignoring the very reasoning that revelation demands?

Defining the Necessary (al-Wajib) and Its Divisions
The commentator defines al-wajib, refines the definition, and divides it into daruri and nazari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defines al-wajib, refines the definition to avoid reliance on the intellect, and divides it into daruri and nazari.

Core Idea
The Necessary is necessary in itself, regardless of any intellect.

The commentator begins by exploring two definitions of the Necessary, then raises an objection and resolves it by distinguishing between conception and assent. He ultimately prefers a definition that does not tie the Necessary to the existence of reason. He then divides the Necessary into two types: one conditional and one absolute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms: al-wajib, al-daruri, al-nazari.
2
Trace the argument
The passage presents two initial definitions, then an objection and an answer. See how the commentator refines the definition by dissociating it from reason.
The key move is replacing 'al-tasawwur' with 'al-tasdiq' to answer the objection.
3
Understand the two divisions
The Necessary is divided into daruri (conditional, like a body occupying space) and nazari (absolute, like God's attributes). Compare the two examples.
Daruri depends on something else; nazari is unconditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفعل للفاعل؛ أي ما لا يمكن بسبب العقل عدمه، أو للمفعول؛ أي: ما لا تدرك النفس بسبب العقل عدمه، لكن يرد على هذا أن النفس قد تدرك عدم الواجب؛ لأن المحال قد يُتصور؛ أي: يدرك ويجاب بأن المراد بالتصور هنا: التصديق، والمعنى حينئذ ما لا تصدق النفس بسبب العقل بعدمه وعلم من هذا: أن العقل آلة في الإدراك؛ والمدرك إنما هو النفس. والأولى عدم ربط الواجب بالعقل، فيقال: الواجب: هو ما لا يقبل الانتفاء؛ لأن الواجب واجب في نفسه وجد عقل أو لم يوجد. والواجب قسمان: ضروري: كتحيز الجرم؛ أي: أخذه قدراً من الحيز، وهو المكان؛ فإنه ما دام الجرم موجوداً يجب أن يتحيز، فهو واجب مقيد بدوام الجرم. ونظري: كصفات الله تعالى.
The verbal noun is for the agent, i.e., what cannot, by reason, be non-existent, or for the object, i.e., what the soul does not perceive, by reason, to be non-existent. But it is objected to this that the soul may perceive the non-existence of the Necessary, because the impossible may be conceived, i.e., perceived. And it is answered that what is meant by conception here is assent (tasdiq), and the meaning then is: what the soul does not assent, by reason, to its non-existence. And from this it is known that reason is an instrument in perception, and the perceiver is only the soul. And the better view is not to link the Necessary to reason, so it is said: the Necessary is what does not admit of negation, because the Necessary is necessary in itself, whether reason exists or not. And the Necessary is two divisions: necessary-by-necessity (daruri), like the occupying of space by a body, i.e., its taking up a portion of space, which is place; for as long as the body exists, it must occupy space, so it is a necessary conditional upon the continuance of the body. And theoretical (nazari), like the attributes of Allah Most High.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of the necessary (al-wajib) in rational terms, beginning with two alternative definitions: one from the perspective of the agent (al-fi'l lil-fa'il) and one from the perspective of the object (al-fi'l lil-maf'ul). The commentator then raises an objection that the soul can conceive the non-existence of the Necessary, since impossible things can be imagined. The answer clarifies that 'conception' here means 'assent' (tasdiq), not mere imagination, so the definition holds. From this, it is derived that the intellect is merely an instrument; the perceiver is the soul. The commentator then prefers a definition that avoids linking the Necessary to the intellect altogether: the Necessary is that which does not admit of non-existence, being necessary in itself regardless of the existence of any intellect. Finally, the Necessary is divided into two types: the necessary-by-necessity (daruri), which is conditional—like a body's occupation of space as long as it exists—and the theoretical (nazari), which is absolute and unconditional, exemplified by the attributes of Allah.

Key Terms

الضروري
النظري
التصور
التصديق
العقل
النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The necessity of the Necessary depends on the intellect's perception.
Correction: The commentator emphasizes that the Necessary is necessary in itself whether reason exists or not.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the preferred definition of al-wajib (the necessary)?
A) What cannot be perceived as non-existent by the soul through reason.
B) What does not admit of negation, necessary in itself whether reason exists or not.
C) What the intellect cannot conceive as non-existent.
D) Everything that exists is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary is defined as what does not admit of negation, and it is either conditional (daruri) or absolute (nazari).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes treat Allah's existence and attributes as if they were dependent on your intellectual affirmation—like a daruri necessary that depends on your awareness? How would your worship change if you truly internalized that Allah is necessary in Himself whether you believe or not?
?
The commentator says reason is merely an instrument; the soul is the perceiver. Have you made reason the master of your faith, demanding that everything be intellectually proven before you submit? What would it mean to let your soul perceive the Necessary directly, beyond logical argument?
?
If the Necessary does not admit of negation, then your denial or neglect of Him does not diminish His reality—it only harms your soul. Are you living as if your indifference could make Him less necessary? What concrete step will you take today to align your life with the fact that He is al-Wajib, whether you acknowledge it or not?
