The Possible and the Impossible: Rational Categories and Divisions
The commentator defines al-ja'iz and al-mumtani' and divides each into daruri and nazari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of the rationally possible (al-ja'iz) and the rationally impossible (al-mumtani'), and their divisions into necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari).

Core Idea
Rational possibility is not the same as religious permissibility.

In this passage, the commentator builds on the definitions of the necessary (al-wajib) to now define the possible and the impossible. These categories are essential for understanding what can be attributed to Allah and His messengers. The key distinction is between what is rationally conceivable and what is religiously prohibited.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of al-ja'iz
Read the definition of the possible: what can be rationally conceived as existing or not existing.
Notice that this includes examples like motion/rest (necessary) and divine punishment/reward (theoretical).
2
Learn the two divisions of al-ja'iz
The possible divides into daruri (by necessity) and nazari (theoretical). Observe that the theoretical possibility remains possible even if prohibited by revelation.
3
Learn the definition and division of al-mumtani'
The impossible is what cannot be rationally conceived. It also divides into daruri, like a body being both in motion and at rest simultaneously.
This ensures conceptual coherence in theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لِلَّهِ وَالجَائِزَ وَالمُمْتَنِعًا وَمِثْلَ ذَا لِرُسُلِهِ فَاسْتَمِعَا قوله : (وَالجَائز) أي : في حقه سبحانه وتعالى عقلاً، وهو معطوف على قوله : (ما قد وجبا)، وقد عرفوه بأنه : ما يصح في العقل وجوده تارة وعدمه أخرى؛ إما ضرورة؛ كحركة الجرم أو سكونه، أو نظراً؛ كتعذيب المطيع ولو معصوماً، لكن التمشدق في حق الأنبياء، بل بقدر ضرورة التعليم، وإثابة العاصي ولو كافراً؛ لأن الكلام في الإمكان العقلي، فلا ينافي أن ذلك ممتنع شرعاً، وعلم من ذلك أن الجائز قسمان: ضروري ونظري. قوله: (وَالمُمْتَنِعَا) أي: المستحيل في حقه تعالى وعرفوه بأنه: ما لا يتصور في العقل وجوده، ببناء الفعل للفاعل أو للمفعول كما تقدم في تعريف الواجب. وهو قسمان: ضروري: كخلو الجرم عن الحركة والسكون معاً.
To Allah belongs the possible and the impossible, and similarly for His messengers, so listen. His saying: 'and the possible' means in respect of Him, Glory be to Him and Most High, rationally, and it is conjoined to His saying: 'what has become necessary'. And they defined it as: what is valid in the intellect for existence sometimes and non-existence at other times; either by necessity, like the motion of a body or its rest, or theoretically, like the punishment of the obedient even if he is infallible, but excessive talk concerning the prophets, only to the extent of the necessity of teaching, and the reward of the disobedient even if he is a disbeliever; because the discussion is about rational possibility, so it does not contradict that such is impossible according to law. And from that it is known that the possible is two divisions: necessary and theoretical. His saying: 'and the impossible' means the impossible (al-mustahil) in respect of Him Most High, and they defined it as: what is not conceivable in the intellect its existence, with the verbal noun construed for the agent or for the object as previously in the definition of the necessary. And it is two divisions: necessary-by-necessity, like a body's being devoid of both motion and rest simultaneously.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage defines the possible (al-ja'iz) and the impossible (al-mumtani') in relation to God and His messengers, following the verse line from the Jawharat al-Tawhid. The commentator first explains the phrase 'and the possible' (wa al-ja'iza) as referring to what is rationally possible in respect of God, i.e., what the intellect can conceive as either existing or not existing. This possibility is of two types: by necessity (daruri), such as a body being in motion or at rest (i.e., it necessarily must be one or the other), and theoretical (nazari), such as God punishing the obedient or rewarding the disobedient, which the intellect finds equally possible, even if revelation forbids it in some cases. The point is that rational possibility is not negated by religious impossibility. The possible thus divides into necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari). Then, the commentator turns to the impossible (al-mumtani'), defining it as what cannot be conceived by the intellect to exist—using the same active/passive construction as in the definition of the necessary. This too divides into two types: the necessary-by-necessity (daruri), like a body being devoid of both motion and rest at the same time—a contradiction that the intellect necessarily judges impossible.

Key Terms
الجائز
الممتنع
المستحيل
الضروري
النظري
الإمكان العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is rationally possible, it must be religiously permissible.
Correction: Rational possibility only concerns what the intellect can conceive; religious law may prohibit some rationally possible acts.

Checkpoint
What is an example given of a theoretical (nazari) possible in relation to God?
A) A body being in motion or at rest.
B) Punishing the obedient even if infallible.
C) A body being devoid of motion and rest simultaneously.
D) The existence of God.

Key Takeaway
The possible (al-ja'iz) is what can be conceived as existing or not, and the impossible (al-mumtani') is what cannot be conceived; both divide into necessary and theoretical, and rational possibility is independent of religious permissibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that rational possibility is distinct from religious permissibility. Reflect on a belief you hold about God that you assume is rationally necessary – could it be only theoretically possible according to this lesson? Will you allow that distinction to deepen your trust in His revelation over your own reasoning?
?
The passage says the impossible is what the intellect cannot conceive. Have you ever dismissed something as impossible in your spiritual life – like forgiveness for a repeated sin or a change in your heart – only because your own imagination failed to conceive it? Will you now pause and ask whether it is truly rationally impossible, or just beyond your current hope?
?
Consider the example of God rewarding the disobedient – rationally possible though religiously impossible. Does your soul resent that possibility? Or does it humble you, knowing that His mercy is not bound by your sense of justice? What specific fear or expectation in your life today needs to be surrendered to His absolute freedom?

Definition and Division of the Possible
Defines al-ja'iz and divides it into necessary and theoretical types, with a footnote on possibility in itself vs. in God's right.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-ja'iz (the possible) as a rational category, its two subdivisions (ḍarūrī and naẓarī), and the crucial distinction between possibility in itself and possibility in God’s right.

Core Idea
Rational possibility is mental conceivability, not legal permissibility.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Murid defines one of the three rational judgments: al-ja'iz (the possible). The commentator first explains what it means for something to be possible in the mind, then divides it into two types, and finally clarifies a subtle but essential distinction between possibility considered in itself and possibility considered in relation to God's power. Understanding this distinction is key to avoiding a common theological confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage (including the footnote) and its literal translation. Pay attention to how the definition is structured and where the division into two types appears.
The passage starts with 'قوله : (وَالجَائز) أي : فى حقه سبحانه وتعالى عقلاً'.
2
Identify the definition and division
Find the two key parts: (1) the definition of al-ja'iz as 'what is sound in the mind to exist at one time and not exist at another', and (2) its division into necessary ($\text{ضروري}$) and theoretical ($\text{نظري}$). Note the examples given for each.
3
Grasp the two types of possibility
The footnote introduces a crucial distinction: possibility in itself (جواز في نفسه) vs. possibility in His right (جواز في حقه). Understand that the first is abstract suitability for existence or non-existence, while the second is being within God's power. The required belief is the second type.
This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction when we say something is rationally possible but legally impossible.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question about the two types of al-ja'iz. Then explain the difference between the two types of possibility in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَالجَائز) أي : فى حقه سبحانه وتعالى عقلاً، وهو معطوف على قوله : (ما قد وجبا)، وقد عرَّفوه بأنه : ما يصح في العقل وجوده تارةً وعدمه أخرى؛ إما ضرورة؛ كحركة الجرم أو سكونه، أو نظراً ؛ كتعذيب المطيع ولو معصوماً، لكن التمشدق في حق الأنبياء، بل بقدرِ ضرورة التعليم، وإثابة العاصي ولو كافراً؛ لأن الكلام في الإمكان العقلي، فلا ينافي أن ذلك ممتنع شرعاً، وعُلمَ من ذلك أن الجائز قسمان: ضروري ونظري . (١) قوله : (والجائز أي : في حقه سبحانه وتعالى) إيضاح هذا أن الجائز المقابل للواجب والمستحيل له جوازان جواز في نفسه ؛ وهو صلاحيته في نفسه ؛ أي : بقطع النظر عنه تعالى، للوجود والعدم، وجواز في حقه تعالى؛ وهو كونه في قبضته سبحانه وتعالى، بمعنى أنه في حال عدمه إن شاء الله أبقاه على عدمه، وإن شاء أوجده، وفي حال وجوده إن شاء أعدمه، وإن شاء أبقاه على وجوده والواجب على المكلف اعتقاد الجواز الثاني، بأن يعتقد أن كل ما هو جائز في نفسه فهو جائز في حقه تعالى، وقد علمت المغايرة بين جوازه في نفسه وجوازه في حَقِّه، فلا ركاكة في قولنا : (الجائز في حقه تعالى كل ممكن ؛ لأن المراد منه أن كل ما أمكن في نفسه .. أي: صلح في نفسه للوجود والعدم، كان جائزاً في حقه تعالى بمعنى أنه في قبضته. انتهى أجهوري.
His saying: (And the possible) i.e., in His right, glorified and exalted, rationally, and it is conjoined to his saying: (what is necessary). And they defined it as: that which is sound in the mind that it exists sometimes and is absent at other times; either necessarily, like the motion or rest of a body, or theoretically, like punishing the obedient even if he were infallible, but exaggeration regarding the prophets is limited to the extent of necessary teaching, and rewarding the disobedient even if he were a disbeliever; because the discussion is about rational possibility, so it does not contradict that such is legally impossible. And from that it is known that the possible is of two types: necessary and theoretical. (1) His saying: (And the possible i.e., in His right, glorified and exalted) clarification of this is that the possible opposite of the necessary and the impossible has two types of possibility: possibility in itself, which is its suitability in itself, i.e., setting aside regard for Him exalted, for existence and non-existence; and possibility in His right, which is its being in His grasp, glorified and exalted, meaning that in its state of non-existence, if God wills He keeps it in non-existence, and if He wills He brings it into existence; and in its state of existence, if He wills He annihilates it, and if He wills He keeps it in existence. And it is obligatory upon the accountable person to believe in the second type of possibility, by believing that everything that is possible in itself is possible in His right, and you have known the difference between its possibility in itself and its possibility in His right, so there is no weakness in our saying: 'The possible in His right, exalted, is every possible thing'; because what is meant is that everything that is possible in itself, i.e., suitable in itself for existence and non-existence, is possible in His right in the sense that it is in His grasp. End of al-Ajhuri.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage defines the rational category of 'al-ja'iz' (the possible) and explains its subdivision. The commentator first identifies the phrase from the poem and clarifies that the possibility is considered 'in His right' (i.e., in relation to God) and rationally, not legally. The definition given is: that which the mind can conceive existing at one time and not existing at another. This is divided into two types: al-ḍarūrī (necessary) and al-naẓarī (theoretical). The commentator provides examples: motion or rest of a body is a necessary possible because the mind instantly judges it as possible; punishing the obedient or rewarding the disobedient is theoretical because it requires reasoning. The commentator notes that such possibilities are rational, so they are not contradicted by legal impossibility. The attached footnote clarifies a subtle point: 'possible in His right' does not mean the same as 'possible in itself'. A thing is possible in itself if its existence and non-existence are both conceivable in the abstract. It is possible in His right if it is within God's power to bring it into existence or not. The required belief is the second type: that everything possible in itself is possible in God's right, i.e., subject to His will.

Key Terms
الجائز
الضروري
النظري
الإمكان العقلي
جواز في نفسه
جواز في حقه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'possible in itself' and 'possible in God's right' are the same, or that rational possibility implies legal permissibility.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes the two: possibility in itself is mere logical conceivability, while possibility in God's right means it is subject to His power. Rational possibility does not mean it must occur or be lawful.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two types of al-ja'iz (the possible)?
A) Daruri and nazari
B) Wajib and mumtani'
C) 'Aqli and shar'i
D) Qadim and muhdath

Key Takeaway
Al-ja'iz is defined as what the mind can conceive as both existing and not existing; it is divided into necessary and theoretical, and crucially, everything possible in itself is possible in God's right — that is the required belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for something seemingly impossible, do you truly believe it is possible for God to grant it? Or does your heart limit His power based on your own reasoning? How can you train yourself to hold the belief that everything possible in itself is fully in God's grasp?
?
Think of a time you felt that something God decreed was 'unfair'. Did you consider that rationally it is possible for God to do it, even if it seems unjust to you? How does the distinction between rational possibility and legal permissibility (i.e., what God actually does) help you submit to His wisdom?
?
The lesson says that the required belief is that everything possible in itself is possible in God's right. Is there an area in your life where you resist accepting that God has full power over it? What would it take for you to genuinely believe that He can bring about or prevent anything, regardless of your own plans?

4 min
Definition and Division of the Impossible
Sanusi defines al-mumtani' and gives its two divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the impossible means in kalam and how it is divided into necessary and theoretical types.

Core Idea
The impossible is what the mind cannot conceive, and its types mirror those of the possible.

This lesson introduces the second of the three rational judgments: the impossible (al-mumtani'). Just as the necessary was defined by its necessary existence, the impossible is defined by its impossible existence. Sanusi provides a definition and then divides it into two kinds, paralleling the divisions of the necessary and the possible. This completes the picture of how reason categorizes all that can be thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic definition of al-mumtani' and its translation. Notice the symmetry with the definition of the necessary.
2
Identify the two types
The passage divides the impossible into necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī). Understand each type and its example.
The necessary impossible is instantly clear; the theoretical requires reasoning.
3
Apply the concept
Consider why a body being both motionless and restful is a necessary impossibility, and why a partner to God is theoretical.
This helps internalize the division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وَالمُمْتَنِعَا) أي: المستحيل في حقِّهِ تعالى وعرَّفوه بأنه : ما لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل وجوده، ببناء الفعل للفاعل أو للمفعول كما تقدَّمَ في تعريف الواجب. وهو قسمان : ضروري: كخلوّ الجرم عن الحركة والسكون معاً. ونظري : كالشريك له تعالى.
His saying: (And the impossible) i.e., the impossible in His right, exalted. And they defined it as: that whose existence is not conceivable in the mind, with the verb constructed for the active or passive subject as preceded in the definition of the necessary. And it is of two types: necessary, like a body being devoid of motion and rest simultaneously; and theoretical, like a partner to Him exalted.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage defines al-mumtani' (the impossible) and gives its division. The commentator explains that the term refers to what is rationally inconceivable in relation to God. The definition is the opposite of the necessary: it is that whose existence the mind cannot conceive at all. Two types follow: al-ḍarūrī (necessary impossible) is instantly recognized as impossible, such as a body being neither in motion nor at rest; al-naẓarī (theoretical impossible) requires reasoning, such as the existence of a partner to God. The passage preserves the symmetry with the divisions of the necessary and the possible.

Key Terms
الممتنع
المستحيل
الضروري
النظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'impossible' in kalam refers to physical impossibility (like flying), not logical impossibility.
Correction: Here 'impossible' means logically impossible, i.e., that which cannot be conceived by the mind, such as contradictions.

Checkpoint
What is the example given for the necessary impossible (al-mumtani' al-ḍarūrī)?
A) A partner to God
B) A body being devoid of motion and rest simultaneously
C) The existence of the world
D) The non-existence of the Creator

Key Takeaway
The impossible is what cannot be conceived, and it divides into the immediately obvious (necessary) and the reasoned (theoretical).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that God cannot have a partner, is that a mere intellectual acceptance, or does it grip your heart with the reality that nothing in your life deserves the devotion due to Him alone?
?
Consider the necessary impossible: a body cannot be both moving and still at the same time. Is there any area in your life where you are trying to combine two opposites—like trusting God while clinging to worldly security? What would it take to resolve that contradiction?
?
The theoretical impossible requires proof to be known. Have you ever lazily accepted a belief about God without truly reasoning it through? Which belief about Allah are you holding without evidence, and will you commit to seeking its proof today?

Six Rational Judgments and Their Illustration
Summary of six divisions, example, and definition of intellect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the three rational judgments (necessary, possible, impossible) each have two subdivisions (necessary and theoretical), making six total, and how a concrete example of motion and rest illustrates them, and finally the significance of these judgments as defining the intellect according to Imam al-Haramayn.

Core Idea
The three rational judgments expand to six divisions and define the very faculty of intellect.

The commentator now brings together the earlier discussion by showing that each of the three categories—necessary, possible, impossible—has two aspects: one known necessarily and one known through reasoning. This yields six distinct judgments. To make this concrete, he provides a crisp example using motion and rest. Finally, he highlights why mastering these distinctions matters: because Imam al-Haramayn equated the intellect itself with the knowledge of these three categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the full meaning.
2
Understand the six-fold division
Note that each of the three categories (necessary, possible, impossible) splits into necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī), giving six types.
3
Study the motion and rest example
See how the example of a body's motion or rest illustrates the necessary, impossible, and possible. The necessary is that one state must exist (unspecified), the impossible is the absence of both, the possible is that a specific state occurs.
4
Reflect on the definition of intellect
Imam al-Haramayn defines the intellect as the knowledge of these three categories. Consider why this definition is significant for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتلخص أن كل واحد من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة ينقسم قسمين: ضروري ونظري، فالجميعُ ستّةٌ ، وقد مرَّ تمثيلها . قال بعضهم : ويمكن تمثيل الأقسام الثلاثةِ بحركة الجرم وسكونه، فالواجب : أحدهما لا بعينه والمستحيل خلوه منهما جميعاً، والجائز: ثبوتُ أحدهما معيَّناً بدلاً عن الآخر. وينبغي الاعتناء بهذه الأحكام ؛ لأن إمام الحرمين يقول بأن معرفتها هي العقل؛ بناءً على أنه العلم بوجوب الواجبات وجواز الجائزات واستحالة المستحيلات.
Thus it is summarized that each of these three categories divides into two types: necessary and theoretical, so the total is six, and their examples have preceded. Some said: And it is possible to represent the three categories by the motion and rest of a body: the necessary is one of them not specifically, the impossible is its being devoid of both, and the possible is the establishment of one of them specifically instead of the other. And it is appropriate to pay attention to these judgments because Imam al-Haramayn says that knowledge of them is the intellect, based on its being the knowledge of the necessity of necessary things, the possibility of possible things, and the impossibility of impossible things.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage provides a summary and a concrete illustration. The commentator first restates that each of the three rational categories (necessary, possible, impossible) is subdivided into necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī), yielding six types. Then an alternative example is offered using the motion and rest of a body: the necessary is that one of them (motion or rest) must exist without specifying which; the impossible is that the body be devoid of both; the possible is that a specific one (e.g., motion) occurs instead of the other. Finally, the commentator stresses the importance of these concepts, citing Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), who identified the intellect itself with the knowledge of these three categories: knowing what is necessarily true, what is possibly true, and what is impossibly true.

Key Terms
أقسام ثلاثة
ضروري
نظري
الحركة
السكون
إمام الحرمين
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the intellect is merely the faculty of reasoning, whereas here it is defined as actual knowledge of the three categories.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that Imam al-Haramayn defines the intellect as the knowledge of the necessity, possibility, and impossibility of things, not just the capacity to reason.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does Imam al-Haramayn define 'al-'aql' (the intellect)?
A) The ability to reason
B) Knowledge of the necessity, possibility, and impossibility of things
C) Logical deduction
D) Instinctive understanding

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments yield six distinct epistemological states, and the intellect is defined as the knowledge of these three categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that knowledge of the necessary, possible, and impossible is the very definition of intellect. Look at your daily decisions: where are you acting as if something forbidden is possible, or as if something obligatory is optional? What does that reveal about your intellect—and what will you do about it today?
?
If the intellect is not simply a faculty but is specific knowledge, then ignorance of these three categories is a form of intellectual deficiency. In what area of your life have you avoided asking whether something is truly necessary or impossible? Will you write down one necessary truth you must affirm and one impossibility you must reject before you sleep tonight?

The Six Subdivisions of Rational Judgments
Sanusi summarizes the three judgments and their subdivision and gives an alternative example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) each subdivide into necessary (dharuri) and theoretical (nadhari), totalling six categories, and how this is exemplified through physical motion.

Core Idea
Each rational judgment comes in two forms: necessary and theoretical — making six in total.

In this passage, Sanusi concludes his discussion of the three rational judgments by showing that each one has two modes: necessary (known automatically) and theoretical (requiring proof). He then offers a concrete analogy using the movement of a body to illustrate the three categories. This lesson unpacks that summary and analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening summary
Read the Arabic passage from 'فتلخص' to 'بدلاً عن الآخر' and its translation. Notice how the author states the sixfold division.
The previous examples are referenced but not repeated here.
2
Understand the alternative analogy
The analogy of a body's motion and rest clarifies the three judgments. Reflect on how necessary, impossible, and possible apply to movement and rest.
The necessary is one of them unspecified, not both.
3
Connect to previous examples
Recall examples from earlier lessons in Tuhfat al-Mureed. This summary ties them together into a systematic framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإلهيات
ونظري : كالشريك له تعالى.
فتلخص أن كل واحد من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة ينقسم قسمين: ضروري ونظري، فالجميعُ ستّةٌ ، وقد مرَّ تمثيلها .
قال بعضهم : ويمكن تمثيل الأقسام الثلاثةِ بحركة الجرم وسكونه، فالواجب : أحدهما لا بعينه والمستحيل خلوه منهما جميعاً، والجائز: ثبوتُ أحدهما معيَّناً بدلاً عن الآخر. وينبغي الاعتناء بهذه الأحكام ؛ لأن إمام الحرمين (١) يقول بأن معرفتها هي العقل؛ بناءً على أنه العلم بوجوب الواجبات وجواز الجائزات واستحالة المستحيلات (۲).
(۱) قوله : (لأن إمام الحرمين ... إلى آخره قيل : المراد بالمعرفة التي جعلها إمام الحرمين نفس العقل تصور المفاهيم الثلاثة : بأن يتصور أن الواجب ما لا يقبل العدم، وأن المستحيل ما لا يقبل الوجود، وأن الجائز ما يصحُ وُجوده وعدمه . وقيل : المراد بتلك المعرفة: التصديق ببعض الضروريات من الأقسام الثلاثة، كأن يصدق بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين، وبأن النار حارة، وأن النقيضين لا يجتمعان : فكون الواحد نصف الاثنين واجب ضرورةً، وثبوت الحرارة للنار جائز ضرورة، واجتماع النقيضين مستحيل ضرورةً، فالتصديق بذلك وما شابهه هو العقل بناءً على هذا القول، يُؤخذ ذلك من حاشية الشرقاوي على الهدهدي». انتهى أجهوري (۲) انظر البرهانه له (۱۹/۱).
Divine matters. And theoretical: like a partner to Him, exalted. So it is concluded that each one of these three divisions is divided into two parts: necessary and theoretical, and thus all are six, and their examples have already passed. Some said: It is possible to represent the three divisions by the movement of a body and its rest; thus the necessary: one of them not specifically, and the impossible: its being devoid of both, and the possible: the establishment of one of them specifically in place of the other. And it is proper to pay attention to these judgments, because Imam al-Haramayn says that knowledge of them is the intellect, based on it being knowledge of the necessity of necessary things, the possibility of possible things, and the impossibility of impossible things. (1) His statement: 'Because Imam al-Haramayn... to its end, it is said: what is meant by knowledge which Imam al-Haramayn made the intellect itself is the conception of the three concepts: that one conceives that the necessary is what does not admit non-existence, the impossible is what does not admit existence, and the possible is what admits both existence and non-existence. And it is said: what is meant by that knowledge is affirmation of some necessary matters from the three divisions, such as one affirms that one is half of two, that fire is hot, and that two contradictories do not combine: thus that one is half of two is necessary necessarily, the establishment of heat for fire is possible necessarily, and the combination of contradictories is impossible necessarily. So affirmation of that and what resembles it is the intellect according to this view. This is taken from the gloss of al-Sharqawi on al-Hudhudi.' Ended al-Ujhuri. (2) See his al-Burhan (19/1).
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a summary and elaboration on the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) and their subdivisions. It begins by stating that divine matters (al-ilahiyyat) include both necessary and theoretical aspects, exemplified by 'a partner to Him' as a theoretical impossibility. Then it concludes that each of the three divisions (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) is further divided into two: dharuri (necessary) and nadhari (theoretical), making six total, with examples previously given. Next, an alternative representation is offered using the movement of a body and its rest: the necessary is one of them without specification, the impossible is the absence of both, and the possible is the specific occurrence of one in place of the other. The author then stresses the importance of these judgments, citing Imam al-Haramayn who states that knowledge of these three categories is the intellect itself, meaning the intellect is defined as the knowledge of the necessity of necessary things, the possibility of possible things, and the impossibility of impossible things. The footnote expands on this: some interpret this knowledge as the conception of the three concepts (necessary as what cannot not exist, impossible as what cannot exist, possible as what can both exist and not exist). Others interpret it as the affirmation of certain necessary propositions from the three categories, such as necessary truths (e.g., one is half of two), possible truths (e.g., fire is hot), and impossible truths (e.g., two contradictories cannot coexist). The affirmation of such propositions is, according to this view, the intellect. This summary reinforces that the three rational judgments are fundamental to rational thinking in kalam.

Key Terms
الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز
ضروري
نظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The alternative example of motion and rest introduces a different classification.
Correction: It is merely a concrete illustration of the same three rational judgments, not a new set.

Checkpoint
How many total subdivisions result from dividing each of the three rational judgments into necessary and theoretical?
A) Three
B) Six
C) Nine
D) Twelve

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments each have two forms: necessary and theoretical, making six fundamental categories of rational evaluation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How many of your core beliefs about God are based on necessary (dharuri) knowledge versus theoretical (nadhari) proof? Which areas are you merely accepting without reflection, and are you comfortable with that?
?
If someone asked you to prove that God cannot have a partner, would you answer with scripture alone or with a rational argument from this lesson? What does your answer reveal about your own reliance on reason?
?
The lesson shows that even 'obvious' judgments like a body needing to be moving or at rest have a necessary and theoretical side. What is one belief you hold about God that you once thought was self-evident but now realize requires deeper intellectual work?

The Intellect and the Three Judgments
The author emphasizes the importance of the three judgments and cites Imam al-Haramayn's definition of intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Haramayn considers knowledge of the three rational judgments to be the intellect itself, and the two interpretations of this claim.

Core Idea
The intellect is defined by its grasp of the necessary, possible, and impossible.

The author stresses the importance of these judgments by citing Imam al-Haramayn's famous statement that knowledge of them is the intellect. This passage explains that claim and offers two interpretations: one focusing on conception, the other on affirmation. This lesson examines that debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on importance
Read the Arabic from 'وينبغي الاعتناء' to the end and its translation. Note the claim that knowledge of judgments is the intellect.
2
Understand the two interpretations
In the footnote, two interpretations are given: conception of definitions or affirmation of basic necessary propositions. Compare them.
The first is conceptual, the second is judgmental.
3
Reflect on implications
Consider how these views affect the study of kalam: the intellect is not separate from its content.

Source Text

قال بعضهم : ويمكن تمثيل الأقسام الثلاثةِ بحركة الجرم وسكونه، فالواجب : أحدهما لا بعينه والمستحيل خلوه منهما جميعاً، والجائز: ثبوتُ أحدهما معيَّناً بدلاً عن الآخر. وينبغي الاعتناء بهذه الأحكام ؛ لأن إمام الحرمين (١) يقول بأن معرفتها هي العقل؛ بناءً على أنه العلم بوجوب الواجبات وجواز الجائزات واستحالة المستحيلات (۲).

Divine matters. And theoretical: like a partner to Him, exalted. So it is concluded that each one of these three divisions is divided into two parts: necessary and theoretical, and thus all are six, and their examples have already passed. Some said: It is possible to represent the three divisions by the movement of a body and its rest; thus the necessary: one of them not specifically, and the impossible: its being devoid of both, and the possible: the establishment of one of them specifically in place of the other. And it is said: what is meant by that knowledge is affirmation of some necessary matters from the three divisions, such as one affirms that one is half of two, that fire is hot, and that two contradictories do not combine: thus that one is half of two is necessary necessarily, the establishment of heat for fire is possible necessarily, and the combination of contradictories is impossible necessarily. So affirmation of that and what resembles it is the intellect according to this view. This is taken from the gloss of al-Sharqawi on al-Hudhudi.' Ended al-Ujhuri. (2) See his al-Burhan (19/1).

Commentary

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult situation, do you ever dismiss a solution as 'impossible' without careful thought? Reflect on a specific instance where you might have confused subjective difficulty with objective impossibility. What would it mean to align your mind with the precise definition of impossibility from this lesson—'what does not admit existence'?
?
Imam al-Haramayn equates the intellect itself with knowledge of the three judgments. If you truly internalized this, how would it change the way you evaluate your own beliefs? Consider one belief you hold about religion or life—is it based on necessary, possible, or impossible categories? Does your certainty match the modal status?
?
The passage gives two interpretations of what 'knowledge' means. Pause and ask: Do you tend to merely conceive of truth (like definitions) without affirming it in your actions? Where in your life are you just 'conceiving' the right path but not 'affirming' it through decisive action? What is one step you can take today to move from conception to affirmation?

Understanding 'Mithl' for the Messengers
Commentary on the line 'Wa mithla dha li-rusulihi' explaining grammatical readings and doctrinal nuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author uses 'mithl' to describe the categories for messengers and the grammatical flexibility in the poem.

Core Idea
The categories for messengers resemble those for God in kind, not in identity.

This passage clarifies a subtle point in the poem: the three rational judgments apply to the messengers as well, but they are not identical to those for God. The author carefully uses 'mithl' to indicate similarity, and the commentator explains two valid grammatical readings of the word.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the commentary. Pay attention to the two readings of 'mithl'.
2
Identify the two grammatical readings
Notice that 'mithl' can be read in the nominative (raf') or accusative (nasb), affecting the sentence structure.
The nominative makes it an independent clause; the accusative connects it to an earlier clause.
3
Understand the meaning of 'mithl'
The commentator explains that 'mithl' indicates similarity, not identity. The categories for messengers are similar in kind but differ in specifics and proofs.
This prevents the mistake of equating God and His messengers.
4
Note why messengers are specified
The passage says some matters like delivering the message are unique to messengers, not prophets. This justifies the focus on messengers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَمِثْلَ ذَا لِرُسُلِهِ) يجوز قراءة (مثل) بالرفع، فتكون الجملة مستأنفة؛ أي: مبتدأ وخبراً، والتقدير : ومثل ذلك كائن لرسله، ويجوز قراءته بالنصب عطفاً على (ما قد وجبا وما بعده والتقدير : ووجب عليه أن يعرف مثل ذلك لرسله . وإفراد اسم الإشارة مع عوده المتعدد نظراً لتأويله بالمذكور الذي هو الواجب والمستحيلُ والجائز، وأشار المصنف بلفظ (مثل) إلى أن الواجب في حقهم عليهم الصلاة والسلام والمستحيل والجائز، ليست هي عين الواجب في حقه تعالى والمستحيل والجائز، فالمراد : المثليَّة في مطلق واجب وجائز ومستحيل وإن اختلفت الأفراد والأدلة . وإنما خص الرسل لأن بعض ما يأتي كالتبليغ خاص بهم دونَ الأنبياء. وقوله : (فَاسْتَمِعا) بقلب نون التوكيد الخفيفة ألفاً فى الوقف؛ كما قاله ابن مالك : من الرجز] وَأَبْدِلَنْها بَعْدَ فَتْحِ أَلِفَا وَقْفاً كَما تَقُولُ فِي قِفَنْ : قِفَا(۱) أي : فاستمعَنْ ما ألقي إليك من الأمور التي معرفتها ترفعُكَ عن الجهل والتقليد استماع تدبر وتفهم، فهو وإن كان تكملة مفيد لما تقدَّم.
His statement: 'And the like of this to His messengers' – it is permissible to read 'mithl' in the nominative, making the clause independent: i.e., a subject and predicate, the estimation being: and the like of that exists for His messengers. And it is permissible to read it in the accusative as a conjunct to 'what has been obligatory' and what is after it, the estimation being: and it is obligatory upon Him to know the like of that for His messengers. And the singularization of the demonstrative pronoun despite its referring to multiple things is due to its interpretation as the mentioned (madhkur) which is the necessary, impossible, and possible. And the author indicated by the word 'mithl' that the necessary, impossible, and possible regarding them, peace and blessings be upon them, are not the very same as the necessary, impossible, and possible regarding Him, exalted; rather, the intent is similarity in absolute necessary, possible, and impossible, even if the individuals and proofs differ. And he specified the messengers only because some of what follows, like delivering the message, is specific to them rather than the prophets (who are not messengers). And his statement: 'fastami'a' – by converting the light nun of emphasis into an alif upon pause, as Ibn Malik said: [from al-rajaz:] 'And replace it after a fatha with an alif as a pause, just as you say in 'qifan': 'qifa' (1), meaning: listen to what is cast to you of matters whose knowledge raises you from ignorance and blind following, with a listening of reflection and understanding; for it, although it is a completion, benefits what preceded.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage provides detailed commentary on the line 'Wa mithla dha li-rusulihi' from the poem. It begins by explaining two permissible grammatical readings of the word 'mithl': in the nominative (raf') as an independent clause (subject and predicate), meaning 'the like of that exists for His messengers,' or in the accusative (nasb) as a conjunct to an earlier clause, meaning 'and it is obligatory upon Him to know the like of that for His messengers.' The commentator then explains why the demonstrative pronoun 'dha' is singular despite referring to multiple things (the three categories): it is interpreted as a reference to the 'mentioned' (madhkur) which collectively denotes the necessary, impossible, and possible. The author's use of 'mithl' (like) indicates that the necessary, impossible, and possible with respect to the messengers are not identical to those with respect to God; they are similar in type (absolute necessity, possibility, impossibility) but differ in the specific instances and proofs. The passage then notes why only messengers are specified: because some matters that follow, particularly delivering the message (tabligh), are specific to messengers and not to other prophets. Finally, the verse 'fastami'a' is explained: the nun of emphasis is replaced with an alif at pause, as Ibn Malik stated. The commentator interprets 'fastami'a' as an imperative to listen attentively with reflection and understanding to what is presented, noting that these matters elevate one from ignorance and blind following (taqlid).

تبليغ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The necessary, impossible, and possible for God and for messengers are exactly the same.
Correction: The word 'mithl' indicates similarity in kind, not identity. The categories are similar in absolute terms but differ in specific instances and proofs.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage emphasize the use of 'mithl' (like) when referring to the necessary, impossible, and possible for the messengers?
A) To show they are exactly the same as for God
B) To indicate a similarity in kind but not identity
C) To deny that messengers have any of these categories
D) To restrict these categories only to messengers

Key Takeaway
The three categories for the messengers are similar in kind to those for God but differ in specific instances and proofs.

The author carefully distinguishes similarity in kind from identity. Have you ever thought of Allah and the prophets as having the same qualities in a way that diminishes Allah's transcendence? How can you adjust your understanding to respect the difference?
?
The imperative 'fastami'a' calls for reflective listening. When was the last time you truly sat with a theological text, not just read it but listened with reflection? Will you set aside time to deeply ponder this passage and its implications for your relationship with Allah and His messengers?

The Correct Opinion on the Obligation of Intellectual Reflection
The commentator declares the third opinion correct and clarifies the absolute nature of the dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct position regarding the obligation of intellectual reflection (naẓar) and why the dispute is considered absolute in scope.

Core Idea
The dispute over the obligation of nazar is absolute—it applies to all types of reflection and all people.

Sanusi's commentator now identifies the correct opinion among the three views on the obligation of intellectual reflection. He clarifies that the disagreement is not limited to certain types of reflection or certain people. This passage sets the record straight on both points.

Guided Path
1
Identify the correct opinion
The commentator states that the third opinion is the correct one. Note that he does not enumerate the first two here, but affirms this one.
2
Understand what 'absolute' means
He explains that the dispute over obligation applies to both reflection leading to knowledge of God and other reflections, contrary to those who restrict it.
This counters a common restriction.
3
Notice the second clarification
He then adds that the dispute also applies to all people, whether from cities or remote mountains, contrary to those who restrict it to isolated individuals.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does it matter that the dispute is absolute? It ensures that the obligation of nazar is universal.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقولُ الحقُّ الذي عليه المعوَّلُ من هذه الأقوال : القول الثالث، والصواب أن هذا الخلاف مطلق؛ أي جارٍ في النظر الموصل لمعرفة الله تعالى وفي غيره ؛ كالنظر الموصل لمعرفة الرسل، خلافاً لمن خصَّ الخلاف بالنظر غير الموصل لمعرفة الله تعالى، وقال: أما النظر الموصل لمعرفة الله تعالى فهو واجب بالإجماع، وقد جرى على ذلك الشيخ عبد السلام والراجح أنه لا فرق في هذا الخلاف بين أهل الأمصار والقرى، وبين من نشأ في شاهق جبل، خلافاً لمن خصّه بمن نشأ في شاهق جبل دون أهل الأمصار والقرى وقد جرى على ذلك الشيخ عبد السلام أيضاً
And the correct statement upon which reliance is placed among these statements is the third statement. And the correct view is that this dispute is absolute, i.e., it applies to reflection that leads to knowledge of God Most High and to other things, such as reflection that leads to knowledge of the messengers, contrary to the one who restricted the dispute to reflection that does not lead to knowledge of God Most High, and said: 'As for reflection that leads to knowledge of God Most High, it is obligatory by consensus.' And Sheikh Abd al-Salam adhered to that. And the more correct view is that there is no difference in this dispute between the people of cities and villages and between one who grew up on a high mountain, contrary to the one who restricted it to one who grew up on a high mountain excluding the people of cities and villages. And Sheikh Abd al-Salam also adhered to that.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a classification and ruling. It identifies the correct position among three opinions (not enumerated here) regarding the obligation of intellectual reflection (naẓar). The commentator states that the third opinion is the correct one, upon which reliance is placed. He then clarifies that the dispute among scholars about whether nazar is obligatory is absolute (muṭlaq), meaning it applies to both reflection that leads to knowledge of God and reflection that leads to other matters, such as knowledge of the prophets. This is contrary to those who restrict the dispute only to reflection that does not lead to knowledge of God, claiming that reflection leading to God is obligatory by consensus. The commentator notes that Sheikh Abd al-Salam held this (restricted) view. Then he adds a further clarification: the more correct view (al-rājiḥ) is that there is no difference in this dispute between people of cities and villages and those who grew up in isolation on a high mountain. This is contrary to those who restrict the dispute only to the latter. Again, Sheikh Abd al-Salam is cited as holding that restrictive view. The passage thus establishes the broader scope of the debate on nazar.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the obligation of nazar is only disputed for reflection that does not lead to knowledge of God, or that it only applies to isolated individuals.
Correction: The passage makes clear that the dispute is absolute: it applies to all types of reflection (including that leading to knowledge of God) and to all people (including city dwellers).

Checkpoint

B) The dispute is absolute and applies to all types of reflection.

Key Takeaway
The correct view is that the obligation of naẓar is a universal dispute, not limited to certain types of reflection or certain individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If you haven't, what is holding you back?
?

The Proof of Temporality from Wondrous Design
Verse and commentary demonstrating the world's temporality through design.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intricate design in creation serves as a rational proof for the world's temporality and the existence of a Creator.

Core Idea
Wondrous design in creation proves the world is temporal and created by Allah.

The author presents a key verse that encapsulates one of the main rational proofs for the temporality of the world. By examining the intricate craftsmanship in creation, we can deduce that the world is contingent and depends on a Creator. This lesson unpacks the verse and its commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Arabic verse and its English translation carefully. Notice the concise phrasing: 'You find therein a wondrous art of wisdoms, but with it the proof of temporality is established.' This sets up the argument.
The verse is the foundation of the proof.
2
Understand 'صنعاً بديع الحكم'
The commentary explains that 'صنعاً' refers to amazing craftsmanship, a metaphor for created accidents. 'بديع الحكم' means an invention without precedent, with 'الحكم' being plural of 'حكمة' meaning precision. Together they highlight the intricate, wise design in creation.
This phrase is the core of the evidence.
3
See the logical inference
The commentary then states that such precise design indicates the Creator's attributes of knowledge, power, will, and life. Because only one possessing these can produce such work. This leads to the conclusion that the world is temporal (contingent) and depends on a Creator.
The proof is rational, based on observed order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٦ - تَجِدْ بِهِ صُنْعاً بَدِيعَ الحِكَمِ لكِنْ بِهِ قامَ دَلِيلُ العَدَمِ. قوله: (تَجِدْ بِهِ صُنْعاً) أي: إن تنظر في أحوال ما ذكر تعلم فيه (صنعاً) بضم الصاد؛ أي: صنعة باهرة، وهي كناية عن الأعراض المخلوقة، فـ(تجد) مجزوم في جواب شرط مقدر ويصح أن يكون مجزوماً في جواب الأمر، والباء بمعنى (في)، والصنع بمعنى الصنعة الباهرة؛ من نقوش متقنة، وألوان مستحسنة ... إلى ما لا يُحصى من الصفات ولا يحيط به إلا خالق الأرض والسماوات، وكلُّ هذا دال على علم صانعه وقدرته وإرادته وحياته ؛ لأنَّ ذلك لا يصدر إلا عمَّن اتصف بما ذكر . قوله : (بَدِيعَ الحِكَم) البديع : المخترَعُ لا على مثال سبق، والحكم ـ بكسر الحاء وفتح الكاف : جمع حكمة بمعنى الإحكام أي الإتقان، وجمعه لتعدُّده بتعدد الصنع الذي هو الصنعة الباهرة.
16. You find therein a wondrous art of wisdoms, but with it the proof of temporality is established. His saying: 'You find therein a wondrous art' means: if you contemplate the conditions of what was mentioned, you will know in it a 'wondrous art' (with ḍamma on ṣād), i.e., an amazing craftsmanship, and it is a metaphor for created accidents. So 'you find' is in the jussive as the apodosis of a suppressed conditional, and it is also correct that it is jussive as the apodosis of an imperative. The 'bā' is in the sense of 'in', and 'al-ṣunʿ' means the amazing craftsmanship: of precise engravings, pleasing colors... to the extent of countless attributes that none encompasses except the Creator of the heavens and the earth. All of this indicates the knowledge, power, will, and life of its Maker, because that only issues from one who is described with what was mentioned. His saying: 'Badīʿ al-ḥikam' – 'al-badīʿ' is the invented without a prior model, and 'al-ḥikam' (with kasr on ḥā and fatḥ on kāf) is the plural of 'ḥikma' meaning precision, i.e., perfection. It is pluralized due to its multiplicity corresponding to the multiplicity of the craft, which is the amazing artistry.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) for the temporality (huduth) of the world. It begins with the verse: 'You find therein a wondrous art of wisdoms, but with it the proof of temporality is established.' The author then explains the verse phrase by phrase. 'You find therein a wondrous art' means that by contemplating the heavens and earth, one sees an amazing craftsmanship, which is a metaphor for the created accidents. The word 'find' is in the jussive mood, indicating the result of a hidden condition: if you look, you will find. The 'art' refers to the intricate designs and colors in creation, countless except by the Creator. All this points to the Creator's attributes of knowledge, power, will, and life, because such art can only come from one possessing these attributes. Then he explains 'badīʿ al-ḥikam': 'badīʿ' means invented without precedent, and 'ḥikam' is plural of 'ḥikma' (precision). The multiplicity of wisdoms corresponds to the multiplicity of the craft, implying that each aspect of creation manifests divine wisdom. Thus, the world's design demonstrates its contingency and dependence on a Creator, establishing its temporality.

Key Terms
الصنع
البديع
الحكم
الأعراض
الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of temporality is based on blind faith, not reason.
Correction: It is a rational argument based on observed order and design in creation.

Checkpoint

B) The created accidents and their precise design
C) The temporary nature of the soul
D) The angels' craftsmanship

Key Takeaway
The intricate design in creation is a clear sign that the world is temporal and depends on a Creator with perfect attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the intricate details in your own body or in nature, do you truly see them as signs of a Creator, or do you just take them for granted? How would your daily awareness change if you paused to see each detail as a proof?

The passage says the art points to the Maker's knowledge, power, will, and life. Which of these attributes do you most need to feel today? Why? And what will you do to strengthen your connection to that attribute?
?
The proof of temporality reminds us that this world is not eternal. Have you been living as if this world will last forever? What is one attachment you need to loosen today because it is only temporal?

The Syllogism of Non-Existence: Proving the World's Creation
The syllogism proving the world's creation from the possibility of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the proof that the world is created based on the possibility of its non-existence.

Core Idea
What can cease to exist cannot be eternal—therefore the world is created.

This passage presents the core syllogism used by Sunni theologians to prove that the world is not eternal. The author, Ibn al-Shannah, states the major premise explicitly, while the minor premise is implied from the earlier discussion of craftsmanship. The commentary by al-Ajhuri unpacks the logic and shows how the conclusion leads to the necessity of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the syllogism's major premise
Read the line 'And everything that non-existence is possible for, upon it absolutely eternity is impossible.' This is the major premise. Notice the decisive language (قطعة).
This premise is universal: it applies to everything that could possibly not exist.
2
Identify the suppressed minor premise
The author assumes you already understand that the world is subject to non-existence because of its changing accidents. Find where the commentator explains that the minor premise is 'understood from the objection'.
The minor premise is: 'The world is subject to non-existence.'
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Follow the syllogism: major premise + minor premise → conclusion: the world cannot be eternal. Then the commentator adds that this implies a Creator.
The conclusion is that the world is originated (حادث) and therefore needs an originator (محدث).
4
Reflect on the proof's purpose
Ask yourself: why is this syllogism the foundation for proving God's existence? Notice that the whole discussion aims at 'reflection leading to knowledge of God.'
This connects the physical world to its Maker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لكن ... إلى آخره ظهر من كلام المحشي أن دليل العدم هو نفس الصنع المذكور في قوله : «تجد به صنعاً»، فكأن المصنف قال: «لكن به قام صنع دال على جواز العدم، وهذا معلوم من قوله : «تجد به صنعاً فلا فائدة فيه، إلا أن يقال : محطّ الفائدة في وصف الصنع بالدلالة على جواز العدم؛ فمعنى قوله : «تجد به صنعاً ... إلى قوله : دليل العدم إلى قوله : دليل العدم . . . » أنك إذا نظرت إليه علمت به صنعة متقنة إتقاناً لم يسبق له مثال دالة على جواز عدمه؛ ف (تجد) بمعنى (تعلم)، منصب على ما قبل الاستدراك وما بعده، هذا ما ظهر من كلامه بعد التأمل). انتهى أجهوري.

الإلهيات

۲۲
حيث كان كذلك فهو قديم، فكأنه قال : لكن العالم وإن كان على غاية من الإتقان حادث، وبحث فيه بأن البديعَ هو المخترَعُ من غير مثال سبق، والمخترع لا يكون إلا حادثاً ، فلا يُتوهَّمُ القدمُ حتى يُحتاج للاستدراك، إلا أن يُقال: ربما يُتوهم من عَجُز التعريف أعني قولهم: (من غير (مثال لا من صدره وهو المخترع، والأقرب أن ؛ : (لكن) هنا لمجرَّدِ التأكيد؛ كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا كَانَ مُحَمَّدٌ أَبَا أَحَدٍ مِّن رِجَالِكُمْ وَلَكِن رَسُولَ اللهِ) [الأحزاب: ١٤٠ (١) .
وقوله : (بِهِ قامَ دَلِيلُ العَدَم أي : بالعالم - بمعنى الأجرام ـ قام دليل جواز العدم، فهو على تقدير مضاف ؛ إذ الفرض أنه موجود والمراد بدليل جواز العدم الأعراضُ الحادثة الملازمة للعالم بمعنى الأجرام.

١٧ - وَكُلُّ مَا جَازَ عَلَيْهِ العَدَمُ عَلَيْهِ قَطعاً يَسْتَحِيلُ القِدَمُ قوله : (وَكُلُّ مَا جَازَ عَلَيْهِ العَدَمُ أي : وكلُّ الذي، أو كلُّ شيء جازَ عليه العدم؛ يعني : الفناء .

وقوله : (عَلَيْهِ قَطعاً يَسْتَحِيلُ القِدَمُ أي: على ما جاز عليه العدم يمتنع القدم جزماً من غير تردُّد، وقد أشار المصنف إلى قياس تركيبه :هكذا العالم من عرشه لفرشه جائز عليه العدم وكل ما جاز عليه العدم استحال عليه القدم ؛ فينتج هذا القياس : أن العالم من عرشه لفرشه استحال عليه القدم، فثبت حدوثه، وإذا ثبت حدوثه فلا بدَّ له من محدث، وهو المطلوب؛ لأن أصل الكلام في النظر الموصل لمعرفة الله تعالى، فطوى المصنف الصغرى لفهمها من الاستدراك، وذكر الكبرى بقوله : وكل ما جاز عليه العدم إلى آخره.

والحاصل (۲) : أنك تثبتُ أولاً حدوث الأعراض بمشاهدة تغيرها من عدم إلى
His statement: (but ... to the end): It became clear from the commentator's words that the proof of non-existence is the very craftsmanship mentioned in his statement: 'you find in it a craftsmanship.' So it is as if the author said: 'But through it a craftsmanship arose indicating the permissibility of non-existence, and this is known from his statement: 'you find in it a craftsmanship' so there is no benefit in it, except that it may be said: The locus of benefit is in describing the craftsmanship as indicating the permissibility of non-existence. So the meaning of his statement: 'you find in it a craftsmanship ... to his statement: proof of non-existence ... to his statement: proof of non-existence ...' is that when you look at it, you know from it a skilled craftsmanship with a perfection that had no previous example, indicating the permissibility of its non-existence. So 'you find' means 'you know', and it applies to what is before the objection and what follows it. This is what appeared from his words after contemplation. End of Ajhuri.

Divine Matters
It is
22
Since it is thus, it is eternal. So it is as if he said: 'But the world, even if it is at the extreme of perfection, is originated.' And he discussed that 'ingenious' is that which is invented without a preceding example, and the invented is only originated, so eternity is not imagined such that an objection is needed, unless it is said: Perhaps it is imagined from the deficiency of the definition, namely their statement: 'without example' not from its beginning, which is 'invented'. And the closest is that 'but' here is merely for emphasis, as in His statement, may He be exalted: 'And Muhammad is not the father of any of your men, but the Messenger of God' [Ahzab 33:40].

And his statement: 'through it the proof of non-existence arose': i.e., through the world — meaning the bodies — arose the proof of the permissibility of non-existence. So it is on the assumption of an addition, since the assumption is that it exists, and by the proof of the permissibility of non-existence is meant the originated accidents that are inseparable from the world meaning the bodies.

The origination of accidents and bodies and the impossibility of eternity upon them
17 — And everything that non-existence is possible for, upon it absolutely eternity is impossible.
His statement: 'And everything that non-existence is possible for': i.e., everything that non-existence is possible for, meaning annihilation.

And his statement: 'upon it absolutely eternity is impossible': i.e., upon that which non-existence is possible for, eternity is impossible decisively without hesitation. And the author alluded to a syllogism composed thus: The world from its Throne to its carpet is such that non-existence is possible for it, and everything for which non-existence is possible has eternity impossible for it. So this syllogism yields: that the world from its Throne to its carpet has eternity impossible for it, so its origination is established, and if its origination is established, then it must have an originator. That is what is sought, because the origin of the discussion is in the reflection leading to knowledge of God Almighty. So the author suppressed the minor premise because it is understood from the objection, and he mentioned the major premise in his statement: 'and everything that non-existence is possible for' etc.

The summary: that you first establish the origination of accidents by observing their change from non-existence to
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the proof of the world's temporality (حدوث العالم). It begins with a clarification of the previous objection (استدراك) concerning the verse and the concept of 'ingenious' (بديع). The commentator, al-Ajhuri, explains that the proof of non-existence (دليل العدم) is actually the very craftsmanship observed in the world. The author's statement 'you find in it a craftsmanship' means you know by looking at the world a perfect craftsmanship that indicates the possibility of its non-existence. The commentator then argues that the word 'but' (لكن) in the poem might be for emphasis rather than objection, citing a Quranic verse as analogy. 

Next, the passage presents the central proof: the world (meaning celestial bodies) is subject to non-existence because it is accompanied by accidents (عوارض) that change, proving it is originated. The commentator quotes the poem's line: 'And everything that non-existence is possible for, upon it absolutely eternity is impossible' (وَكُلُّ مَا جَازَ عَلَيْهِ العَدَمُ عَلَيْهِ قَطعاً يَسْتَحِيلُ القِدَمُ). He explains that this is the major premise of a syllogism. The minor premise—that the world (from Throne to carpet) is subject to non-existence—is implied from the earlier discussion. The conclusion is that the world cannot be eternal, so it is originated (حادث), and therefore requires an originator (محدث), which is God. This is the goal of the discussion. The commentator notes that the author suppressed the minor premise because it is understood from the objection. The passage then begins to outline a method (الحاصل) to first prove the temporality of accidents by observing change, but the text cuts off before completing.

صغرى
كبرى

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'possibility of non-existence' means the world actually will cease to exist or is impermanent in a temporal sense.
Correction: The term 'جواز العدم' here refers to logical possibility, not actual annihilation. The argument is that if a thing's existence is not necessary, it cannot be eternal; it must have come into being.

Checkpoint

D) The world is perfectly made.

Key Takeaway
The world's possibility of non-existence proves it is not eternal, hence created, and therefore requires a Creator.

?

The Logical Foundation: Everything Admitting Non-Existence Cannot Be Eternal
Commentator states the major premise and explains its role in the syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major premise of the kalām argument for the world's temporality: that everything which can cease to exist cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
Admitting non-existence is incompatible with being eternal.

This lesson introduces the core logical principle used to prove the world's temporality. The commentator explains that any entity that can possibly not exist cannot have existed from eternity. This principle, stated as a universal major premise, forms the backbone of the entire argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the formulation of the major premise.
Pay attention to the phrase 'wa kullu mā jāza alayhi al-Adam'.
2
Identify the major premise
Locate the statement that everything possible of non-existence is impossible of pre-existence. Understand its meaning: 'al-Adam' refers to annihilation.
3
Understand the syllogism
See how the author constructs a syllogism with this premise. Note that the minor premise (the world is possible of non-existence) is omitted but understood.
The conclusion is that the world is temporal and requires a Maker.

[Section on] The temporality (ḥudūth) of accidents (a'rāḍ) and bodies (ajrām) and the impossibility of pre-existence (qidam) upon them. 17 - And everything upon which non-existence (al-'adam) is possible, upon it pre-existence (al-qidam) is absolutely impossible. His statement: 'And everything upon which non-existence is possible' i.e., everything that, or every thing upon which non-existence is possible; meaning: annihilation (al-fanā'). And his statement: 'upon it pre-existence is absolutely impossible' i.e., upon that which non-existence is possible, pre-existence is prevented decisively without hesitation. The author indicated its syllogistic construction: thus: the world from its Throne to its carpet [i.e., entire world] is possible of non-existence, and everything possible of non-existence is impossible of pre-existence; so this syllogism yields: that the world from its Throne to its carpet is impossible of pre-existence, so its temporality is established, and when its temporality is established, it must have a Maker (muḥdith), which is what is sought. Because the origin of discourse is in rational investigation (al-naẓar) leading to knowledge of Allah the Exalted. So the author omitted the minor premise because it is understood from the correction (al-istidrāk), and mentioned the major premise by his statement: 'And everything possible of non-existence...' etc. The conclusion: that you first establish the temporality of accidents by observing their change from non-existence to existence and vice versa. So you say: accidents are observed to change from non-existence to existence and vice versa, and everything that is so is temporal (ḥādith); it yields that accidents are temporal. Then you establish the temporality of bodies and the impossibility of pre-existence upon them by their inseparability from temporal accidents. So you say: bodies are inseparable from temporal accidents, and everything that is so is temporal and pre-existence is impossible upon it; it yields that bodies are temporal and pre-existence is impossible upon them.

Commentary
This passage presents a key proof in kalām for the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-'ālam). It begins with a heading indicating the topic: the temporality of accidents and bodies and the impossibility of pre-existence. The major premise is stated: 'Everything upon which non-existence (al-'adam) is possible, pre-existence (al-qidam) is absolutely impossible.' The commentator explains that 'non-existence' means annihilation (fanā'), and 'pre-existence' is decisively prevented from such things. The author (of the poem) constructed a syllogism: the world is possible of non-existence, and everything possible of non-existence cannot be eternal; therefore the world is not eternal. This yields its temporality, and thus it requires a Maker (muḥdith). The author omitted the minor premise (that the world is possible of non-existence) because it is understood from the context (the word 'lākin' indicating correction). Then the commentator provides the complete method (al-ḥāṣil): first prove accidents are temporal by observing their change, then prove bodies are temporal because they are inseparable from temporal accidents. This two-step argument establishes that bodies (ajrām) are temporal and that pre-existence is impossible for them.

Proving the World's Temporality: From Accidents to Bodies
Commentator details the two-step proof: accidents temporal by change, bodies temporal by inseparability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-step method for proving that bodies (ajrām) are temporal: first establish the temporality of accidents through observation of change, then establish bodies' temporality through their inseparability from temporal accidents.

Core Idea
Bodies are proven temporal because they are inseparable from changing accidents.

The commentator now provides the detailed method for proving the world's temporality. He explains that one must first prove accidents are temporal by observing their change from non-existence to existence and vice versa. Then, since bodies are always accompanied by accidents, and accidents are temporal, bodies must also be temporal. This two-step argument forms the complete proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the method
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the two-step proof.
Notice the structure: first accidents, then bodies.
2
Identify the two sub-proofs
Find the argument for accidents (observation of change) and the argument for bodies (inseparability from temporal accidents).
3
Understand the logical connection
See why the temporality of bodies depends on the temporality of accidents. Bodies cannot exist without accidents, so if accidents are temporal, bodies must be too.

The Proof of Contingency from Accidents
The author presents a two-step syllogistic proof that bodies are contingent based on their inseparability from changing accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-step syllogistic proof that establishes the contingency of bodies based on their inseparability from changing accidents.

Core Idea
Bodies are contingent because they are inseparable from contingent accidents.

This lesson presents a classic kalam proof (dalil al-a'rad). The author first shows that accidents are contingent because they change, then argues that bodies, being inseparable from such accidents, must also be contingent. This proof lays the groundwork for demonstrating that the universe is created and requires a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage carefully. Notice how it is structured as two connected syllogisms.
The first syllogism proves accidents are contingent. The second proves bodies are contingent.
2
Understand the first syllogism
Identify the premise that accidents are observed to change, and the rule that whatever changes is contingent. This yields the conclusion that accidents are contingent.
The term 'change' here means coming into existence after non-existence.
3
Understand the second syllogism
Note the premise that bodies are inseparable from contingent accidents. Apply the rule that whatever is inseparable from the contingent is itself contingent. Conclude that bodies are contingent and not eternal.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this proof serves as a foundation for later proofs of God's existence. It only establishes contingency, not the need for a creator directly.
The next step in kalam is to argue that every contingent needs a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأعراض شُوهد تغيَّرُها من عدم إلى وجود وعكسه، وكلُّ ما كذلك فهو حادث؛ ينتج أن الأعراض حادثة، ثم تثبتُ حدوث الأجرام واستحالة القدم عليها بملازمتها للأعراض الحادثة؛ فتقول: الأجرام ملازمة للأعراض الحادثة، وكلُّ ما كان كذلك فهو حادث ويستحيل عليه القدم، فينتج أن الأجرام حادثة، ويستحيل عليها القدم.
Accidents are observed to change from non-existence to existence and vice versa, and everything that is like that is contingent (hadith); it follows that accidents are contingent. Then the contingency of bodies and the impossibility of pre-eternity (qidam) for them is established by their inseparability from contingent accidents; so you say: Bodies are inseparable from contingent accidents, and everything that is like that is contingent and pre-eternity is impossible for it; it follows that bodies are contingent, and pre-eternity is impossible for them.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents a two-step syllogistic proof (qiyas) for the contingency (huduth) of bodies (ajram). First, it establishes that accidents (a'rad) are contingent because they are observed to change from existence to non-existence—a sign of need for a cause. Second, it argues that bodies are inseparable from such accidents; whatever is inseparable from the contingent is itself contingent. Therefore bodies cannot be eternal (qadim). The proof follows the standard kalam method of 'proof from accidents' (dalil al-a'rad).

Key Terms
الأعراض (a'rad)
الأجرام (ajram)
حادث (hadith)
قديم (qadim)
ملازمة (mulazama)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof directly proves the existence of God.
Correction: This proof only establishes that bodies are contingent (not eternal). The next step in the kalam argument is to argue that every contingent needs a cause, leading to the existence of a Creator.

Checkpoint
What is the major premise in the second syllogism of this proof?
A) Accidents are observed to change.
B) Bodies are inseparable from contingent accidents.
C) Everything contingent needs a creator.
D) Bodies are observed to be composite.

Key Takeaway
This passage teaches a foundational kalam proof: because bodies are inseparable from changing accidents, they must themselves be contingent and cannot be eternal.

The First Demand and the Four Syllogisms
The author refutes the denial of accidents and presents four syllogisms proving the world's contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first philosophical objection (denial of accidents) is refuted through a chain of four syllogisms that prove the world's contingency and its need for a Creator.

Core Idea
The existence of accidents is the foundation for proving the world's contingency.

The philosophers' first demand denies the existence of accidents altogether. The author counters by laying out a four-step syllogistic chain that starts with observable changes in accidents and culminates in the necessity of a Creator. Let's unpack this logical progression.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once, focusing on the poetic word 'Zayd' and the subsequent explanation. Notice that 'Zayd' represents the first objection.
The passage includes a detailed explanation of four syllogisms.
2
Identify the objection
The objection is that accidents do not exist as separate entities (zā'id) beyond bodies. The author refutes this by building a case for their existence through observation.
3
Follow the four syllogisms
Trace each syllogism: from accidents being contingent, to the world being possible of non-existence, to its pre-eternity being impossible, to its need for a Creator.
Each syllogism uses the conclusion of the previous one as a premise.
4
Understand the cumulative proof
Notice how the four syllogisms form a single argument that moves from observable reality to the necessity of a Creator.
This shows the logical rigor of kalām.
