بيان المطالب السبعة ] واعلم أن لهم هنا مطالب سبعةً، نظمها بعضهم في قوله: [من الرجز] زَيدٌ مَ قامَ مَا انْتَقَلْ ما كَمَنا ما انْفَكَ لا عُدْمَ قَدِيمٌ لا حَنا (١) .۱ فقوله : (زيد) ردُّ لقول الفلاسفة: (لا) نسلّمُ ثبوت زائد على الأجرام حتى يصح الأعراض على أن العالم بمعنى الأجرام يجوز عليه العدم، ثم بجواز عدمه على حدوثه، ثم بحدوثه على أنه يحتاج إلى مُحدث، فيحتاج حينئذ إلى أربعة أقيسة، بيانها : أن يقال : الأعراض شُوهد تغيرها من وجود إلى عدم وعكسه، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو حادث، ينتج أن الأعراض حادثة. ثم يقال : العالم بمعنى الأجرام ملازم للأعراض الحادثة، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو جائز العدم، ينتج العالم يجوز عليه العدم تؤخذ هذه النتيجة وتجعل صغرى . قياس ثالث تقريره هكذا ؛ العالم يجوز عليه العدم وكل ما كان كذلك استحال قدمه، وثبت حدوثه، ينتج أن العالم استحال قدمه وثبت حدوثه، تؤخذ هذه النتيجة وتجعل صغرى. قیاس رابع نظمه هكذا ؛ العالم استحال قدمه وثبت حدوثه، وكل ما كان كذلك فلا بد له من محدث، ينتج أن العالم لا بد له من محدث، وهذا هو المقصود بالنظر، وإنما قلنا ذلك؛ لأن المصنف جعل الأعراض دالة على جواز العدم لا على الحدوث، حيث قال: «لكن به قام دليل العدم. انتهى. وقوله : (أيضاً والحاصل ... إلى آخره) تلخص من كلامه هنا أن الأعراض باعتبار حدوثها دالة على حدوث الأجرام الدال على أنها - أي الأجرام - لا بدَّ لها من محدث، وذكر عند قوله : تجد به صنعاً أن الأعراض تدل على كمال الصانع وعموم علمه وإرادته، من جهة إتقانها إتقاناً ؛ فللأعراض :جهتان: جهة حدوثها، وجهة إتقانها، ولهذا تعددت دلالتها . انتهى أجهوري. بديعاً؛ (۱) انظر (حاشية الأمير على عبد السلام (ص) ،(٤٨) ، وقد نظمها العلامة الأمير بقوله : سبق الإله كذا العدم تدريجه إمكانه مع موجب أثر طرا الاستدلال به على حدوث الأجرام)، ودليل ثبوت الزائد الذي هو العرضُ: المشاهدة. .۲ وقوله (مَ قام بحذف ألف ( ما ) للوزن ردُّ لقولهم : (لا نسلم عدم العرض؛ لجواز أنه يقوم بنفسِهِ إذا لم يتّصف به الجرم، ودليل أنه لا يقوم بنفسه : أنه لا يعقل صفةٌ من غير موصوف، فلا يعقل حركة من غير متحرك مثلاً . ٣. وقوله: (ما انتقل) بسكون اللام للوزن ردُّ لقولهم : (لا نسلم عدم العرض؛ لجواز أنه ينتقل من جرم إلى جرم آخر ودليل أنه لا ينتقل : أنه لو انتقل لكان بعد مفارقة الأول وقبل وصول الثاني قائماً بنفسه، وقد بطل قبل ذلك . .. وقوله : (ما) كمنا رةٌ لقولهم: (لا) نسلّمُ عدم العرض ؛ لجواز أنه كمن في الجرم، فتكمنُ الحركة في الجرم إذا سكن (مثلاً)، ودليل أنه لا يكمنُ : أنه يلزم عليه جمعُ الصِّدِّينِ، وهو باطل. ه. وقوله : (ما انفك) ردُّ لقولهم : (لا نسلّمُ ملازمة الجرم للعرض؛ لجواز أن ينفك عنه، ودليلُ أنه لا ينفكُ: أنه لا يُعقلُ جرمٌ خالٍ عن حركة ولا حركة مثلاً؛ لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين . ٦. وقوله : (لا عدم قديم) رد لقولهم : لا نسلّمُ حدوث العرض؛ لجواز أن يكون قديماً وينعدم ودليل أن القديم لا ينعدم أن القديمَ لا يكون وجوده إلا واجباً، فلا يقبل العدم. .٧ وقوله (لاحنا) منتحت من قولنا : (لا حوادث لا أوّل لها، وهو ردُّ لقولهم: (لا) نسلّم أن ملازم الحادث حادث؛ لجواز أن تكون الأعراض حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها، فيكون ملازمُها قديماً)، ودليل أنه لا حوادث لا أوّلَ لها : أنها حيث كانت حوادث لزم أن يكونَ لها أول فيلزم على قولهم : حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها التناقض، وممَّا يبطله برهان القطع والتطبيق، وهو مبسوط في غير هذا المحل. وهذه المطالب السبعةُ لا يعرفُها إلا الراسخون في العلم (۱)، قال السنوسي : (وبها
Explanation of the seven demands. Know that they have here seven demands, which someone versified in his saying [in rajaz]: 'Zayd, ma qama, ma intaqal, ma kamana, ma infakka, la 'adamu qadimun, la hana.' So his saying 'Zayd' is a rebuttal of the philosophers' statement: 'We do not accept the existence of an extra (zā'id) upon bodies so that accidents are valid'—so that the world in the sense of bodies is possible of non-existence, then by the possibility of its non-existence upon its contingency, then by its contingency upon its need for a creator. So it needs four syllogisms: their explanation is that it is said: 'Accidents are observed to change from existence to non-existence and vice versa, and everything that is like that is contingent; it follows that accidents are contingent.' Then it is said: 'The world in the sense of bodies is inseparable from contingent accidents, and everything that is like that is possible of non-existence; it follows that the world is possible of non-existence.' This result is taken and made the minor premise. The third syllogism: 'The world is possible of non-existence, and everything that is like that its pre-eternity is impossible and its contingency is established; it follows that the world's pre-eternity is impossible and its contingency is established.' This result is taken and made the minor premise. The fourth syllogism: 'The world's pre-eternity is impossible and its contingency is established, and everything that is like that must have a creator; it follows that the world must have a creator.' This is the intended goal of inquiry. And we only said that because the author made accidents indicate the possibility of non-existence, not contingency, where he said: 'But by it the proof of non-existence is established.' End. And his saying: 'Also the conclusion... etc.' Summarizes from his speech here that accidents in terms of their contingency indicate the contingency of bodies, which indicates that they—i.e., bodies—must have a creator. And he mentioned at his saying: 'You find by it a skilled work' that accidents indicate the perfection of the Maker and the universality of His knowledge and will, in terms of their precision. So for accidents there are two aspects: the aspect of their contingency and the aspect of their precision, and thus their indication is multiple. End of Ajhuri. Marvelous. (1) See (Hashiyat al-Amir on Abd al-Salam, p. 48), and the scholar al-Amir versified it: 'Precedence of the divine, likewise non-existence, its gradation, possibility, with a cause, effect occurred.' The inference from it for the contingency of bodies, and the proof of the extra which is accident: observation. 2. And his saying 'ma qama' (with the alif of 'ma' dropped for meter) is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the non-existence of accident due to the possibility that it subsists by itself when the body is not characterized by it.' And the proof that it does not subsist by itself is that a quality without a qualified is not conceivable, so motion without a moving thing, for example, is not conceivable. 3. And his saying 'ma intaqal' (with sukun on the lam for meter) is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the non-existence of accident due to the possibility that it transfers from one body to another body.' And the proof that it does not transfer is that if it transferred, it would, after leaving the first and before reaching the second, be subsistent by itself, and that has already been disproven. 4. And his saying 'ma kamana' is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the non-existence of accident due to the possibility that it lies latent in the body, so motion lies latent in the body when it becomes still, for example.' And the proof that it does not lie latent is that it would necessitate the conjunction of two contradictory propositions, which is false. 5. And his saying 'ma infakka' is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the inseparability of the body from accident due to the possibility that it detaches itself from it.' And the proof that it does not detach is that a body devoid of motion and rest, for example, is not conceivable, because affirming both contradictories is impossible. 6. And his saying 'la 'adamu qadim' is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the contingency of accident due to the possibility that it is eternal and then ceases.' And the proof that the eternal does not cease is that the eternal's existence is only necessary (wajib), so it does not accept non-existence. 7. And his saying 'la hana' is derived from our saying 'no accidents without beginning,' and it is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept that the inseparable concomitant of the contingent is contingent, due to the possibility that accidents are events without beginning, so their concomitant would be eternal.' And the proof that there are no accidents without beginning is that since they are events, they must have a beginning, so their claim 'events without beginning' leads to contradiction. Among the things that refute it is the proof of intersection and application (burhan al-qat' wa al-tatbiq), which is expounded elsewhere. These seven demands are known only to those firmly rooted in knowledge (al-rasikhun fi al-'ilm). Sanusi said: 'With them...'
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and refutation of seven philosophical objections (talbāt) to the proof of contingency from accidents. The author first presents the objections in a poetic line: 'Zayd, ma qama, ma intaqal, ma kamana, ma infakka, la 'adamu qadimun, la hana.' Each word corresponds to an objection the philosophers raise to block the proof. The author then explains each word's meaning and the rebuttal. (1) 'Zayd' refutes the denial that there is anything beyond bodies (i.e., accidents) by restating the full syllogistic chain from contingency to the need for a creator. (2) 'Ma qama' refutes the claim that accidents could subsist by themselves—impossible because a quality requires a subject. (3) 'Ma intaqal' refutes the claim that accidents transfer between bodies—impossible because they would have to be independent during transfer. (4) 'Ma kamana' refutes the claim that accidents lie latent in bodies—impossible because it would entail contradictory states simultaneously. (5) 'Ma infakka' refutes the claim that a body could be free from all accidents—impossible because it would involve the conjunction of two opposites (existence and non-existence of accidents). (6) 'La 'adamu qadim' refutes the claim that an accident could be eternal and then cease—impossible because the eternal is necessary in existence and cannot become non-existent. (7) 'La hana' refutes the claim that there could be an infinite regress of accidents—impossible because an endless series of events would have no beginning, contradicting their nature as events. The author concludes that only the deeply learned grasp these subtle points.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof from accidents relies only on the observation of change.
Correction: The author shows it is a syllogistic chain that builds from observation to the necessity of a Creator.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the fourth syllogism?
A) Accidents are contingent.
B) The world is possible of non-existence.
C) The world must have a Creator.
D) The world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The first demand is refuted by a chain of four syllogisms that prove the world's contingency and its need for a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that the very contingency of accidents—things that change—points to a Creator. Look at the accidental things in your life: your health, your wealth, your relationships. Do you actively see them as signs of the Creator's existence and care? What would change if you truly internalized that?
?
The four syllogisms build a chain that leaves no room for eternal matter. Is there any aspect of your life where you act as if something is self-sufficient or eternal apart from Allah? How can you break that assumption today?
?
The author notes that accidents also indicate the perfection of the Maker through their precision. When was the last time you reflected on the precise design of something in creation and felt awe towards its Maker? Commit to doing that this week for at least one minute.

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
The Second and Third Demands: Accidents Cannot Subsist by Themselves or Transfer
The author refutes two objections: that accidents subsist by themselves or transfer between bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second and third philosophical objections—that accidents might subsist by themselves or transfer between bodies—are logically impossible.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot exist independently; they always require a body.

The philosophers argued that accidents might not need a body at all, either by subsisting by themselves or by moving from one body to another. The author counters by pointing out that such ideas are conceptually absurd. Let's examine why.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passages
Read the two short passages corresponding to 'ma qama' and 'ma intaqal'. Notice the poetic words and their explanations.
These are the second and third demands.
2
Identify the objections
The objections deny that accidents cease when a body changes. They claim accidents could self-subsist or transfer. The author refutes each.
3
Understand the rebuttals
For self-subsistence: a quality without a qualified is inconceivable. For transfer: if an accident transferred, it would have to exist independently during the move, which is impossible.
Both rebuttals rely on the necessity of a subject for accidents.
4
Connect to the overall proof
These rebuttals preserve the inseparability of accidents from bodies, which is essential for the second syllogism in the earlier proof.

Explanation of the seven demands. Know that they have here seven demands, which someone versified in his saying [in rajaz]: 'Zayd, ma qama, ma intaqal, ma kamana, ma infakka, la 'adamu qadimun, la hana.' So his saying 'Zayd' is a rebuttal of the philosophers' statement: 'We do not accept the existence of an extra (zā'id) upon bodies so that accidents are valid'—so that the world in the sense of bodies is possible of non-existence, then by the possibility of its non-existence upon its contingency, then by its contingency upon its need for a creator. So it needs four syllogisms: their explanation is that it is said: 'Accidents are observed to change from existence to non-existence and vice versa, and everything that is like that is contingent; it follows that accidents are contingent.' Then it is said: 'The world in the sense of bodies is inseparable from contingent accidents, and everything that is like that is possible of non-existence; it follows that the world is possible of non-existence.' This result is taken and made the minor premise. The third syllogism: 'The world is possible of non-existence, and everything that is like that its pre-eternity is impossible and its contingency is established; it follows that the world's pre-eternity is impossible and its contingency is established.' This result is taken and made the minor premise. The fourth syllogism: 'The world's pre-eternity is impossible and its contingency is established, and everything that is like that must have a creator; it follows that the world must have a creator.' This is the intended goal of inquiry. And we only said that because the author made accidents indicate the possibility of non-existence, not contingency, where he said: 'But by it the proof of non-existence is established.' End. And his saying: 'Also the conclusion... etc.' Summarizes from his speech here that accidents in terms of their contingency indicate the contingency of bodies, which indicates that they—i.e., bodies—must have a creator. And he mentioned at his saying: 'You find by it a skilled work' that accidents indicate the perfection of the Maker and the universality of His knowledge and will, in terms of their precision. So for accidents there are two aspects: the aspect of their contingency and the aspect of their precision, and thus their indication is multiple. End of Ajhuri. Marvelous. (1) See (Hashiyat al-Amir on Abd al-Salam, p. 48), and the scholar al-Amir versified it: 'Precedence of the divine, likewise non-existence, its gradation, possibility, with a cause, effect occurred.' The inference from it for the contingency of bodies, and the proof of the extra which is accident: observation. 2. And his saying 'ma qama' (with the alif of 'ma' dropped for meter) is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the non-existence of accident due to the possibility that it subsists by itself when the body is not characterized by it.' And the proof that it does not subsist by itself is that a quality without a qualified is not conceivable, so motion without a moving thing, for example, is not conceivable. 3. And his saying 'ma intaqal' (with sukun on the lam for meter) is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the non-existence of accident due to the possibility that it transfers from one body to another body.' And the proof that it does not transfer is that if it transferred, it would, after leaving the first and before reaching the second, be subsistent by itself, and that has already been disproven. 4. And his saying 'ma kamana' is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the non-existence of accident due to the possibility that it lies latent in the body, so motion lies latent in the body when it becomes still, for example.' And the proof that it does not lie latent is that it would necessitate the conjunction of two contradictory propositions, which is false. 5. And his saying 'ma infakka' is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the inseparability of the body from accident due to the possibility that it detaches itself from it.' And the proof that it does not detach is that a body devoid of motion and rest, for example, is not conceivable, because affirming both contradictories is impossible. 6. And his saying 'la 'adamu qadim' is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the contingency of accident due to the possibility that it is eternal and then ceases.' And the proof that the eternal does not cease is that the eternal's existence is only necessary (wajib), so it does not accept non-existence. 7. And his saying 'la hana' is derived from our saying 'no accidents without beginning,' and it is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept that the inseparable concomitant of the contingent is contingent, due to the possibility that accidents are events without beginning, so their concomitant would be eternal.' And the proof that there are no accidents without beginning is that since they are events, they must have a beginning, so their claim 'events without beginning' leads to contradiction. Among the things that refute it is the proof of intersection and application (burhan al-qat' wa al-tatbiq), which is expounded elsewhere. These seven demands are known only to those firmly rooted in knowledge (al-rasikhun fi al-'ilm). Sanusi said: 'With them...'
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and refutation of seven philosophical objections (talbāt) to the proof of contingency from accidents. The author first presents the objections in a poetic line: 'Zayd, ma qama, ma intaqal, ma kamana, ma infakka, la 'adamu qadimun, la hana.' Each word corresponds to an objection the philosophers raise to block the proof. The author then explains each word's meaning and the rebuttal. (1) 'Zayd' refutes the denial that there is anything beyond bodies (i.e., accidents) by restating the full syllogistic chain from contingency to the need for a creator. (2) 'Ma qama' refutes the claim that accidents could subsist by themselves—impossible because a quality requires a subject. (3) 'Ma intaqal' refutes the claim that accidents transfer between bodies—impossible because they would have to be independent during transfer. (4) 'Ma kamana' refutes the claim that accidents lie latent in bodies—impossible because it would entail contradictory states simultaneously. (5) 'Ma infakka' refutes the claim that a body could be free from all accidents—impossible because it would involve the conjunction of two opposites (existence and non-existence of accidents). (6) 'La 'adamu qadim' refutes the claim that an accident could be eternal and then cease—impossible because the eternal is necessary in existence and cannot become non-existent. (7) 'La hana' refutes the claim that there could be an infinite regress of accidents—impossible because an endless series of events would have no beginning, contradicting their nature as events. The author concludes that only the deeply learned grasp these subtle points.

ما انتقل
الأعراض
الموصوف

Accidents cannot subsist by themselves or transfer; they require a body, which is essential for the proof of the world's contingency.

?

?

?

The Fourth and Fifth Demands: Accidents Cannot Lie Latent or Be Detached
The author refutes two objections: that accidents lie latent in bodies or that bodies can be free of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth and fifth philosophical objections—that accidents might lie latent in a body or that a body could be free from all accidents—are logically impossible.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot be hidden or absent from bodies; they are always present.

The philosophers continued to object: what if accidents become latent and then reappear, or what if a body could exist without any accidents? The author shows that both ideas lead to contradictions. Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passages
Read the two passages for 'ma kamana' and 'ma infakka'. Notice the poetic words and their rebuttals.
2
Understand the objections
The fourth demand claims accidents can lie latent (like motion becoming stillness). The fifth claims a body can be without accidents at all.
3
Analyze the rebuttals
For latency: it would require opposite states at once, a contradiction. For detachment: a body cannot be devoid of all accidents because that would mean both existence and non-existence of accidents are false, which is impossible.
Both rely on the impossibility of contradictions.
4
See the importance
These rebuttals establish that accidents are constantly and inseparably present, which is needed for the proof that bodies are always associated with accidents.

Source Text

بيان المطالب السبعة ] واعلم أن لهم هنا مطالب سبعةً، نظمها بعضهم في قوله: [من الرجز] زَيدٌ مَ قامَ مَا انْتَقَلْ ما كَمَنا ما انْفَكَ لا عُدْمَ قَدِيمٌ لا حَنا (١) .۱ فقوله : (زيد) ردُّ لقول الفلاسفة: (لا) نسلّمُ ثبوت زائد على الأجرام حتى يصح الأعراض على أن العالم بمعنى الأجرام يجوز عليه العدم، ثم بجواز عدمه على حدوثه، ثم بحدوثه على أنه يحتاج إلى مُحدث، فيحتاج حينئذ إلى أربعة أقيسة، بيانها : أن يقال : الأعراض شُوهد تغيرها من وجود إلى عدم وعكسه، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو حادث، ينتج أن الأعراض حادثة. ثم يقال : العالم بمعنى الأجرام ملازم للأعراض الحادثة، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو جائز العدم، ينتج العالم يجوز عليه العدم تؤخذ هذه النتيجة وتجعل صغرى . قياس ثالث تقريره هكذا ؛ العالم يجوز عليه العدم وكل ما كان كذلك استحال قدمه، وثبت حدوثه، ينتج أن العالم استحال قدمه وثبت حدوثه، تؤخذ هذه النتيجة وتجعل صغرى. قیاس رابع نظمه هكذا ؛ العالم استحال قدمه وثبت حدوثه، وكل ما كان كذلك فلا بد له من محدث، ينتج أن العالم لا بد له من محدث، وهذا هو المقصود بالنظر، وإنما قلنا ذلك؛ لأن المصنف جعل الأعراض دالة على جواز العدم لا على الحدوث، حيث قال: «لكن به قام دليل العدم. انتهى. وقوله : (أيضاً والحاصل ... إلى آخره) تلخص من كلامه هنا أن الأعراض باعتبار حدوثها دالة على حدوث الأجرام الدال على أنها - أي الأجرام - لا بدَّ لها من محدث، وذكر عند قوله : تجد به صنعاً أن الأعراض تدل على كمال الصانع وعموم علمه وإرادته، من جهة إتقانها إتقاناً ؛ فللأعراض :جهتان: جهة حدوثها، وجهة إتقانها، ولهذا تعددت دلالتها . انتهى أجهوري. بديعاً؛ (۱) انظر (حاشية الأمير على عبد السلام (ص) ،(٤٨) ، وقد نظمها العلامة الأمير بقوله : سبق الإله كذا العدم تدريجه إمكانه مع موجب أثر طرا الاستدلال به على حدوث الأجرام)، ودليل ثبوت الزائد الذي هو العرضُ: المشاهدة. .۲ وقوله (مَ قام بحذف ألف ( ما ) للوزن ردُّ لقولهم : (لا نسلم عدم العرض؛ لجواز أنه يقوم بنفسِهِ إذا لم يتّصف به الجرم، ودليل أنه لا يقوم بنفسه : أنه لا يعقل صفةٌ من غير موصوف، فلا يعقل حركة من غير متحرك مثلاً . ٣. وقوله: (ما انتقل) بسكون اللام للوزن ردُّ لقولهم : (لا نسلم عدم العرض؛ لجواز أنه ينتقل من جرم إلى جرم آخر ودليل أنه لا ينتقل : أنه لو انتقل لكان بعد مفارقة الأول وقبل وصول الثاني قائماً بنفسه، وقد بطل قبل ذلك . .. وقوله : (ما) كمنا رةٌ لقولهم: (لا) نسلّمُ عدم العرض ؛ لجواز أنه كمن في الجرم، فتكمنُ الحركة في الجرم إذا سكن (مثلاً)، ودليل أنه لا يكمنُ : أنه يلزم عليه جمعُ الصِّدِّينِ، وهو باطل. ه. وقوله : (ما انفك) ردُّ لقولهم : (لا نسلّمُ ملازمة الجرم للعرض؛ لجواز أن ينفك عنه، ودليلُ أنه لا ينفكُ: أنه لا يُعقلُ جرمٌ خالٍ عن حركة ولا حركة مثلاً؛ لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين . ٦. وقوله : (لا عدم قديم) رد لقولهم : لا نسلّمُ حدوث العرض؛ لجواز أن يكون قديماً وينعدم ودليل أن القديم لا ينعدم أن القديمَ لا يكون وجوده إلا واجباً، فلا يقبل العدم. .٧ وقوله (لاحنا) منتحت من قولنا : (لا حوادث لا أوّل لها، وهو ردُّ لقولهم: (لا) نسلّم أن ملازم الحادث حادث؛ لجواز أن تكون الأعراض حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها، فيكون ملازمُها قديماً)، ودليل أنه لا حوادث لا أوّلَ لها : أنها حيث كانت حوادث لزم أن يكونَ لها أول فيلزم على قولهم : حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها التناقض، وممَّا يبطله برهان القطع والتطبيق، وهو مبسوط في غير هذا المحل. وهذه المطالب السبعةُ لا يعرفُها إلا الراسخون في العلم (۱)، قال السنوسي : (وبها
Explanation of the seven demands. Know that they have here seven demands, which someone versified in his saying [in rajaz]: 'Zayd, ma qama, ma intaqal, ma kamana, ma infakka, la 'adamu qadimun, la hana.' So his saying 'Zayd' is a rebuttal of the philosophers' statement: 'We do not accept the existence of an extra (zā'id) upon bodies so that accidents are valid'—so that the world in the sense of bodies is possible of non-existence, then by the possibility of its non-existence upon its contingency, then by its contingency upon its need for a creator. So it needs four syllogisms: their explanation is that it is said: 'Accidents are observed to change from existence to non-existence and vice versa, and everything that is like that is contingent; it follows that accidents are contingent.' Then it is said: 'The world in the sense of bodies is inseparable from contingent accidents, and everything that is like that is possible of non-existence; it follows that the world is possible of non-existence.' This result is taken and made the minor premise. The third syllogism: 'The world is possible of non-existence, and everything that is like that its pre-eternity is impossible and its contingency is established; it follows that the world's pre-eternity is impossible and its contingency is established.' This result is taken and made the minor premise. The fourth syllogism: 'The world's pre-eternity is impossible and its contingency is established, and everything that is like that must have a creator; it follows that the world must have a creator.' This is the intended goal of inquiry. And we only said that because the author made accidents indicate the possibility of non-existence, not contingency, where he said: 'But by it the proof of non-existence is established.' End. And his saying: 'Also the conclusion... etc.' Summarizes from his speech here that accidents in terms of their contingency indicate the contingency of bodies, which indicates that they—i.e., bodies—must have a creator. And he mentioned at his saying: 'You find by it a skilled work' that accidents indicate the perfection of the Maker and the universality of His knowledge and will, in terms of their precision. So for accidents there are two aspects: the aspect of their contingency and the aspect of their precision, and thus their indication is multiple. End of Ajhuri. Marvelous. (1) See (Hashiyat al-Amir on Abd al-Salam, p. 48), and the scholar al-Amir versified it: 'Precedence of the divine, likewise non-existence, its gradation, possibility, with a cause, effect occurred.' The inference from it for the contingency of bodies, and the proof of the extra which is accident: observation. 2. And his saying 'ma qama' (with the alif of 'ma' dropped for meter) is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the non-existence of accident due to the possibility that it subsists by itself when the body is not characterized by it.' And the proof that it does not subsist by itself is that a quality without a qualified is not conceivable, so motion without a moving thing, for example, is not conceivable. 3. And his saying 'ma intaqal' (with sukun on the lam for meter) is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the non-existence of accident due to the possibility that it transfers from one body to another body.' And the proof that it does not transfer is that if it transferred, it would, after leaving the first and before reaching the second, be subsistent by itself, and that has already been disproven. 4. And his saying 'ma kamana' is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the non-existence of accident due to the possibility that it lies latent in the body, so motion lies latent in the body when it becomes still, for example.' And the proof that it does not lie latent is that it would necessitate the conjunction of two contradictory propositions, which is false. 5. And his saying 'ma infakka' is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the inseparability of the body from accident due to the possibility that it detaches itself from it.' And the proof that it does not detach is that a body devoid of motion and rest, for example, is not conceivable, because affirming both contradictories is impossible. 6. And his saying 'la 'adamu qadim' is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the contingency of accident due to the possibility that it is eternal and then ceases.' And the proof that the eternal does not cease is that the eternal's existence is only necessary (wajib), so it does not accept non-existence. 7. And his saying 'la hana' is derived from our saying 'no accidents without beginning,' and it is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept that the inseparable concomitant of the contingent is contingent, due to the possibility that accidents are events without beginning, so their concomitant would be eternal.' And the proof that there are no accidents without beginning is that since they are events, they must have a beginning, so their claim 'events without beginning' leads to contradiction. Among the things that refute it is the proof of intersection and application (burhan al-qat' wa al-tatbiq), which is expounded elsewhere. These seven demands are known only to those firmly rooted in knowledge (al-rasikhun fi al-'ilm). Sanusi said: 'With them...'

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and refutation of seven philosophical objections (talbāt) to the proof of contingency from accidents. The author first presents the objections in a poetic line: 'Zayd, ma qama, ma intaqal, ma kamana, ma infakka, la 'adamu qadimun, la hana.' Each word corresponds to an objection the philosophers raise to block the proof. The author then explains each word's meaning and the rebuttal. (1) 'Zayd' refutes the denial that there is anything beyond bodies (i.e., accidents) by restating the full syllogistic chain from contingency to the need for a creator. (2) 'Ma qama' refutes the claim that accidents could subsist by themselves—impossible because a quality requires a subject. (3) 'Ma intaqal' refutes the claim that accidents transfer between bodies—impossible because they would have to be independent during transfer. (4) 'Ma kamana' refutes the claim that accidents lie latent in bodies—impossible because it would entail contradictory states simultaneously. (5) 'Ma infakka' refutes the claim that a body could be free from all accidents—impossible because it would involve the conjunction of two opposites (existence and non-existence of accidents). (6) 'La 'adamu qadim' refutes the claim that an accident could be eternal and then cease—impossible because the eternal is necessary in existence and cannot become non-existent. (7) 'La hana' refutes the claim that there could be an infinite regress of accidents—impossible because an endless series of events would have no beginning, contradicting their nature as events. The author concludes that only the deeply learned grasp these subtle points.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Sixth and Seventh Demands: Eternality of Accidents and Infinite Regress
The author refutes two final objections: that accidents could be eternal and then cease, or that they could form an infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the last two philosophical objections—that an accident could be eternal and then cease, or that there could be an infinite series of accidents—are refuted.

Core Idea
Eternal things cannot cease, and an infinite regress of events is impossible.

The philosophers' final objections strike at the very notion of contingency: what if an accident is eternal but later ceases? Or what if there is an endless chain of accidents? The author uses logical principles to block these escape routes. Let's delve into the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passages
Read the passages for 'la 'adamu qadim' and 'la hana'. Note the poetic words and their detailed rebuttals.
2
Understand the sixth demand
The objection: an accident might be eternal (qadim) and then cease. The rebuttal: the eternal's existence is necessary (wajib), so it cannot cease.
This ties into the nature of eternity and necessity.
3
Understand the seventh demand
The objection: there might be an infinite series of accidents without a beginning. The rebuttal: that would involve events without a first event, which is contradictory and refuted by the proof of intersection and application.
4
Appreciate the closure
These final rebuttals complete the defense of the proof from accidents. The author notes that only the deeply learned grasp these demands.

Explanation of the seven demands. Know that they have here seven demands, which someone versified in his saying [in rajaz]: 'Zayd, ma qama, ma intaqal, ma kamana, ma infakka, la 'adamu qadimun, la hana.' So his saying 'Zayd' is a rebuttal of the philosophers' statement: 'We do not accept the existence of an extra (zā'id) upon bodies so that accidents are valid'—so that the world in the sense of bodies is possible of non-existence, then by the possibility of its non-existence upon its contingency, then by its contingency upon its need for a creator. So it needs four syllogisms: their explanation is that it is said: 'Accidents are observed to change from existence to non-existence and vice versa, and everything that is like that is contingent; it follows that accidents are contingent.' Then it is said: 'The world in the sense of bodies is inseparable from contingent accidents, and everything that is like that is possible of non-existence; it follows that the world is possible of non-existence.' This result is taken and made the minor premise. The third syllogism: 'The world is possible of non-existence, and everything that is like that its pre-eternity is impossible and its contingency is established; it follows that the world's pre-eternity is impossible and its contingency is established.' This result is taken and made the minor premise. The fourth syllogism: 'The world's pre-eternity is impossible and its contingency is established, and everything that is like that must have a creator; it follows that the world must have a creator.' This is the intended goal of inquiry. And we only said that because the author made accidents indicate the possibility of non-existence, not contingency, where he said: 'But by it the proof of non-existence is established.' End. And his saying: 'Also the conclusion... etc.' Summarizes from his speech here that accidents in terms of their contingency indicate the contingency of bodies, which indicates that they—i.e., bodies—must have a creator. And he mentioned at his saying: 'You find by it a skilled work' that accidents indicate the perfection of the Maker and the universality of His knowledge and will, in terms of their precision. So for accidents there are two aspects: the aspect of their contingency and the aspect of their precision, and thus their indication is multiple. End of Ajhuri. Marvelous. (1) See (Hashiyat al-Amir on Abd al-Salam, p. 48), and the scholar al-Amir versified it: 'Precedence of the divine, likewise non-existence, its gradation, possibility, with a cause, effect occurred.' The inference from it for the contingency of bodies, and the proof of the extra which is accident: observation. 2. And his saying 'ma qama' (with the alif of 'ma' dropped for meter) is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the non-existence of accident due to the possibility that it subsists by itself when the body is not characterized by it.' And the proof that it does not subsist by itself is that a quality without a qualified is not conceivable, so motion without a moving thing, for example, is not conceivable. 3. And his saying 'ma intaqal' (with sukun on the lam for meter) is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the non-existence of accident due to the possibility that it transfers from one body to another body.' And the proof that it does not transfer is that if it transferred, it would, after leaving the first and before reaching the second, be subsistent by itself, and that has already been disproven. 4. And his saying 'ma kamana' is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the non-existence of accident due to the possibility that it lies latent in the body, so motion lies latent in the body when it becomes still, for example.' And the proof that it does not lie latent is that it would necessitate the conjunction of two contradictory propositions, which is false. 5. And his saying 'ma infakka' is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the inseparability of the body from accident due to the possibility that it detaches itself from it.' And the proof that it does not detach is that a body devoid of motion and rest, for example, is not conceivable, because affirming both contradictories is impossible. 6. And his saying 'la 'adamu qadim' is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept the contingency of accident due to the possibility that it is eternal and then ceases.' And the proof that the eternal does not cease is that the eternal's existence is only necessary (wajib), so it does not accept non-existence. 7. And his saying 'la hana' is derived from our saying 'no accidents without beginning,' and it is a rebuttal of their statement: 'We do not accept that the inseparable concomitant of the contingent is contingent, due to the possibility that accidents are events without beginning, so their concomitant would be eternal.' And the proof that there are no accidents without beginning is that since they are events, they must have a beginning, so their claim 'events without beginning' leads to contradiction. Among the things that refute it is the proof of intersection and application (burhan al-qat' wa al-tatbiq), which is expounded elsewhere. These seven demands are known only to those firmly rooted in knowledge (al-rasikhun fi al-'ilm). Sanusi said: 'With them...'
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and refutation of seven philosophical objections (talbāt) to the proof of contingency from accidents. The author first presents the objections in a poetic line: 'Zayd, ma qama, ma intaqal, ma kamana, ma infakka, la 'adamu qadimun, la hana.' Each word corresponds to an objection the philosophers raise to block the proof. The author then explains each word's meaning and the rebuttal. (1) 'Zayd' refutes the denial that there is anything beyond bodies (i.e., accidents) by restating the full syllogistic chain from contingency to the need for a creator. (2) 'Ma qama' refutes the claim that accidents could subsist by themselves—impossible because a quality requires a subject. (3) 'Ma intaqal' refutes the claim that accidents transfer between bodies—impossible because they would have to be independent during transfer. (4) 'Ma kamana' refutes the claim that accidents lie latent in bodies—impossible because it would entail contradictory states simultaneously. (5) 'Ma infakka' refutes the claim that a body could be free from all accidents—impossible because it would involve the conjunction of two opposites (existence and non-existence of accidents). (6) 'La 'adamu qadim' refutes the claim that an accident could be eternal and then cease—impossible because the eternal is necessary in existence and cannot become non-existent. (7) 'La hana' refutes the claim that there could be an infinite regress of accidents—impossible because an endless series of events would have no beginning, contradicting their nature as events. The author concludes that only the deeply learned grasp these subtle points.

Key Terms
لا عدم قديم
لا حنا
واجب
برهان القطع والتطبيق
التناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think an eternal thing could cease if God wills it.
Correction: The author argues that the eternal's existence is necessary, so non-existence is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why can an eternal accident not cease to exist?
A) Because it is too strong.
B) Because the eternal has necessary existence.
C) Because it is a body.
D) Because it is not contingent.

Key Takeaway
The final two demands are refuted by the necessary existence of the eternal and the impossibility of an infinite regress of events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that the eternal has necessary existence and cannot cease. Is there any part of your life—a habit, a relationship, a worldview—that you treat as if it is eternal and unchangeable, when in reality it is contingent and must be renewed by Allah? What would it take to submit that thing to His will today?
?
The proof that events must have a beginning refutes the idea of an infinite regress. Does your own life feel like it has a meaningful beginning or are you drifting as if you are just one in an endless chain of causes? How does knowing that Allah is the First Cause change your sense of purpose this week?
?
If the eternal cannot cease, then Allah's mercy, justice, and sovereignty are permanent. Are you living as if you can exhaust His mercy by your sins, or as if His justice might fail? Which attribute of Allah have you doubted in practice, and what is one concrete step you will take to trust in its permanence?

Accidents Cannot Subsist by Themselves or Transfer
Refutation of objections that accidents can subsist by themselves or transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why accidents cannot exist independently of a body or transfer between bodies, and the logical principle that an attribute requires a subject.

Core Idea
An accident is an attribute; it cannot exist without a subject.

Observation establishes accidents as real entities. But skeptics raise two objections: perhaps accidents can subsist by themselves if the body lacks them, or they can transfer between bodies. This passage refutes both, grounding the proof in the conceptual dependence of attributes on subjects.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening proof
Start with the statement that observation proves the existence of accidents. Then read the first refutation about subsistence.
Notice the poetic phrasing 'ما قام'.
2
Analyze the first objection and refutation
Identify the objection: accidents could exist without a body. Then see the refutation: an attribute is inconceivable without an attributed thing.
Example: motion requires a moving thing.
3
Analyze the second objection and refutation
The next objection: accidents could transfer. The refutation: during transfer, the accident would need to subsist by itself, which is impossible.
This refutation builds on the first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلال به على حدوث الأجرام، ودليل ثبوت الزائد الذي هو العرض: المشاهدة. وقوله (ما قام) بحذف ألف (ما) للوزن رد لقولهم: لا نسلم عدم العرض؛ لجواز أنه يقوم بنفسه إذا لم يتصف به الجرم، ودليل أنه لا يقوم بنفسه: أنه لا يعقل صفة من غير موصوف، فلا يعقل حركة من غير متحرك مثلاً. وقوله (ما انتقل) بسكون اللام للوزن رد لقولهم: لا نسلم عدم العرض؛ لجواز أنه ينتقل من جرم إلى جرم آخر، ودليل أنه لا ينتقل: أنه لو انتقل لكان بعد مفارقة الأول وقبل وصول الثاني قائماً بنفسه، وقد بطل قبل ذلك. وقوله (ما يكمن) رد لقولهم: لا نسلم عدم العرض؛ لجواز أنه يكمن في الجرم، فتكمن الحركة في الجرم إذا سكن مثلاً، ودليل أنه لا يكمن: أنه يلزم عليه جمع الضدين، وهو باطل. وقوله (ما انفك) رد لقولهم: لا نسلم ملازمة الجرم للعرض؛ لجواز أن ينفك عنه، ودليل أنه لا ينفك: أنه لا يعقل جرم خال عن حركة ولا سكون مثلاً؛ لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين. وقوله (لا عدم قديم) رد لقولهم: لا نسلم حدوث العرض؛ لجواز أن يكون قديماً وينعدم، ودليل أن القديم لا ينعدم: أن القديم لا يكون وجوده إلا واجباً، فلا يقبل العدم. وقوله (لاحنا) منتحت من قولنا: لا حوادث لا أول لها، وهو رد لقولهم: لا نسلم أن ملازم الحادث حادث؛ لجواز أن تكون الأعراض حوادث لا أول لها، فيكون ملازمها قديماً، ودليل أنه لا حوادث لا أول لها: أنها حيث كانت حوادث لزم أن يكون لها أول، فيلزم على قولهم حوادث لا أول لها التناقض، ومما يبطله برهان القطع والتطبيق، وهو مبسوط في غير هذا المحل. وهذه المطالب السبعة لا يعرفها إلا الراسخون في العلم.
The proof by it of the temporal origination of bodies, and the proof of the existence of the additional [attribute] which is the accident: observation. And his statement 'ma qama' with the elision of the alif of 'ma' for meter is a refutation of their statement: 'We do not concede the non-existence of the accident; because it is possible that it subsists by itself if the body is not qualified by it.' And the proof that it does not subsist by itself: that an attribute is not intelligible without an attributed thing, so motion is not intelligible without a moving thing, for example. And his statement 'ma intaqala' with sukun of the lam for meter is a refutation of their statement: 'We do not concede the non-existence of the accident; because it is possible that it transfers from one body to another body.' And the proof that it does not transfer: that if it transferred, it would, after separating from the first and before reaching the second, be subsisting by itself, and it has been invalidated before that. And his statement 'ma yakmun' is a refutation of their statement: 'We do not concede the non-existence of the accident; because it is possible that it is latent in the body, so motion becomes latent in the body when it is at rest, for example.' And the proof that it does not become latent: that it would entail the combination of opposites, which is invalid. And his statement 'ma infakka' is a refutation of their statement: 'We do not concede the inseparable association of the body with the accident; because it is possible that it disassociates from it.' And the proof that it does not disassociate: that a body devoid of motion or rest, for example, is not intelligible; because of the impossibility of the removal of the two contradictories. And his statement 'la 'adamu qadim' is a refutation of their statement: 'We do not concede the temporal origination of the accident; because it is possible that it is eternal and becomes non-existent.' And the proof that the eternal does not become non-existent: that the eternal's existence is only necessary, so it does not admit non-existence. And his statement 'lahina' derived from our statement: 'There are no events with no beginning' is a refutation of their statement: 'We do not concede that that which is inseparable from the temporally originated is temporally originated; because it is possible that the accidents are events with no beginning, so their inseparable associate is eternal.' And the proof that there are no events with no beginning: that since they are events, they must have a beginning, so their statement 'events with no beginning' entails contradiction. And that which invalidates it is the proof of severing and matching, which is expounded elsewhere. And these seven points are not known except by those firmly rooted in knowledge.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents seven rebuttals (مطالب) against objections to the argument for the temporal origination of bodies (حدوث الأجرام). Each rebuttal begins with a phrase from the poem (indicated by 'وقوله') which is explained as a refutation of a specific objection. The first objection claims accidents could subsist by themselves; the refutation is that an attribute is inconceivable without a subject. The second claims accidents could transfer; the refutation is that they would need to subsist independently during transfer, which is impossible. The third claims accidents could be latent; the refutation is that it would require combining opposites (e.g., motion and rest simultaneously), which is absurd. The fourth claims accidents could disassociate from bodies; the refutation is that a body cannot be devoid of all accidents (e.g., motion or rest) because that would involve the removal of contradictories. The fifth claims accidents could be eternal and then perish; the refutation is that the eternal has necessary existence and cannot cease. The sixth and seventh are combined: they claim accidents could be beginningless events (حوادث لا أول لها); the refutation is that an event must have a beginning, so the phrase 'beginningless events' is a contradiction. The author notes that this is invalidated by the proof of 'severance and matching' (برهان القطع والتطبيق), which is detailed elsewhere. The passage concludes that these seven points are known only to those firmly rooted in knowledge.

Key Terms
عرض
جوهر
صفة
موصوف
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents can exist without a subject or can move between bodies.
Correction: An accident is an attribute that necessarily inheres in a body; it cannot subsist by itself nor transfer.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that an accident can transfer from one body to another?
A) By arguing that transfer would require the accident to subsist by itself during the transfer, which is impossible.
B) By stating that accidents are not real entities.
C) By claiming that transfer is only possible for substances.
D) By appealing to the consensus of theologians.

Key Takeaway
Accidents are dependent attributes; they cannot exist independently or transfer between bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that an attribute cannot exist without an attributed thing—a motion must have a moving thing. Now ask yourself: In your daily life, do you ever act as if your own attributes (like your anger, kindness, or patience) exist independently of you, as if they just happen without your choice? How does realizing they are accidents that depend entirely on you change your sense of responsibility for them? What will you do differently tomorrow morning when you feel your first emotion?
?
If accidents cannot transfer, then your past sins and virtues are permanently attached to you—you cannot transfer blame to circumstances or other people. Does this truth sit comfortably with you? When you remember a mistake you made years ago, do you still feel its weight? What would it mean to fully accept that your actions are your own, and then turn to the One who can forgive and replace them with good?

Accidents Cannot Be Latent or Disassociate from Bodies
Refutations of objections that accidents can be latent or disassociate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why accidents cannot be latent within a body (e.g., motion hiding during rest) nor disassociate from bodies entirely.

Core Idea
Accidents are constantly present in bodies; they cannot hide or separate.

Two further objections are addressed: that accidents might be latent within a body (like motion being hidden during rest) or that they might disassociate from bodies entirely. The refutations rely on logical impossibilities: combining opposites and the impossibility of a body devoid of all accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the third refutation (latency)
Examine the objection: accidents could be latent, e.g., motion hidden during rest. Then see the refutation: it would require combining opposites (motion and rest), which is impossible.
The phrase 'ما يكمن' refers to latency.
2
Read the fourth refutation (disassociation)
Objection: accidents could disassociate from bodies. Refutation: a body cannot be devoid of all accidents like motion or rest, as that would remove contradictories.
The phrase 'ما انفك' means 'did not disassociate'.
3
Understand the underlying principles
Both refutations use basic logical principles: no combination of opposites, and no negation of both contradictories.
These principles are foundational in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلال به على حدوث الأجرام، ودليل ثبوت الزائد الذي هو العرض: المشاهدة. وقوله (ما قام) بحذف ألف (ما) للوزن رد لقولهم: لا نسلم عدم العرض؛ لجواز أنه يقوم بنفسه إذا لم يتصف به الجرم، ودليل أنه لا يقوم بنفسه: أنه لا يعقل صفة من غير موصوف، فلا يعقل حركة من غير متحرك مثلاً. وقوله (ما انتقل) بسكون اللام للوزن رد لقولهم: لا نسلم عدم العرض؛ لجواز أنه ينتقل من جرم إلى جرم آخر، ودليل أنه لا ينتقل: أنه لو انتقل لكان بعد مفارقة الأول وقبل وصول الثاني قائماً بنفسه، وقد بطل قبل ذلك. وقوله (ما يكمن) رد لقولهم: لا نسلم عدم العرض؛ لجواز أنه يكمن في الجرم، فتكمن الحركة في الجرم إذا سكن مثلاً، ودليل أنه لا يكمن: أنه يلزم عليه جمع الضدين، وهو باطل. وقوله (ما انفك) رد لقولهم: لا نسلم ملازمة الجرم للعرض؛ لجواز أن ينفك عنه، ودليل أنه لا ينفك: أنه لا يعقل جرم خال عن حركة ولا سكون مثلاً؛ لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين. وقوله (لا عدم قديم) رد لقولهم: لا نسلم حدوث العرض؛ لجواز أن يكون قديماً وينعدم، ودليل أن القديم لا ينعدم: أن القديم لا يكون وجوده إلا واجباً، فلا يقبل العدم. وقوله (لاحنا) منتحت من قولنا: لا حوادث لا أول لها، وهو رد لقولهم: لا نسلم أن ملازم الحادث حادث؛ لجواز أن تكون الأعراض حوادث لا أول لها، فيكون ملازمها قديماً، ودليل أنه لا حوادث لا أول لها: أنها حيث كانت حوادث لزم أن يكون لها أول، فيلزم على قولهم حوادث لا أول لها التناقض، ومما يبطله برهان القطع والتطبيق، وهو مبسوط في غير هذا المحل. وهذه المطالب السبعة لا يعرفها إلا الراسخون في العلم.

And his statement 'la 'adamu qadim' is a refutation of their statement: 'We do not concede the temporal origination of the accident; because it is possible that it is eternal and becomes non-existent.' And the proof that the eternal does not become non-existent: that the eternal's existence is only necessary, so it does not admit non-existence. And his statement 'lahina' derived from our statement: 'There are no events with no beginning' is a refutation of their statement: 'We do not concede that that which is inseparable from the temporally originated is temporally originated; because it is possible that the accidents are events with no beginning, so their inseparable associate is eternal.' And the proof that there are no events with no beginning: that since they are events, they must have a beginning, so their statement 'events with no beginning' entails contradiction. And that which invalidates it is the proof of severing and matching, which is expounded elsewhere. And these seven points are not known except by those firmly rooted in knowledge.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents seven rebuttals (مطالب) against objections to the argument for the temporal origination of bodies (حدوث الأجرام). Each rebuttal begins with a phrase from the poem (indicated by 'وقوله') which is explained as a refutation of a specific objection. The first objection claims accidents could subsist by themselves; the refutation is that an attribute is inconceivable without a subject. The second claims accidents could transfer; the refutation is that they would need to subsist independently during transfer, which is impossible. The third claims accidents could be latent; the refutation is that it would require combining opposites (e.g., motion and rest simultaneously), which is absurd. The fourth claims accidents could disassociate from bodies; the refutation is that a body cannot be devoid of all accidents (e.g., motion or rest) because that would involve the removal of contradictories. The fifth claims accidents could be eternal and then perish; the refutation is that the eternal has necessary existence and cannot cease. The sixth and seventh are combined: they claim accidents could be beginningless events (حوادث لا أول لها); the refutation is that an event must have a beginning, so the phrase 'beginningless events' is a contradiction. The author notes that this is invalidated by the proof of 'severance and matching' (برهان القطع والتطبيق), which is detailed elsewhere. The passage concludes that these seven points are known only to those firmly rooted in knowledge.

Key Terms
عرض
كمون
انفكاك
ضدان
نقيضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents can hide in a body or leave it entirely.
Correction: Latency would require impossible combination of opposites; disassociation would leave a body without necessary accidents, which is inconceivable.

Checkpoint
Why can an accident not be latent in a body?
A) Because latency implies it existed before the body.
B) Because it would require combining two opposites, which is impossible.
C) Because accidents are not real.
D) Because the body would then have no accidents.

Key Takeaway
Accidents cannot be latent or disassociate; bodies always have some accidents, and opposites cannot coexist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own body is never free from accidents—every moment you are either moving or at rest, changing or static—does that not remind you that you are constantly dependent on the One who creates those accidents? When you feel 'in control,' are you aware that even your stillness is a created accident?
?
The proof says a body cannot be without some accident. Consider: what would it mean for your soul to be without a single attribute of faith or heedlessness? Are you ever spiritually 'neutral'? Or are you always in a state of either drawing near to Allah or drifting away?
?
If the impossibility of 'latency' shows that nothing can be hidden in creation without manifesting its effects, what does that imply about the state of your heart? Can a seed of hypocrisy or pride remain latent without showing in your actions? What would the proof of 'combining opposites' say about pretending to have both sincerity and show?

Eternal Accidents and Beginningless Events Are Impossible
Refutations of objections that accidents can be eternal or beginningless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why accidents cannot be eternal and why there cannot be beginningless events (حوادث لا أول لها).

Core Idea
The eternal cannot perish; events must have a beginning.

The final two refutations address objections concerning eternality and beginningless series. The first argues that an eternal accident cannot cease to exist because the eternal has necessary existence. The second argues that there cannot be a series of events without a beginning, as the very concept 'event' implies a start. This is supported by the proof of severance and matching.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth refutation (لا عدم قديم)
Objection: an accident could be eternal and then cease. Refutation: the eternal has necessary existence and cannot be annihilated.
The phrase 'لا عدم قديم' means 'no non-existence of the eternal'.
2
Read the sixth refutation (لاحنا)
Objection: accidents could be beginningless events. Refutation: events must have a beginning; 'beginningless event' is a contradiction.
The coined word 'لاحنا' combines 'لا حوادث لا أول لها'.
3
Note the mention of برهان القطع والتطبيق
The proof of severance and matching is referenced as a detailed invalidator of beginningless events.
This proof is elaborated elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلال به على حدوث الأجرام، ودليل ثبوت الزائد الذي هو العرض: المشاهدة. وقوله (ما قام) بحذف ألف (ما) للوزن رد لقولهم: لا نسلم عدم العرض؛ لجواز أنه يقوم بنفسه إذا لم يتصف به الجرم، ودليل أنه لا يقوم بنفسه: أنه لا يعقل صفة من غير موصوف، فلا يعقل حركة من غير متحرك مثلاً. وقوله (ما انتقل) بسكون اللام للوزن رد لقولهم: لا نسلم عدم العرض؛ لجواز أنه ينتقل من جرم إلى جرم آخر، ودليل أنه لا ينتقل: أنه لو انتقل لكان بعد مفارقة الأول وقبل وصول الثاني قائماً بنفسه، وقد بطل قبل ذلك. وقوله (ما يكمن) رد لقولهم: لا نسلم عدم العرض؛ لجواز أنه يكمن في الجرم، فتكمن الحركة في الجرم إذا سكن مثلاً، ودليل أنه لا يكمن: أنه يلزم عليه جمع الضدين، وهو باطل. وقوله (ما انفك) رد لقولهم: لا نسلم ملازمة الجرم للعرض؛ لجواز أن ينفك عنه، ودليل أنه لا ينفك: أنه لا يعقل جرم خال عن حركة ولا سكون مثلاً؛ لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين. وقوله (لا عدم قديم) رد لقولهم: لا نسلم حدوث العرض؛ لجواز أن يكون قديماً وينعدم، ودليل أن القديم لا ينعدم: أن القديم لا يكون وجوده إلا واجباً، فلا يقبل العدم. وقوله (لاحنا) منتحت من قولنا: لا حوادث لا أول لها، وهو رد لقولهم: لا نسلم أن ملازم الحادث حادث؛ لجواز أن تكون الأعراض حوادث لا أول لها، فيكون ملازمها قديماً، ودليل أنه لا حوادث لا أول لها: أنها حيث كانت حوادث لزم أن يكون لها أول، فيلزم على قولهم حوادث لا أول لها التناقض، ومما يبطله برهان القطع والتطبيق، وهو مبسوط في غير هذا المحل. وهذه المطالب السبعة لا يعرفها إلا الراسخون في العلم.
The proof by it of the temporal origination of bodies, and the proof of the existence of the additional [attribute] which is the accident: observation. And his statement 'ma qama' with the elision of the alif of 'ma' for meter is a refutation of their statement: 'We do not concede the non-existence of the accident; because it is possible that it subsists by itself if the body is not qualified by it.' And the proof that it does not subsist by itself: that an attribute is not intelligible without an attributed thing, so motion is not intelligible without a moving thing, for example. And his statement 'ma intaqala' with sukun of the lam for meter is a refutation of their statement: 'We do not concede the non-existence of the accident; because it is possible that it transfers from one body to another body.' And the proof that it does not transfer: that if it transferred, it would, after separating from the first and before reaching the second, be subsisting by itself, and it has been invalidated before that. And his statement 'ma yakmun' is a refutation of their statement: 'We do not concede the non-existence of the accident; because it is possible that it is latent in the body, so motion becomes latent in the body when it is at rest, for example.' And the proof that it does not become latent: that it would entail the combination of opposites, which is invalid. And his statement 'ma infakka' is a refutation of their statement: 'We do not concede the inseparable association of the body with the accident; because it is possible that it disassociates from it.' And the proof that it does not disassociate: that a body devoid of motion or rest, for example, is not intelligible; because of the impossibility of the removal of the two contradictories. And his statement 'la 'adamu qadim' is a refutation of their statement: 'We do not concede the temporal origination of the accident; because it is possible that it is eternal and becomes non-existent.' And the proof that the eternal does not become non-existent: that the eternal's existence is only necessary, so it does not admit non-existence. And his statement 'lahina' derived from our statement: 'There are no events with no beginning' is a refutation of their statement: 'We do not concede that that which is inseparable from the temporally originated is temporally originated; because it is possible that the accidents are events with no beginning, so their inseparable associate is eternal.' And the proof that there are no events with no beginning: that since they are events, they must have a beginning, so their statement 'events with no beginning' entails contradiction. And that which invalidates it is the proof of severing and matching, which is expounded elsewhere. And these seven points are not known except by those firmly rooted in knowledge.

Misconception & Correction

What is the proof that the eternal does not become non-existent?

?

?
The proof shows that every event has a beginning. Your own life began at a specific moment. What does that beginning demand of you in terms of accountability to the One who started it? What will you do today to prepare for the meeting with Him?
?
You rely on things that begin and end every day. How does realizing that only the Necessary Being truly sustains you change how you face your next disappointment or loss? Commit to one action that reflects trust in the Eternal rather than in what passes away.

The Seven Refutations: Conclusion and Significance
Concluding remark on the seven refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of the seven refutations and why they are known only to those firmly rooted in knowledge.

Core Idea
The seven refutations are profound points accessible only to advanced scholars.

The passage concludes by stating that these seven refutations (المطالب السبعة) are not known except by those firmly rooted in knowledge (الراسخون في العلم). This highlights the depth of kalam reasoning and the need for serious study.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding sentence
Read the final line: 'وهذه المطالب السبعة لا يعرفها إلا الراسخون في العلم'.
This is a direct statement about the difficulty of these points.
2
Reflect on the meaning
Consider that these refutations are not superficial; they require deep understanding of logical principles and theology.
The term 'الراسخون في العلم' is from the Quran.
3
Connect to the broader lesson
These seven points collectively establish the impossibility of accidents being eternal, which is a key proof for the temporal origination of bodies.
This ties back to the overall argument for a necessary Creator.

Definition of Hajj and Dispute on Obligation Year
Defines Hajj linguistically and legally, then lists opinions on its obligation year.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and legal definitions of Hajj and the scholarly dispute regarding the year it was made obligatory.

Core Idea
Hajj is more than a journey; it is a ritual intent culminating at Arafat.

This lesson unpacks the foundational definition of Hajj, both in language and in Islamic law, and then navigates the historical debate among scholars about exactly when this pillar was commanded. You'll see how the author prioritizes terms and why even the timing of obligation matters for understanding the development of Islamic practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Let the author's flow introduce the definitions and the dispute.
Notice the author's comment on the order of topics in the poem.
2
Understand the two definitions
Identify the linguistic definition (absolute intent) and the legal definition (intending the Kaaba for the ritual including standing at Arafat). Reflect on why both are given.
The legal definition adds precision to the general linguistic meaning.
3
List the opinions on obligation year
Go through each opinion: before Hijra, after Hijra in 5th, 6th (preferred by Shafi'is), 7th, 8th, or 9th year (preferred by Ibn al-Kamal). Note which are emphasized.
The dispute shows ijtihad in determining historical rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الحَجُ) قدَّمَهُ لضرورة النظم، وإن كانت الصلاة أفضل منه، فإن بعضهم يُكفر بتركها كسلاً بعد أمر الإمام، بل الصيام أفضلُ من الحج على المعتمد . وهو لغةٌ : مطلقُ القصد، وشرعاً : قصد الكعبة للنُّسكِ المشتمل على الوقوف بعرفة . وقد اختلف في أي سنةٍ فُرضَ ؟ فقيل : فُرِضَ قبل الهجرة، ونزول قوله تعالى: وَلِلَّهِ عَلَى النَّاسِ حِجُ الْبَيْتِ) [آل عمران: ۹۷] الآية بعدها إنما هو للتأكيد ؛ وقيل : فُرِضَ بعد الهجرة، وعليه؛ فقيل : في الخامسة وقيل في السادسة وصححه الشافعية، : : وقيل في السابعة وقيل في الثامنة ، وقيل : في التاسعة وصححه ابن الكمال.
His saying: 'Al-Hajj' – he preceded it due to necessity of order, even though prayer is more excellent than it, for indeed some declare disbelief upon its abandonment out of laziness after the Imam's command, rather fasting is more excellent than Hajj according to the relied-upon. It is linguistically: absolute intent, and legally: intending the Kaaba for the ritual that includes standing at Arafat. And they differed concerning which year it was obligated. It was said: it was obligated before the Hijra, and the revelation of His statement 'And to Allah is upon the people the Hajj of the House' (3:97) after it is only for emphasis. And it was said: it was obligated after the Hijra, and accordingly: it was said in the fifth, and it was said in the sixth – and the Shafi'is corrected it – and it was said in the seventh, and it was said in the eighth, and it was said in the ninth – and Ibn al-Kamal corrected it.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines Hajj and presents the scholarly dispute on when it became obligatory. The author first explains why the word 'Hajj' is placed before prayer in the original poem: for poetic order, despite prayer being more virtuous. He notes that some scholars even consider abandoning prayer out of laziness as disbelief, and that fasting is considered better than Hajj in the relied-upon view. Then he gives the linguistic definition of Hajj as 'absolute intent' and the legal definition as 'intending the Kaaba for the ritual that includes standing at Arafat.' Finally, he lists the various opinions on the year of obligation: before the Hijra (with the verse being only for emphasis), or after the Hijra in the fifth, sixth (preferred by Shafi'is), seventh, eighth, or ninth year (preferred by Ibn al-Kamal). The passage thus provides both a clear definition and a summary of the historical disagreement.

Key Terms
الحج
النسك
الوقوف بعرفة
الفرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Hajj is just a journey to Mecca without specific ritual conditions.
Correction: The legal definition specifies that Hajj is intending the Kaaba for the ritual that includes standing at Arafat – it is a defined act of worship, not merely travel.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal (shar‘i) definition of Hajj?
A) Absolute intent (al-qasd al-mutlaq)
B) Intending the Kaaba for the ritual that includes standing at Arafat
C) Visiting the Kaaba at any time
D) The physical journey to Mecca

Key Takeaway
Hajj is defined linguistically as absolute intent, legally as the Kaaba-bound ritual that includes standing at Arafat, and scholars disagreed on whether it was obligated before or after the Hijra, with opinions ranging from the fifth to the ninth year.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you intend to do something for Allah, how certain are you that your intention is purely for Him? This lesson defines Hajj as 'absolute intent' towards the Kaaba. Is your spiritual intent that absolute? In what areas of your life have you been content with vague intentions? Commit today to clarify one intention before acting.
?
The scholars disagreed on the exact year Hajj was obligated, yet all agreed on its obligation. You may not know the exact details of every obligation, but do you act on what you know? What is one religious duty you have been delaying, using uncertainty as an excuse? Resolve to fulfill it immediately.
?
The passage ranks prayer above Hajj, yet some declare disbelief for abandoning prayer. Do you treat prayer with that seriousness? Or have you been casual about it? What would change if you truly believed prayer is more excellent than Hajj?

Rational Proof for Increase and Decrease of Iman
The author presents a rational proof using reduction to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a rational reductio argument establishes that iman must increase and decrease.

Core Idea
Iman must vary because otherwise sinful believers would equal prophets.

The author now presents a rational proof (hujja 'aqliyya) to support the claim that iman increases and decreases. This proof does not rely on scripture but on logical necessity. By considering an absurd consequence, the argument shows that variation in iman is unavoidable. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure 'if...then'.
The proof is short but dense.
2
Identify the premise and consequence
Identify the premise (P: no variation in iman) and the consequence (Q: equality between sinful believers and prophets). Understand that Q is considered false.
The key terms 'lazim' (necessary consequence) and 'malzum' (that which necessitates) are crucial.
3
Recognize the logical form
The argument is a classic modus tollens: If P then Q, not Q, therefore not P. This proves that variation exists.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the implication
Understand that this rational proof compels the conclusion that iman must increase and decrease. Reflect on how this aligns with logical necessity.
The proof does not rely on revelation but on reason alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما العقلية : فهي أنَّه لو لم تتفاوت حقيقة الإيمان بالزيادة والنقص، لكان إيمان آحاد الأمة بل المنهمكين على الفسق والمعاصي مساوياً لإيمان الأنبياء والملائكة واللازم - وهو المساواة - باطل، فكذا الملزومُ الذي هو عدم التفاوت بالزيادة والنقص.
As for the rational proof, it is that if the reality of iman did not vary by increase and decrease, then the iman of individual members of the community, even those immersed in immorality and sins, would be equal to the iman of the prophets and angels. The necessary consequence—which is equality—is false, so likewise that which necessitates it, which is the absence of variation by increase and decrease [is false].
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (hujja 'aqliyya) for the position that iman increases and decreases. The author constructs a conditional argument: if it were true that the reality of iman does not admit of increase and decrease (i.e., it is uniform and unchanging), then the iman of ordinary believers—even those who are deeply immersed in sin—would be equal to the iman of the prophets and angels. But this equality is obviously false (batil) because the faith of a sinful person is clearly deficient compared to that of a prophet or angel. Therefore, the premise that iman does not vary is also false. The argument uses the logical structure: if P (no variation), then Q (equality). Q is false, so P is false. This is a classic reductio ad absurdum. The key terms are: 'reality of iman' (haqiqat al-iman), 'vary' (tatawatu), 'by increase and decrease', 'necessary consequence' (lazim), 'that which necessitates it' (malzum). The author emphasizes that the absurdity is the equality between sinful believers and prophets/angels.

Key Terms
عقلية
حقيقة الإيمان
تتفاوت
زيادة
نقص
لازم
ملزوم
مساواة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes the conclusion that iman varies.
Correction: The proof does not assume variation; it assumes non-variation and derives a false equality, thereby disproving non-variation. It is a reductio ad absurdum.

Checkpoint
What is the false necessary consequence in the rational proof for increase and decrease of iman?
A) That iman increases without decreasing
B) That the iman of sinners equals that of prophets and angels
C) That prophets' iman decreases
D) That angels' iman increases

Key Takeaway
Iman must increase and decrease because denying this leads to the impossible conclusion that all believers have equal faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof forces you to admit that your own iman cannot equal that of the prophets. When you look at a sin you committed today, does your heart feel the weight of that distance from prophetic faith? What will you do tonight to close that gap, even by a single degree?
?
If iman can decrease, then there are actions and thoughts that diminish it. Can you name one habit or distraction in your life that you suspect is eroding your faith? What would it cost you to abandon it?
?
You have now seen that the equality of all believers is logically impossible. Yet many Muslims live as though their faith is immune to decline. Are you among them? What concrete measure will you take this week to increase your iman, not just wish for it?
