The Rational Proof for the Increase and Decrease of Faith
The author presents a rational argument demonstrating that faith must increase and decrease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the rational argument proving that faith admits of increase and decrease.

Core Idea
Faith must vary because otherwise sinners equal prophets.

This passage presents a crisp rational proof (ḍalīl ʿaqli) for the Sunni position that faith (īmān) increases and decreases. The argument is built on a simple but powerful conditional: if faith were uniform, then the faith of the worst sinners would equal that of the highest prophets. Since this consequence is obviously false, the premise is false. In this lesson, we will unpack each step of the proof and see why it is considered decisive in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Begin by reading the Arabic text aloud or silently. Then read the literal translation. Notice the conciseness: the entire proof is contained in two sentences.
The key terms are: حجة عقلية (rational proof), تفاوت (variation), مساواة (equality), لازم (consequence), ملزوم (antecedent).
2
Identify the conditional
Locate the 'if' statement: 'if the reality of faith did not vary by increase and decrease'. This is the antecedent (ملزوم) that the proof intends to reject.
3
Identify the consequence
Find the 'then' part: 'then the faith of individual members of the community... would be equal to the faith of the prophets and angels.' This consequence (لازم) is asserted to be false.
Why is this consequence false? Because the faith of prophets and angels is inherently superior to that of sinners.
4
Understand the logical form
The argument follows the modus tollens pattern: If P then Q; Not Q; Therefore not P. Here P = 'faith does not vary', Q = 'all believers have equal faith'. Since Q is false, P must be false.
This logical form is universally valid in both classical and modern logic.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is implicit: faith must vary by increase and decrease. This establishes the rational basis for the doctrine that faith is not a fixed quantity but admits of degrees.
This conclusion aligns with the Ashʿarī position that faith consists of both conviction (taṣdīq) and works (ʿamal), with works contributing to increase.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد احتجوا على أن الإيمان يزيد وينقص بحجة عقلية ونقلية : أما العقلية : فهي أنَّه لو لم تتفاوت حقيقة الإيمان بالزيادة والنقص، لكان إيمان آحاد الأمة بل المنهمكين على الفسق والمعاصي مساوياً لإيمان الأنبياء والملائكة واللازم - وهو المساواة - باطل، فكذا الملزومُ الذي هو عدم التفاوت بالزيادة والنقص .
And they adduced in support that faith increases and decreases a rational and transmitted proof. As for the rational: it is that if the reality of faith did not vary by increase and decrease, then the faith of individual members of the community, indeed those immersed in immorality and sins, would be equal to the faith of the prophets and angels. And the necessary consequence—equality—is false, so likewise the antecedent, which is the absence of variation by increase and decrease.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (al-ʿaqliyya) for the position that faith increases and decreases. The argument is structured as a conditional proof. The premise is: if faith did not vary (i.e., it is uniform without degrees), then the faith of any ordinary person—even those deeply engaged in sin—would be equal to the faith of prophets and angels. This consequence (musāwāt, equality) is clearly false (bāṭil). Therefore, the antecedent must also be false. That is, the assumption that faith has no increase or decrease is false. The proof is valid because Muslims intuitively recognize that the faith of prophets and angels is superior to that of sinners. Thus faith must admit of degrees.

Key Terms

تفاوت
مساواة
لازم
ملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think rational proofs in kalām are weak compared to revelation.
Correction: This proof shows that a simple rational argument can be decisive. The rational proof is not opposed to revelation but supports it. The author later presents a transmitted proof as well, showing the harmony of reason and revelation.

Checkpoint

A) The faith of sinners increases
B) The faith of all believers is equal
C) The faith of prophets decreases
D) Faith does not exist in sinners

Key Takeaway
Faith must admit of increase and decrease because otherwise the faith of sinners would equal that of prophets, which is obviously false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your faith today were exactly the same as your faith a year ago, would you be satisfied? What does your answer reveal about whether you believe faith can increase?
?
Consider the logical consequence: if faith does not vary, then your faith equals that of the most sinful person. Does that thought disturb you enough to actively seek ways to increase your faith? If not, why not?
?
The argument uses the clear absurdity of sinners equaling prophets. Do you live as if your faith is meant to grow, or have you become comfortable with stagnation? Commit to one concrete action today to increase your faith.

Definitions of Circularity and Infinite Regress
Defines al-dawr and al-tasalsul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of circularity (al-dawr) and infinite regress (al-tasalsul) as used in kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
Circularity and infinite regress are logical impossibilities used to prove a first cause.

Before we can appreciate the proof that requires an unconditioned Necessary Being, we must clearly define two key terms: circularity and infinite regress. These are not ordinary everyday concepts but precise logical conditions that invalidate chains of reasoning. The author provides concise definitions that form the building blocks of the kalam argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the precise wording used for each term.
Both definitions are short and technical.
2
Contrast the two terms
Understand how circularity involves a closed loop (thing depends on itself indirectly) while infinite regress involves an endless chain without a beginning.
3
Reflect on implications
Ask yourself: Why would both be impossible in a chain of causes? This prepares you for the proof that relies on these concepts.
Consider everyday examples like a chicken-and-egg scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحقيقة الدور: توقف الشيء على ما توقف عليه إما بمرتبة أو أكثر. وحقيقة التسلسل : ترتب أمورٍ غير متناهية(۱) .
And the reality of circularity (al-dawr) is: a thing's dependence on that which it itself depends upon, either by one degree or more. And the reality of infinite regress (al-tasalsul) is: the arrangement of infinite things.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage provides concise definitions of two key terms: al-dawr (circularity) and al-tasalsul (infinite regress). Al-dawr is defined as a situation where a thing depends on something that itself depends on the original thing, either directly (one degree) or through multiple steps. Al-tasalsul is defined as an ordered chain of things that has no end. These definitions are foundational for the proof in the previous lesson.

Key Terms
دور
تسلسل
توقف
ترتب
غير متناهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think any mutual dependence is circularity, but here it specifically means a logical circle that is vicious.
Correction: The text defines circularity as a thing's dependence on that which it depends upon, creating a closed loop. Not all mutual dependence is illogical; only when it prevents an ultimate foundation.

Checkpoint

A) Exactly one degree
B) Two degrees
C) One or more degrees
D) An infinite number of degrees

Key Takeaway
Circularity is a closed loop of dependence, and infinite regress is an endless chain; both are used in kalam to show that only a self-sufficient, uncaused cause can stop the regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything you rely on for meaning or security is itself dependent on something else, eventually you face an infinite regress. This lesson shows that only a self-sufficient cause (Allah) can end that regress. Examine your life: What is the final cause you are trusting for your purpose and ultimate hope? Is it truly Allah, or is it something that itself depends on other things? Commit to making Allah the sole ultimate cause of your life today.
?
The definition of circularity includes loops of dependency. Think about your worship: Do you sometimes perform good deeds seeking a worldly return, which in turn motivates more deeds? That is a subtle circularity. This lesson calls you to break that loop and worship Allah alone as the uncaused cause. Are your intentions pure from such hidden circles? Resolve to purify your intention (ikhlas) for Allah alone.
?
Infinite regress is impossible in a causal chain. Your daily actions set off a chain of consequences that stretch into the future. This lesson reminds you that the ultimate end of all chains is the decree of Allah, who is the first cause. How does this certainty affect your anxiety about what will happen tomorrow? Will you trust that the final outcome is in His hands and act accordingly?

Impossibility of Circularity (al-Dawr)
Demonstrates why al-dawr is rationally impossible via example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why circularity (al-dawr) is rationally impossible because it necessitates a logical contradiction of a thing being both prior and posterior to itself.

Core Idea
Circularity makes a single thing both prior and posterior to itself — impossible.

The author now addresses a key logical pitfall: circular reasoning in causal relationships. He demonstrates that if two things are mutually dependent in a circle, it leads to an absurd contradiction. By examining a simple example, we see why circularity is not just unlikely but rationally impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic original and its translation to grasp the definition and example.
2
Understand the example
Focus on the Zayd and 'Amr scenario: how does it illustrate the contradiction of self-priority and self-posteriority?
The example makes abstract logic concrete.
3
Reflect on the rule
The author states that circularity necessitates a thing being prior and posterior to itself. Why is this a logical contradiction?
This principle is foundational for many kalam arguments against infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما كان الدور مستحيلاً لأنه يلزم عليه كون الشيء الواحدِ سابقاً على نفسه مسبوقاً بها، فإذا فرضنا أن زيداً أوجد عمراً، وأن عمراً أوجد زيداً، لزم أن زيداً متقدم على نفسه متأخّرُ عنها، وأن عمراً كذلك (٢) .
And circularity is only impossible because it necessitates that a single thing be prior to itself and posterior to itself. For if we assume that Zayd created 'Amr, and that 'Amr created Zayd, it necessarily follows that Zayd is prior to himself and posterior to himself, and likewise 'Amr.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage explains why circularity (al-dawr) is impossible. The author gives a concrete example: if Zayd created 'Amr, and 'Amr created Zayd, then Zayd would have to be both before himself (as creator of 'Amr) and after himself (as created by 'Amr). This is contradictory. Therefore, circularity entails a logical contradiction and is thus impossible.

Key Terms

Key Takeaway
Circularity is rationally impossible because it forces a single entity to be both prior and posterior to itself, a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

We learn that circularity is impossible because it makes a thing prior and posterior to itself. In your own life, are there patterns of thought or action that are circular—where you keep returning to the same excuses or sins, making yourself both the cause and the effect of your problems? What concrete step will you take today to break that cycle and submit fully to Allah?

But have you ever made your own happiness or success dependent on something that depends on you, creating a closed loop of self-sufficiency independent of Allah? How can you reorient your heart so that your ultimate reliance is only on the Creator, not on created means?

The Overlapping Proof Against Infinite Regress
The author presents the main proof against infinite regress: the overlapping argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the burhan al-tatbiq proof and why it demonstrates the impossibility of an actual infinite regress.

Core Idea
If two infinite series are overlapped, absurdities arise — proving infinite regress is impossible.

Theologians developed several proofs to show that an actual infinite regress is impossible. One of the most elegant is the proof of overlapping (burhan al-tatbiq). In this lesson, we will examine how overlapping two supposed infinite series leads to contradictions that force us to reject the possibility of an infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup of the proof
Imagine two series: one starting now and going infinite in the past, another starting from the Flood (a finite time ago) and going infinite in the past. Overlap them by matching individuals.
The series are not coextensive; one begins later.
2
Consider the three possibilities
There are three outcomes: both run out, neither runs out, or one runs out before the other. Each leads to an impossibility.
3
Conclude the impossibility
Since all three outcomes are absurd, the original assumption of an actual infinite regress must be false.
The proof targets actual infinity in the past, not potential future infinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما كان التسلسلُ مستحيلاً لأدلة أقامها المتكلمون، أجلُّها برهان التطبيق (۳) ؛ وتقريرُهُ أنَّك لو فرضت سلسلتين، وجعلت إحداهما من الآن إلى ما لا نهاية له، والأخرى من الطوفان إلى ما لا نهاية له وطبَّقت بينهما ؛ بأن قابلت (٤) بين أفرادهما من أولهما، فكلَّما طرحت من الآنيَّةِ واحداً طرحت في مقابلته من الطوفانية واحداً ... وهكذا ؛ فلا يخلو : فإما أن يفرغا معاً فيكون كلّ منهما له نهاية وهو خلاف الفرض . وإن لم يفرغا لزمَ مساواة الناقص للكامل وهو باطل. وإن فرغت الطوفانيَّةُ دون الآنيَّةِ كانت الطوفانية متناهية والآنيَّة أيضاً كذلك ؛ لأنَّها إنما (۱) زادَتْ على الطوفانيَّةِ بقدر متناه، وهو ما من الطوفان إلى الآن، ومن المعلوم أن الزائد على شيء متناءٍ بقدر متناه يكون متناهياً بالضرورة.
And infinite regress is only impossible due to proofs established by the theologians, the greatest of which is the proof of overlapping (burhan al-tatbiq). Its exposition: You suppose two series, one from now to infinity, and the other from the Flood to infinity, and you overlay them by matching their individuals from their beginnings. So whenever you remove one from the 'now' series, you remove one in its counterpart from the 'flood' series, and so on. Then it is inevitable: either both run out, in which case each has an end, contrary to the assumption; or they do not run out, which would require the deficient to equal the complete, which is false; or the 'flood' series runs out before the 'now' series, then the 'flood' series is finite and likewise the 'now' series, because it only exceeds the 'flood' series by a finite amount – that which is from the Flood to the present – and it is known that what exceeds a finite thing by a finite amount is necessarily finite.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents the famous proof of overlapping (burhan al-tatbiq) to demonstrate the impossibility of an actual infinite regress. The author considers two parallel infinite series: one starting now and extending infinitely into the past, and another starting from the time of the Flood (a finite time ago) and extending infinitely into the past. They are overlapped by pairing each term of the first series with a corresponding term of the second. Three possibilities arise: (1) both series run out, contradicting the assumption of infinity; (2) neither runs out, implying the shorter series (from Flood) equals the longer (from now), which is false; (3) the Flood series runs out first, which forces the now series to also be finite because it exceeds the Flood series by a finite amount. All three are impossible, so an actual infinite regress cannot exist.

Key Terms
تسلسل
برهان التطبيق
غير متناهية
متناهية
ناقص
كامل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof applies to any infinite regress, including future potential infinity.
Correction: The proof only applies to an actual infinite regress in the past (e.g., a series of past events). It does not rule out potential infinity extending into the future, as that is not a completed totality.

Checkpoint
In the second possibility of the burhan al-tatbiq, what absurd consequence follows?
A) Both series are finite
B) The deficient equals the complete
C) The longer series has more terms
D) The series are identical

Key Takeaway
The burhan al-tatbiq shows that an actual infinite regress leads to logical contradictions, thereby proving that existence cannot have an infinite chain of causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that an infinite chain of excuses or delays is logically impossible. What is one 'finite' step you are avoiding today that would break a chain holding you back from a responsibility you know is yours?
?
If every chain must have a first cause, and you acknowledge Allah as that First Cause, does your daily life reflect a genuine dependence on Him as your ultimate starting point, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient?
?
The third outcome of the proof reveals that adding a finite amount to a finite series still yields a finite series. In your spiritual journey, are you trying to add finite good deeds to an otherwise infinite gap with Allah, or have you sought the only infinite solution—His mercy and guidance?

The Proof Against Infinite Regress and Modern Skepticism
Warning about modern skepticism on infinite regress proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational role of the proof against infinite regress in kalam and the modern controversy surrounding it.

Core Idea
The proof against infinite regress is the backbone of kalam, but it has been challenged.

This lesson explores a footnote from the commentator that highlights a crucial debate within Islamic theology: the validity of the proof against infinite regress. While classical theologians considered it essential, some modern scholars have cast doubt. Understanding this debate helps you see how kalam arguments have been received over time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noting that it begins by referring to longer works and then presents a warning (tanbih) about modern scholars.
The passage includes a footnote embedded in parentheses.
2
Identify the key proofs and scholars
The commentator mentions two proofs relied upon by al-Ghazali: the exhaustion of what has no end and the impossibility of a chain of contingent things. Notice also the names Muhammad Abduh and Muhammad Bakhit al-Muti‘i as critics.
3
Reflect on the commentator's stance
The commentator strongly criticizes the modern scholars, stating that mastering these proofs is of utmost importance. Ask yourself why he feels so strongly.
This connects to the learning objective about the foundational role of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتعلَّق به هنا مباحث تطلب من المطوّلات (۲) . (۲) أورد العلامة الأمير في حاشيته على عبد السلام (ص) (۱۹) - (۷۳) أحد عشر دليلاً، العاشر والحادي عشر منها هما اللذان عوّل عليهما إمامنا الغزالي وهما : انقضاء ما لا نهاية له، وذكره في «الاقتصاد»، وكون سلسلة الحوادث التي لا نهاية لها بزعمهم أفرادها حادثة جائزة، وما كان مؤلفاً من جائز حادث فهو جائز حادث، وذكر هذا في «المستظهري». قال العلامة الأمير بعد ذكر هذه الأدلة التي الواحد منها يُغني عن سائرها، ومن لم يقنع بفرد منها أقنعه آخر قال: (والأنور في شرح القلب الفزع إلى القرآن والسنة المؤيدة بالمعجزات المتمم نورها على مدى الأوقات، وفيهما ما يدل على أنه تعالى هو الأول. تنبيه : ذهب بعض المحدثين كالشيخ محمد عبدو رحمه الله تعالى إلى استبعاد أو إنكار وجود دليل على بطلان التسلسل، وقد نبه العلماء إلى أن إتقان دليلي بطلان التسلسل والدور من أهم الأمور ؛ إذ على ذلك تعويل أكثر أدلة المتكلمين وقد مالَ إلى قول الشيخ محمد عبدو عصريه العلامة التحرير مفتي الديار المصرية الشيخ محمد بخيت المطيعي، حيث قال كلمة غير مرضية : (إن بطلان التسلسل لغاية اليوم لم يقم عليه دليل برهاني سالم مما يبطله !!) ، وله مع هذه الكلمة آراء منغصة ؛ كقوله بتعليل أفعاله تعالى بالأغراض والمصالح، ومسألة افتقار الممكن بعضه إلى بعض، وكفى المرء نبلاً أن تعد معايبه، وإنما كان التنبيه لأن العلامة المطيعي علم كبير له منة في أعناق من بعده، وهذه الهنات تقصى غفر الله لنا جميعاً .
And related to it here are investigations to be sought from the longer works. (2) The scholar al-Amir cited in his commentary on 'Abd al-Salam eleven proofs, the tenth and eleventh being those relied upon by our Imam al-Ghazali, namely: the exhaustion of what has no end, mentioned in al-Iqtisad, and that the chain of events which has no end according to them has individuals that are originated and contingent, and whatever is composed of contingent originated things is contingent and originated, and this is mentioned in al-Mustazhari. After mentioning these proofs, of which one suffices and if one is not convinced by one, another will convince him, he said: 'The clearest way is to resort to the Qur'an and Sunnah supported by miracles, whose light is complete over time, and in them is indication that He is the First.' Warning: Some moderns, like Shaykh Muhammad ‘Abduh, went to the extreme of rejecting or denying the existence of a proof for the impossibility of infinite regress. Scholars have warned that mastering the two proofs of the impossibility of infinite regress and circularity is among the most important matters, since most proofs of the theologians rely on that. His contemporary the scholarly editor, the Mufti of the Egyptian lands, Shaykh Muhammad Bakhit al-Muti‘i, inclined to the opinion of Shaykh Muhammad ‘Abduh, saying a displeasing word: 'The impossibility of infinite regress has not, up to today, been established by a sound proof free of what invalidates it!!' Along with this word he had other unpleasant opinions, like his saying that Allah's actions are caused by purposes and interests, and the issue of the need of some contingent things for others. Sufficient for a man's nobility is that his faults are counted. This warning is because al-Muti‘i is a great scholar, having favors upon those after him, but these flaws are objectionable. May Allah forgive us all.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by the author of Tuhfat al-Mureed (or the editor) that first refers to longer discussions on the proofs, then quotes from al-Amir's commentary that lists eleven proofs against infinite regress, highlighting that the tenth and eleventh are those of al-Ghazali. Then there is a warning (tanbih) about modern scholars who have doubted the validity of the proof against infinite regress, specifically Muhammad ‘Abduh and Muhammad Bakhit al-Muti‘i. The commentator strongly criticizes their views, emphasizing the foundational importance of these proofs in kalam. This lesson shows the later debate within Islamic theology about the philosophical arguments.

Correction: Some modern scholars like Muhammad Abduh and Muhammad Bakhit al-Muti‘i have questioned or denied the existence of a sound proof.

Distinguishing Our Non-Existence from That of the Impossible
The author answers an objection distinguishing our pre-eternal non-existence from that of the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critical distinction between the necessity of our pre-eternal non-existence and the absolute necessity of the impossible's non-existence, and why this distinction preserves the rationality of God's creation.

Core Idea
Our non-existence was necessary only in the past; the impossible's non-existence is necessary forever.

This short passage tackles a subtle objection that might confuse the beginner: if both our non-existence and that of the impossible are necessary in pre-eternity, how are they different? The author's answer hinges on a key distinction about the scope of necessity. Let's examine the precise wording and see how the argument resolves the apparent equivalence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the objection is phrased as a question and the answer begins with 'فإن' and 'لأنا نقول'.
Pay attention to the structure: objection, then answer.
2
Identify the key terms 'مقيَّد' and 'على الإطلاق'
These two terms are the heart of the distinction. Compare how necessity is qualified in each case: restricted vs absolute.
Think about the temporal dimension: past vs all times.
3
Reflect on the theological consequence
If our non-existence were absolutely necessary, we could never exist. The distinction shows that God's bringing us into existence is not impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يُقالُ: أيُّ فرق بين عدينا وعدم المستحيل كالشريك؟ فإن كلا منهما واجب في الأزلِ؛ لأنا نقول: وجوب عدمنا مقيَّد بالأزلِ، فهو ممكن فيما لا يزال، وأما عدمُ المستحيل فواجب على الإطلاق(٥) .
It is not said: What is the difference between our non-existence and the non-existence of the impossible, like the partner (to God)? For each of them is necessary in pre-eternity. So we say: The necessity of our non-existence is restricted to pre-eternity, so it is possible in that which has no end (i.e., future). As for the non-existence of the impossible, it is absolutely necessary.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that both our non-existence in pre-eternity and the non-existence of an impossible thing (like a partner to God) are necessary in pre-eternity, so they seem equivalent. The author responds by showing the difference: our non-existence is necessary only in the past (pre-eternity), but it becomes possible in the future (since we later exist). In contrast, the non-existence of the impossible is necessary at all times—absolute. The word 'مقيَّد' (restricted) and 'على الإطلاق' (absolutely) highlight this distinction. The footnote marker (٥) refers to a citation confirming the source.

Key Terms
عدم
أزل
واجب
ممكن
مستحيل
إطلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That our pre-eternal non-existence is equivalent to the non-existence of an impossible thing because both are necessary in pre-eternity.
Correction: Our non-existence is necessary only for the past; the impossible's non-existence is necessary at all times, including the future.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the necessity of our non-existence in pre-eternity from the necessity of the impossible's non-existence?
A) Ours is absolute, the impossible's is temporary.
B) Ours is restricted to pre-eternity, the impossible's is absolute.
C) Both are absolute.
D) Neither is necessary.

Key Takeaway
Our non-existence in pre-eternity was necessary only for that past period, while the impossible's non-existence is necessary at all times.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that your existence was not necessary from eternity but only came about by God's choice, does your daily life reflect that gratitude? What would change if you lived with the constant awareness that you could have remained non-existent forever?
?
The impossibility of a partner to God is absolute—it will never exist. Yet you, a mere human, exist by God's will. Does that not demand a humble and certain conviction that He alone is worthy of worship? In what areas of your life do you act as if you could ignore this truth?

The Proof for Mukhalafah from Qidam
Sanusi presents the proof for God's dissimilarity based on His established eternity, in two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's eternal existence (qidam) serves as the proof for His dissimilarity to all contingent things.

Core Idea
Eternity proves uniqueness: what is eternal cannot be like what is temporal.

The attribute of mukhalafah—God's dissimilarity to creation—is a cornerstone of Islamic theology. Sanusi provides a concise proof based on the already established eternity (qidam) of God. This lesson unpacks that proof in its two complementary formulations, showing why God's necessary eternity rules out any similarity to contingent beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse everything. Then read the translation. Let the flow of the proof come through.
Notice that the author presents two ways to understand the line from the matn.
2
Follow the conditional argument
Trace the first interpretation: If God were not unlike contingent things, He would be similar to them. If similar, then He would be contingent. But His qidam has been proven, so He cannot be contingent. Therefore, He must be unlike them.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the opposite leads to a contradiction.
3
Understand the direct argument
Consider the second interpretation: qidam itself is the proof. Whoever is qadim (eternal) cannot be annihilated, but all contingent things can be annihilated. So nothing contingent is qadim, hence God (who is qadim) is not among contingent things.
This argument uses the necessary property of qidam (impossibility of annihilation) to separate God from contingent beings.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this proof is important. It establishes God's transcendence without relying on specific attributes—just His necessary eternity. It also shows the logical connection between the attributes of God.
This proof is foundational in kalam for defending tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بُرْهَانُ هذَا القِدَمُ) أي : دليلُ ما ذُكر من أنه مخالف للحوادث، دليل القدم، فكلام المصنف على تقدير مضاف . وتقرير البرهان أن تقول : لو لم يكن مخالفاً للحوادث، لكان مماثلاً لها، ولو كان مماثلاً لها، لكان حادثاً، كيف وقد ثبت قدمُهُ بالدليل السابق؟! ويصح إبقاء كلام المصنف على ظاهره فيكون نفس القدم هو الدليل على المخالفة ؛ لأن كلَّ مَن وجب له القدم، استحالَ عليه العدم، ولا شيء من الحوادث يستحيل عليه العدم، فلا شيء منها بقديم، فثبتت المخالفة.
His saying: (The proof of this is qidam) i.e., the evidence for what was mentioned that He is unlike contingent things is the evidence of qidam, so the author's speech is on the estimation of an addition. The presentation of the proof is that you say: If He were not unlike contingent things, He would be similar to them, and if He were similar to them, He would be contingent, especially since His qidam has been established by the previous evidence. And it is valid to keep the author's speech on its apparent meaning, so that qidam itself is the evidence for dissimilarity, because everyone for whom qidam is necessary, annihilation is impossible for him, and no contingent thing has annihilation impossible for it, so none of them is qadim, thus the dissimilarity is established.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage explains the proof (burhan) for the attribute of mukhalafah based on qidam (eternity). The author first clarifies that the matn line 'the proof of this is al-qidam' means that the evidence for God's dissimilarity is His proven eternity. He then presents the argument in two ways: (1) If God were not unlike contingent things, He would be similar to them in reality (mumathalah fi al-mahiyyah). But similarity to contingent things would entail contingency (huduth), since contingent things are essentially temporal. However, God's qidam has already been proven, so He cannot be contingent, hence He must be unlike them. (2) Alternatively, take the statement at face value: qidam itself is the proof. Everything that is qadim (eternal) necessarily cannot be annihilated, whereas all contingent things are subject to annihilation. Therefore, no contingent thing is qadim, so God, being qadim, is not from among contingent things, establishing His dissimilarity. This proof is foundational in kalam for defending God's transcendence.

Key Terms
برهان
قدم
مماثلة
حادث
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof of qidam already assumes dissimilarity, making the argument circular.
Correction: Actually, the proof uses qidam (which is already proven independently) to derive dissimilarity. It does not assume dissimilarity; rather, it shows that dissimilarity follows necessarily from qidam.

Checkpoint
According to the proof in its first formulation, if God were similar to contingent things, what would follow?
A) He would be eternal
B) He would be contingent
C) He would be unlike them
D) He would be impossible

Key Takeaway
The proof from qidam establishes mukhalafah by showing that God's necessary eternity is incompatible with the contingency inherent in all temporal beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what ways have you, perhaps unknowingly, likened God to something created in your thoughts or actions? How does the logical proof in this lesson expose that error, and what will you do to correct it starting today?
?
The proof shows that God's eternity makes it impossible for Him to be anything like contingent beings. How does this truth confront the anxiety you feel about change and impermanence in your life? What comfort or demand does it place on you right now?
?
If the impossibility of God's annihilation is absolute, how does that affect the way you trust His promises and His constancy? Are there any areas of your life where you act as if God could fail or change? Give one concrete step to align your heart with this truth.

The Three Aspects of Divine Oneness
Sanusi introduces the threefold division of tawhid and situates the present discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that tawhid encompasses three aspects: oneness of essence, oneness of attributes, and oneness of acts, and that the present passage focuses on the first two in terms of having no equal.

Core Idea
Divine oneness has three inseparable aspects: essence, attributes, and acts.

Sanusi opens his discussion of tawhid by highlighting its supreme importance: the science is named after it and the Quran repeatedly praises it. He then clarifies exactly what ‘oneness’ means in the present context—it is primarily oneness of essence and attributes in the sense that nothing is equal to Allah in either. Other aspects (non-composition of essence and oneness of acts) are addressed elsewhere. This passage thus sets the conceptual stage for the detailed analysis that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi first exalts the topic of oneness before defining it.
The passage uses a very compressed style; take your time.
2
Identify the three aspects of oneness
The passage mentions three distinct kinds of oneness: oneness of essence (absence of equal), oneness of attributes (no duplicate attributes), and oneness of acts (no effect from other than Allah). List them.
Only the first two are developed in this section; the third is deferred.
3
Understand what is covered here vs elsewhere
Sanusi explicitly says that oneness of essence as non-composition was already covered, and oneness of acts will come later. Ask yourself: why does he separate them?
This helps you see the structure of the entire matn.
4
Reflect on the Quranic emphasis
The passage cites Quran 2:163 to show the importance of tawhid. Consider how the Quran consistently stresses Allah’s oneness as the foundation of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومبحث الوحدانية أشرفُ مباحث هذا الفن، ولذلك سُمِّي باسم مشتق منها، فقيل : (علم التوحيد)، ولعظم العناية به كثُرَ التنبيه والثناء عليه في الآي القرآنية؛ فقال تعالى: ﴿وَإِلَهُكُمْ إِلَهُ وَاحِدٌ لا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ الرَّحْمَنُ الرَّحِيمُ﴾ [البقرة: ١٦٣] ... إلى غير ذلك من الآيات.

الإلهيات
والمراد منها هنا : وحدة الذات والصفات؛ بمعنى عدم النظير فيهما ؛ وأما وحدة الذاتِ بمعنى عدم التركب من أجزاء، فسبقت في المخالفة للحوادث، ووحدة الصفاتِ بمعنى عدم تعدُّدها من جنس واحدٍ كقدرتين فأكثر وعلمين فأكثر وهكذا، فستاتي في قوله : ( ووحدة أوجب لها) ووحدة الأفعال بمعنى أنه لا تأثير لغيره في فعل من الأفعال، فستأتي أيضاً في قوله : فخالق لعبده وما عمل.
And the topic of oneness (waḥdāniyya) is the noblest of the topics of this art; for that reason it was named with a name derived from it, so it is said: 'the science of tawḥīd'. Because of the great attention to it, there is much admonition and praise of it in the Quranic verses. Allah Most High said: 'And your God is one God; there is no god but Him, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful' [al-Baqarah 2:163] ... and other verses besides.

The Divine Matters
And what is intended by it here is: oneness of the essence (dhāt) and attributes (ṣifāt) meaning the absence of an equal (naẓīr) in both. As for oneness of essence in the sense of not being composed of parts, it preceded in the discussion of dissimilarity to contingents. And oneness of attributes in the sense of there not being multiple attributes of a single kind (like two powers or more, two knowledges or more, etc.) will come in his saying: 'and oneness [of attributes] obligates it'. And oneness of acts (afʿāl) means that there is no effect from other than Him in any act; that will also come in his saying: 'For He is the Creator of His servant and what he does.'
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage establishes the central importance of the attribute of oneness (waḥdāniyya) in the science of kalam, noting that the very name 'tawḥīd' is derived from it and that the Quran repeatedly emphasizes it. The author then defines what is meant by 'oneness' in the present context: it is the oneness of divine essence and attributes, i.e., that nothing is equal or similar to Allah in either. He briefly distinguishes this from two other aspects that are treated elsewhere: (1) oneness of essence in the sense of non-composition (already covered in the attribute of dissimilarity to created things), and (2) oneness of acts (to be discussed later under the statement 'fa-khāliqun li-ʿabdihi wa-mā ʿamila'). Oneness of attributes here means that there cannot be, for example, two powers or two knowledges in Allah; each attribute is unique in its kind. This segment thus sets the foundation for the detailed enumeration of five modes of 'quantity' that follows.

Key Terms
وَحْدَانِيَّة
التَّوْحِيد
وَحْدَة الذَّات
وَحْدَة الصِّفَات
وَحْدَة الأَفْعَال
عِلْمُ التَّوْحِيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think oneness of attributes means Allah has only one attribute (e.g., just power).
Correction: Oneness of attributes means there are no multiple attributes of the same kind (like two powers), but Allah has many distinct attributes (power, will, knowledge, etc.).

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does 'oneness of attributes' (waḥdat al-ṣifāt) mean?
A) Allah has only one attribute.
B) There are no multiple attributes of a single kind in Allah.
C) Attributes are not real.
D) Allah's attributes are like human attributes.

Key Takeaway
Divine oneness is not a single concept but has three distinct aspects—essence, attributes, and acts—and the current discussion focuses on the first two in the sense that nothing is equal to Allah in either.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly affirm that nothing in your life—your successes, your wealth, your relationships—has any independent effect apart from Allah? When you plan your day, do you act as if your own power is the cause, or do you live by the conviction that only Allah has real effect? What habit will you adopt today to remind yourself of waḥdat al-afʿāl?
?
When you learn that oneness of attributes means no two powers or knowledges can exist in Allah, does it make you pause and consider that all praise for any attribute you see in creation ultimately points back to the unique perfection of the Creator? How often do you let the unity of Allah’s attributes transform your worship from distracted routine to focused awe?
?
The passage mentions that tawḥīd is the noblest topic and the Quran praises it abundantly. Have you studied it with the reverence it deserves? Or do you skim over these truths as mere theology? What will change in your daily life if you held this science as the most precious—will you set aside time to learn it deeply, or let other concerns crowd it out?

The Five Modes of Quantity Negated by Tawhid
Enumeration of five modes of quantity negated by divine oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five modes of quantity (kumm) that are negated by the threefold oneness of essence, attributes, and acts in Sunni theology.

Core Idea
Tawhid negates five types of quantity: no parts, no partners, no duplicate attributes, no divine-like creatures, no creator but Allah.

This passage from al-Sanusi's Jawharat al-Tawhid systematically enumerates the five modes of quantity that are incompatible with tawhid. These are the only possible forms of multiplicity or similarity that could be imagined in the divine realm. By negating them, the author clarifies exactly what tawhid means for the essence, attributes, and acts of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the five modes
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author lists five modes in three categories: two for essence, two for attributes, one for acts.
The five modes are all negated by the comprehensive oneness.
2
Understand continuous vs. discontinuous quantity
Continuous quantity refers to internal composition (parts); discontinuous refers to external multiplicity (separate entities). Apply this distinction to essence, attributes, and acts.
For acts, only discontinuous quantity is negated because acts have no internal parts.
3
Examine the objection and answer on attributes
The author anticipates an objection: continuous quantity normally applies to composed things, not attributes. The answer: they treat the inherence of attributes in one essence as analogous to composition.
This shows careful theological reasoning.
4
Note the refutation of the Mu'tazila
The final mode negates the idea that creatures create their own acts. The affirmation of kasb (acquisition) refutes the Mu'tazilite view.
This is a key doctrinal point in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن الوحدانيَّة الشاملة لوحدانية الذات ووحدانية الصفات ووحدانية الأفعال تنفي كموماً خمسة:
الكمُّ المتصل في الذات وهو تركبها من أجزاء.
والكم المنفصل فيها : وهو تعدُّدها بحيث يكون هناك إله ثانٍ فأكثر، وهذان الكمان منفيَّان بوحدة الذات.
والكمُّ المتصل في الصفات وهو التعدد في صفاته تعالى من جنس واحد؛ كقدرتين فأكثر، ويُبحث في هذا بأن الكم المتصل مداره على شيء ذي أجزاء، ولا كذلك الصفاتُ ! ويُجاب بأنهم نزَّلوا كونَها قائمة بذاتٍ واحدة منزلة التركب.
والكم المنفصل في الصفات : وهو أن يكون لغير الله صفةٌ تشبه صفته تعالى؛ كأن يكون لزيد قدرة يوجد بها ويعدمُ بها كقدرته تعالى أو إرادة تخصص الشيء ببعض الممكنات، أو علم محيط بجميع الأشياء، وهذان الكمَّانِ منفيان بوحدانية الصفات.
والكم المنفصلُ في الأفعال : وهو أن يكون لغير الله فعل من الأفعال على وجه الإيجادِ، وإنَّما ينسب الفعلُ له على وجه الكسب والاختيار، وهذا الكم منفي بوحدانية الأفعال، وفي ذلك ردّ على المعتزلة القائلين بأن العبد يخلق أفعال نفسه.
And the summary is that the comprehensive oneness (waḥdāniyya) that includes oneness of essence, oneness of attributes, and oneness of acts negates five modes of quantity (kumūm): The continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttaṣil) in the essence, which is its composition from parts. The discontinuous quantity (al-kamm al-munfaṣil) in it, which is its multiplicity such that there is a second god or more. These two modes of quantity are negated by oneness of essence. The continuous quantity in the attributes, which is multiplicity in His attributes of a single kind, like two powers or more. It is objected that continuous quantity concerns something that has parts, which is not the case for attributes. The answer is that they treat their inherence in one essence as analogous to composition. The discontinuous quantity in the attributes, which is that another than Allah has an attribute resembling His attribute, such as Zayd having a power by which he creates and destroys like His power, or an will that specifies a thing among some possibles, or a knowledge encompassing all things. These two modes of quantity are negated by oneness of attributes. The discontinuous quantity in the acts, which is that another than Allah performs an act in the manner of bringing into existence (ījād); and the act is attributed to him only in the manner of acquisition (kasb) and choice (ikhtiyār). This mode of quantity is negated by oneness of acts, and in this is a refutation of the Muʿtazila who say that the servant creates his own voluntary acts.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage enumerates the five modes of quantity (kumūm, plural of kamm) that are negated by the threefold oneness: essence, attributes, and acts. The author explains each pair: Two pertain to the essence: continuous (composition) and discontinuous (multiplicity of gods). Two pertain to attributes: continuous (multiple attributes of the same kind, e.g., two powers) and discontinuous (a creature possessing a divine-like attribute). The last is about acts: discontinuous (a creature creating his own acts). The author anticipates an objection: continuous quantity normally applies to something composed of parts, but attributes are not composed. The response is that the inherence of attributes in one essence is treated as analogous to composition. The final point refutes the Muʿtazilite doctrine that humans create their own acts; the author affirms acquisition (kasb). This is a complete logical classification that systematically excludes all possible forms of multiplicity or similarity in the divine realm.

Key Terms
الكم المتصل
الكم المنفصل
الذات
الصفات
الأفعال
الكسب
الاختيار
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that humans create their own voluntary acts independently of Allah.
Correction: The passage negates discontinuous quantity in acts, affirming that no creature brings things into existence; acts are only attributed to creatures via acquisition (kasb) and choice, not creation.

Checkpoint
How is the objection answered that continuous quantity cannot apply to attributes since attributes are not composed of parts?
A) It is a category mistake.
B) They treat the inherence of attributes in one essence as analogous to composition.
C) The objection is considered invalid.
D) They deny the existence of attributes.

Key Takeaway
Tawhid negates all forms of quantity in essence, attributes, and acts, affirming the absolute oneness and uniqueness of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself attributing ultimate control or knowledge to a human being—a leader, a scholar, even yourself—as if they possess a share in Allah's attributes? How does this passage challenge that tendency, and what practical step can you take today to redirect your reliance solely to Allah?
?
When you consider your own actions—your successes, your mistakes—do you genuinely see them as created by Allah and only acquired by you, or do you feel a sense of independent origination? What would change in your daily gratitude and humility if you truly believed that every act is from Allah alone?
?
This lesson refutes the Mu'tazila who claimed humans create their own acts. In what subtle ways might you be acting as if you are the ultimate creator of your deeds, and how can you realign your heart to the truth of oneness of acts?

Rational Judgments
beginner
5 min
Definition of Kasbī and Its Inapplicability to Allah
Sanusi's commentator defines kasbī and states its inapplicability to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of kasbī knowledge and why Allah's knowledge cannot be described as kasbī.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge is eternal; calling it 'acquired' implies imperfection.

The commentator begins by defining acquired (kasbī) knowledge, which is obtained through reflection or involves an originated power. He then argues that this category cannot apply to Allah because it would entail originated accidents in His essence and prior ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the commentator presents two distinct definitions of kasbī.
2
Identify the two definitions
The first definition: kasbī is knowledge obtained by reflection and inference. The second: kasbī is what the originated power (qudra) is attached to, which includes sensory knowledge.
The second definition is broader than the first.
3
Understand the argument against applying it to Allah
On both definitions, calling Allah's knowledge kasbī would lead to impossible consequences: originated accidents (hawādith) inhering in His essence and prior ignorance. Both are impossible (muḥāl).
This establishes that Allah's knowledge is not acquired.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينتج العالم حادث، فالعلم بحدوث العالم حاصل عن نظر واستدلال، فهو : :
وقيل : الكسبي هو ما تعلقت به القدرة الحادثة.
وعلى هذا التعريف فيشمل العلم الضروري الحاصل بالحواس كالعلم الحاصل بالإبصار أو بالشم، بخلافه على التعريف الأول ؛ وعلى كل من التعريفين لا يُقال لعلم الله : كسبي ؛ لأنَّه يلزم منه قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى، ويلزم منه أيضاً سبق الجهل في حقه تعالى، وهو محال؛ وما ورد ممَّا يوهم اكتساب علمه تعالى كقوله جلَّ مِنْ قائل : وثُمَّ بَعَثْنَهُمْ لِنَعْلَمَ أَيُّ الْحِزْبَيْنِ أَحْصَى [الكهف: (۱۱۲) مؤول على أن المراد والله أعلم ليظهر لهم متعلَّق ،علمنا ، أو أن المراد بـ (نَعْلَم) مفتوح (النون واللام (نُعْلِم) مضموم النون ومكسور اللام كما قاله الشيخ الملوي، ومما لا يقال : إنه من باب تنزيل المتكلم منزلة من لم يعلم، وإن ذكره في اليواقيت عن ابن العربي، ولا أظنه إلا مدسوساً على الشيخ.
فإن قيل : ظاهر الآية التعليل مع أن أفعال الله لا تعلل أجيب بجعل لامه للعاقبة والفائدة، فالآية أوهمت أن علمه مكتسب، وقد علمت جوابه، وأوهمت تعليل فعله وقد علمت جوابه فالكلام في مقامين وإن أوهم كلام الشارح خلافَهُ .
واعلم أنه كما لا يقال : علمه مكتسب، لا يقال : علمه ضروري ولا نظري ولا بديهي : أما الضروري فهو وإن كان يطلق على ما لا يتوقف على نظر واستدلال، وهو صحيح في حقه تعالى، لكن يطلق أيضاً على ما قارنته الضرورة، فيمتنع أن يُقال: علمه ضروري خوفاً من توهم هذا المعنى (۲)
وأما النظري فهو ما توقف على النظر والاستدلال، فهو مرادف للكسبي على تعريفه الأول فيمتنع أن يقال علمه نظري لاستلزامه الحدوث كما مر في الكسبي.
(۱) قوله : (ثم بعثناهم ... إلخ) في «الجلالين أن ( بعثناهم ) بمعنى أيقظناهم، وأحصى فعل ماض بمعنى ضبط. انتهى أجهوري. (۲) انظر بحث امتناع إطلاق العلم الضروري والبديهي وما شاكل عليه سُبحانه عند العلامة السنوسي في شرح الكبرى (ص ٢٦٠).
وأما البديهي فهو وإن كان يطلق على ما لا يتوقف على نظر واستدلال، فيكون مرادفاً للضروري على أحد معنييه، لكن يطلق أيضاً على العلم الحاصل للنفس بغتة، يقال : ( بده النفس الأمرُ ) إذا أتاها بغتةً، فيمتنع أن يقال علمه بديهي لإيهامه هذا المعنى .
The world is produced as originated (ḥādith), so knowledge of the world's origination (ḥudūth) is obtained by reflection (naẓar) and inference (istidlāl), so it is: :
And it is said: al-kasbī (acquired) is what the originated (ḥāditha) power (qudra) is attached to.
And according to this definition, it includes necessary (ḍarūrī) knowledge obtained by the senses, like knowledge obtained by sight or smell, contrary to the first definition; and on each of the two definitions, it is not said of Allah's knowledge: kasbī; because from that would follow the occurrence of originated things (ḥawādith) in His Essence (dhāt), and from that would also follow prior ignorance in His right, and that is impossible (muḥāl); and whatever (texts) have come that imply the acquisition of His knowledge, like His saying, Glory be to the Speaker: 'Then We awakened them that We might know which of the two parties was best at counting' [al-Kahf 18:12] is interpreted (mu'awwal) that the meaning—and Allah knows best—is that they might manifest to them the object of Our knowledge, or that the meaning of 'naʿlam' (with opened nūn and lām) is 'nuʿlim' (with damma on nūn and kasra on lām) as said by Shaykh al-Mallawī. And that it is not said: that it is of the type of placing the speaker in the position of one who does not know, even though it is mentioned in al-Yawāqīt from Ibn al-ʿArabī, I do not think it is anything but inserted falsely upon the Shaykh.
For if it is said: The apparent meaning of the verse is causation (taʿlīl) while the actions of Allah are not caused, the answer is by making its lām for consequence (ʿāqiba) and benefit (fāʾida). So the verse gave the illusion that His knowledge is acquired, and you have known its answer, and it gave the illusion that His action is caused, and you have known its answer. So the speech is about two stations, even if the commentator's speech appears otherwise.
And know that just as it is not said: His knowledge is acquired, so it is not said: His knowledge is necessary (ḍarūrī) nor theoretical (naẓarī) nor intuitive (badīhī): As for al-ḍarūrī: even though it is applied to what does not depend on reflection and inference, and that is correct with respect to His right, it is also applied to what is accompanied by necessity (ḍarūra), so it is impossible to say: His knowledge is necessary, for fear of implying this meaning.
And as for al-naẓarī: it is what depends on reflection and inference, so it is synonymous with al-kasbī according to its first definition, so it is impossible to say His knowledge is theoretical because it entails origination (ḥudūth) as passed in al-kasbī.
(1) His saying: 'Then We awakened them...' in al-Jalālayn: 'baʿathnāhum' means 'we awakened them', and 'aḥṣā' is a past verb meaning 'counted'. End of Ajuhrī. (2) See the discussion on the impossibility of applying necessary (ḍarūrī) and intuitive (badīhī) knowledge and the like to Him, may He be glorified, in ʿAllāma al-Sanūsī's Sharḥ al-Kubrā (p. 260).
And as for al-badīhī: even though it is applied to what does not depend on reflection and inference, so it is synonymous with al-ḍarūrī in one of its two senses, it is also applied to knowledge that comes to the soul suddenly; it is said: 'badaha nafsahu al-amru' when it comes to it suddenly, so it is impossible to say His knowledge is intuitive because it implies this meaning.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage classifies human knowledge into types and argues that none of these categories apply to Allah's knowledge. It begins by defining acquired (kasbī) knowledge: it is knowledge obtained through reflection and inference, or alternatively, knowledge attached to an originated power (qudra). This second definition includes necessary knowledge gained through the senses. The commentator stresses that on either definition, Allah's knowledge cannot be called kasbī, because that would entail originated accidents (ḥawādith) inhering in His Essence and would imply prior ignorance—both absurd. He then addresses a potential objection from the Qur'anic verse (18:12) that seems to imply acquisition of knowledge by Allah. He offers two interpretations: either the verse means to manifest the object of His eternal knowledge to creatures, or the verb is read as nuʿlim (to cause to know). He dismisses a third interpretation (placing the speaker in the position of one who does not know) as a forgery. The objection that the verse appears to give a cause for Allah's action is answered by interpreting the lām as indicating consequence, not causation. The commentator then extends the argument to other categories: necessary (ḍarūrī) knowledge cannot be applied because it also implies compulsion; theoretical (naẓarī) is synonymous with kasbī and thus entails origination; intuitive (badīhī) implies sudden acquisition, which is also impossible for Allah. The overall argument is that Allah's knowledge is eternal, uncaused, and transcends human epistemological categories.

Key Terms
علم كسبي
قدرة حادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'acquired' knowledge could be attributed to Allah metaphorically without implying imperfection.
Correction: The commentator argues that it necessarily entails originated accidents and prior ignorance, which are impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible to call Allah's knowledge 'kasbī'?
A) Because it would imply that Allah uses reflection and inference.
B) Because it would entail originated accidents in His Essence and prior ignorance.
C) Because the Qur'an explicitly denies it.
D) Because human knowledge is limited.

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge is not acquired because it is eternal and uncaused, and calling it kasbī would imply imperfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever mentally imagine that Allah 'finds out' or 'learns' about your actions after they occur? If that thought has crossed your mind, how does this lesson correct it?
?
What subtle ways have you attributed imperfection to Allah in your heart—perhaps by thinking His knowledge of future events is like your own speculation? Will you replace that with the certainty that His knowledge is eternal?
?
When you reflect on your own dependence on acquired knowledge, does it deepen your awe of Allah's complete self-sufficiency and eternal knowledge? How can you cultivate that awe today?

Rational Judgments
intermediate

Interpreting the Verse That Seems to Imply Acquired Knowledge
Commentator interprets the verse and answers the objection of causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic verse (18:12) is interpreted to avoid implying that Allah's knowledge is acquired, and how the issue of causation in divine actions is resolved.

Core Idea
Qur'anic expressions about divine knowledge are interpreted to protect Allah's transcendence.

The commentator addresses a potential objection from a Qur'anic verse that seems to suggest Allah acquires knowledge through an event. He offers two mainstream interpretations and dismisses a third as a forgery, while also addressing the issue of causation in divine actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the objection
The verse 'Then We awakened them that We might know...' (al-Kahf 18:12) appears to give a reason for an action, implying acquisition of knowledge. The commentator lists interpretations.
2
Understand the first two interpretations
First interpretation: 'that We might know' means 'that they might manifest to them the object of Our knowledge.' Second interpretation: read 'naʿlam' as 'nuʿlim' (to cause to know). Both avoid implying that Allah acquired knowledge.
3
Notice the dismissal of a third interpretation
A third interpretation (placing the speaker in the position of one who does not know) is dismissed as a forgery (madssūs).
4
Address the causation issue
The objection that the verse gives a cause (taʿlīl) for Allah's action is answered by interpreting the lām as indicating consequence (ʿāqiba) and benefit (fāʾida), not causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۱) قوله : (ثم بعثناهم ... إلخ) في «الجلالين أن ( بعثناهم ) بمعنى أيقظناهم، وأحصى فعل ماض بمعنى ضبط. انتهى أجهوري. (۲) انظر بحث امتناع إطلاق العلم الضروري والبديهي وما شاكل عليه سُبحانه عند العلامة السنوسي في شرح الكبرى (ص ٢٦٠).

The world is produced as originated (ḥādith), so knowledge of the world's origination (ḥudūth) is obtained by reflection (naẓar) and inference (istidlāl), so it is: :
And it is said: al-kasbī (acquired) is what the originated (ḥāditha) power (qudra) is attached to.

(1) His saying: 'Then We awakened them...' in al-Jalālayn: 'baʿathnāhum' means 'we awakened them', and 'aḥṣā' is a past verb meaning 'counted'. End of Ajuhrī. Commentary
This passage classifies human knowledge into types and argues that none of these categories apply to Allah's knowledge. It begins by defining acquired (kasbī) knowledge: it is knowledge obtained through reflection and inference, or alternatively, knowledge attached to an originated power (qudra). This second definition includes necessary knowledge gained through the senses. The commentator stresses that on either definition, Allah's knowledge cannot be called kasbī, because that would entail originated accidents (ḥawādith) inhering in His Essence and would imply prior ignorance—both absurd. He then addresses a potential objection from the Qur'anic verse (18:12) that seems to imply acquisition of knowledge by Allah. He offers two interpretations: either the verse means to manifest the object of His eternal knowledge to creatures, or the verb is read as nuʿlim (to cause to know). He dismisses a third interpretation (placing the speaker in the position of one who does not know) as a forgery. The objection that the verse appears to give a cause for Allah's action is answered by interpreting the lām as indicating consequence, not causation. The commentator then extends the argument to other categories: necessary (ḍarūrī) knowledge cannot be applied because it also implies compulsion; theoretical (naẓarī) is synonymous with kasbī and thus entails origination; intuitive (badīhī) implies sudden acquisition, which is also impossible for Allah. The overall argument is that Allah's knowledge is eternal, uncaused, and transcends human epistemological categories.

Key Terms
علم كسبي
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'that We might know' proves Allah's knowledge is acquired because it states a purpose tied to an event.
Correction: The verse is interpreted to mean the manifestation of knowledge to creatures or to cause them to know, not that Allah Himself acquires new knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the correct interpretation of 'that We might know' in the verse?
A) It shows Allah's knowledge is acquired through events.
B) It means that they might manifest the object of Allah's knowledge to others.
C) It means Allah was ignorant before.
D) It is an example of anthropomorphism.

Key Takeaway
Qur'anic verses that seem to imply acquisition or causation are interpreted to protect divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Qur'anic verse that seems to describe Allah in a way that feels human, does your heart waver for a moment? What does that reveal about your reliance on scholars and your own understanding?
?
The commentator rejects a forged interpretation as 'not to be said.' Have you ever accepted an explanation of a religious matter without verifying its source? What cost might that have on your faith?
?
The verse appears to give a cause for Allah's action, but the commentator says it is for consequence. When you think about why Allah does things, do you sometimes assume He acts for reasons we can fully grasp? How does this lesson correct that assumption?

Why Other Categories (Ḍarūrī, Naẓarī, Badīhī) Do Not Apply to Allah
Commentator refutes the application of ḍarūrī, naẓarī, and badīhī to Allah's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's knowledge cannot be described as necessary (ḍarūrī), theoretical (naẓarī), or intuitive (badīhī), even though some definitions might seem compatible.

Core Idea
Human epistemic categories carry connotations of limitation that cannot apply to Allah.

The commentator extends the argument beyond kasbī to other classifications of knowledge. He shows that terms like necessary (ḍarūrī), theoretical (naẓarī), and intuitive (badīhī) are also inappropriate, because they imply compulsion, origination, or sudden acquisition—all incompatible with divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the three categories
The commentator states that just as kasbī is inappropriate, so are ḍarūrī, naẓarī, and badīhī. Read each argument carefully.
2
Understand why ḍarūrī is rejected
Although ḍarūrī can mean independent of reflection, it also implies necessity (ḍarūra) and compulsion, which is not befitting Allah.
3
Understand why naẓarī is rejected
Naẓarī is synonymous with kasbī in its first definition (dependent on reflection), so it entails origination (ḥudūth) and is therefore impossible for Allah.
4
Understand why badīhī is rejected
Badīhī means knowledge that comes to the soul suddenly, implying acquisition at a moment, which cannot apply to Allah's eternal knowledge.

وقيل : الكسبي هو ما تعلقت به القدرة الحادثة.
وعلى هذا التعريف فيشمل العلم الضروري الحاصل بالحواس كالعلم الحاصل بالإبصار أو بالشم، بخلافه على التعريف الأول ؛ وعلى كل من التعريفين لا يُقال لعلم الله : كسبي ؛ لأنَّه يلزم منه قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى، ويلزم منه أيضاً سبق الجهل في حقه تعالى، وهو محال؛ وما ورد ممَّا يوهم اكتساب علمه تعالى كقوله جلَّ مِنْ قائل : وثُمَّ بَعَثْنَهُمْ لِنَعْلَمَ أَيُّ الْحِزْبَيْنِ أَحْصَى [الكهف: (۱۱۲) مؤول على أن المراد والله أعلم ليظهر لهم متعلَّق ،علمنا ، أو أن المراد بـ (نَعْلَم) مفتوح (النون واللام (نُعْلِم) مضموم النون ومكسور اللام كما قاله الشيخ الملوي، ومما لا يقال : إنه من باب تنزيل المتكلم منزلة من لم يعلم، وإن ذكره في اليواقيت عن ابن العربي، ولا أظنه إلا مدسوساً على الشيخ.
فإن قيل : ظاهر الآية التعليل مع أن أفعال الله لا تعلل أجيب بجعل لامه للعاقبة والفائدة، فالآية أوهمت أن علمه مكتسب، وقد علمت جوابه، وأوهمت تعليل فعله وقد علمت جوابه فالكلام في مقامين وإن أوهم كلام الشارح خلافَهُ .
واعلم أنه كما لا يقال : علمه مكتسب، لا يقال : علمه ضروري ولا نظري ولا بديهي : أما الضروري فهو وإن كان يطلق على ما لا يتوقف على نظر واستدلال، وهو صحيح في حقه تعالى، لكن يطلق أيضاً على ما قارنته الضرورة، فيمتنع أن يُقال: علمه ضروري خوفاً من توهم هذا المعنى (۲)
وأما النظري فهو ما توقف على النظر والاستدلال، فهو مرادف للكسبي على تعريفه الأول فيمتنع أن يقال علمه نظري لاستلزامه الحدوث كما مر في الكسبي.
(۱) قوله : (ثم بعثناهم ... إلخ) في «الجلالين أن ( بعثناهم ) بمعنى أيقظناهم، وأحصى فعل ماض بمعنى ضبط. انتهى أجهوري. (۲) انظر بحث امتناع إطلاق العلم الضروري والبديهي وما شاكل عليه سُبحانه عند العلامة السنوسي في شرح الكبرى (ص ٢٦٠).
وأما البديهي فهو وإن كان يطلق على ما لا يتوقف على نظر واستدلال، فيكون مرادفاً للضروري على أحد معنييه، لكن يطلق أيضاً على العلم الحاصل للنفس بغتة، يقال : ( بده النفس الأمرُ ) إذا أتاها بغتةً، فيمتنع أن يقال علمه بديهي لإيهامه هذا المعنى .
The world is produced as originated (ḥādith), so knowledge of the world's origination (ḥudūth) is obtained by reflection (naẓar) and inference (istidlāl), so it is: :
And it is said: al-kasbī (acquired) is what the originated (ḥāditha) power (qudra) is attached to.
And according to this definition, it includes necessary (ḍarūrī) knowledge obtained by the senses, like knowledge obtained by sight or smell, contrary to the first definition; and on each of the two definitions, it is not said of Allah's knowledge: kasbī; because from that would follow the occurrence of originated things (ḥawādith) in His Essence (dhāt), and from that would also follow prior ignorance in His right, and that is impossible (muḥāl); and whatever (texts) have come that imply the acquisition of His knowledge, like His saying, Glory be to the Speaker: 'Then We awakened them that We might know which of the two parties was best at counting' [al-Kahf 18:12] is interpreted (mu'awwal) that the meaning—and Allah knows best—is that they might manifest to them the object of Our knowledge, or that the meaning of 'naʿlam' (with opened nūn and lām) is 'nuʿlim' (with damma on nūn and kasra on lām) as said by Shaykh al-Mallawī. And that it is not said: that it is of the type of placing the speaker in the position of one who does not know, even though it is mentioned in al-Yawāqīt from Ibn al-ʿArabī, I do not think it is anything but inserted falsely upon the Shaykh.
For if it is said: The apparent meaning of the verse is causation (taʿlīl) while the actions of Allah are not caused, the answer is by making its lām for consequence (ʿāqiba) and benefit (fāʾida). So the verse gave the illusion that His knowledge is acquired, and you have known its answer, and it gave the illusion that His action is caused, and you have known its answer. So the speech is about two stations, even if the commentator's speech appears otherwise.
And know that just as it is not said: His knowledge is acquired, so it is not said: His knowledge is necessary (ḍarūrī) nor theoretical (naẓarī) nor intuitive (badīhī): As for al-ḍarūrī: even though it is applied to what does not depend on reflection and inference, and that is correct with respect to His right, it is also applied to what is accompanied by necessity (ḍarūra), so it is impossible to say: His knowledge is necessary, for fear of implying this meaning.
And as for al-naẓarī: it is what depends on reflection and inference, so it is synonymous with al-kasbī according to its first definition, so it is impossible to say His knowledge is theoretical because it entails origination (ḥudūth) as passed in al-kasbī.
(1) His saying: 'Then We awakened them...' in al-Jalālayn: 'baʿathnāhum' means 'we awakened them', and 'aḥṣā' is a past verb meaning 'counted'. End of Ajuhrī. (2) See the discussion on the impossibility of applying necessary (ḍarūrī) and intuitive (badīhī) knowledge and the like to Him, may He be glorified, in ʿAllāma al-Sanūsī's Sharḥ al-Kubrā (p. 260).
And as for al-badīhī: even though it is applied to what does not depend on reflection and inference, so it is synonymous with al-ḍarūrī in one of its two senses, it is also applied to knowledge that comes to the soul suddenly; it is said: 'badaha nafsahu al-amru' when it comes to it suddenly, so it is impossible to say His knowledge is intuitive because it implies this meaning.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage classifies human knowledge into types and argues that none of these categories apply to Allah's knowledge. It begins by defining acquired (kasbī) knowledge: it is knowledge obtained through reflection and inference, or alternatively, knowledge attached to an originated power (qudra). This second definition includes necessary knowledge gained through the senses. The commentator stresses that on either definition, Allah's knowledge cannot be called kasbī, because that would entail originated accidents (ḥawādith) inhering in His Essence and would imply prior ignorance—both absurd. He then addresses a potential objection from the Qur'anic verse (18:12) that seems to imply acquisition of knowledge by Allah. He offers two interpretations: either the verse means to manifest the object of His eternal knowledge to creatures, or the verb is read as nuʿlim (to cause to know). He dismisses a third interpretation (placing the speaker in the position of one who does not know) as a forgery. The objection that the verse appears to give a cause for Allah's action is answered by interpreting the lām as indicating consequence, not causation. The commentator then extends the argument to other categories: necessary (ḍarūrī) knowledge cannot be applied because it also implies compulsion; theoretical (naẓarī) is synonymous with kasbī and thus entails origination; intuitive (badīhī) implies sudden acquisition, which is also impossible for Allah. The overall argument is that Allah's knowledge is eternal, uncaused, and transcends human epistemological categories.

علم ضروري
علم نظري
علم بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since 'necessary knowledge' (ḍarūrī) means independent of reflection, it can be safely applied to Allah.
Correction: The term also implies compulsion (ḍarūra) and has connotations of necessity that are not befitting Allah. Therefore, it is avoided.

Checkpoint

B) Because it implies knowledge coming to the soul suddenly.
C) Because it is synonymous with kasbī.

Key Takeaway
All human epistemic categories imply temporality or limitation and thus cannot describe Allah's eternal, uncaused knowledge.

Defining the Permissible for Allah and Defending Against Redundancy
Definition of the permissible for Allah and defense against redundancy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defines the permissible (ja'iz) for Allah and defends it against the charge of tautology using grammatical analysis.

Core Idea
The permissible for Allah is defined by the possibility of action or inaction, not just a category name.

In this passage, the author completes the three rational judgments by defining the permissible. He not only gives a definition but also addresses a logical objection that the definition might be circular. By analyzing the Arabic grammar, he shows how the definition specifies actions, not just repeating the category.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a definition followed by a discussion.
The passage includes both the matn (verse) and its commentary.
2
Identify the definition of ja'iz
Find the definition: 'jā'iz fī ḥaqqihī mā amkanā ījādan wa i'dāman'. Understand that it means 'what we can conceive of as existent or non-existent'.
3
Understand the grammatical structure
Note the grammatical parsing: 'jā'iz' is a forward predicate, 'mā amkanā' is the delayed subject, and 'ījādan' and 'i'dāman' are tamyīz (specifications) transformed from the possessed.
This structure is key to the defense.
4
Follow the first objection and answer
The objection is that the definition is redundant because ja'iz and mumkin are synonyms. The answer: the tamyīz transforms the meaning to 'bringing into existence the possible and annihilating it', so the statement specifies actions.
The commentator's paraphrase 'doing every possible and leaving it' clarifies.
5
Consider the second objection and resolution
A second objection says that doing and leaving are themselves possible, so redundancy remains. The resolution: strong verbal differentiation (between the phrases) is sufficient to remove redundancy.
This shows a nuanced understanding of linguistic redundancy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يجوز في حقه تعالى] ٤٤ - وَجَائِزٌ فِي حَقِّهِ مَا أَمْكَنَا إيجاداً إِعْدَاماً كَرَزْقِهِ الغِنَى قوله: (وَجَائِزٌ فِي حَقِّهِ ... إلخ) لما فرغ من الكلام على الواجب والمستحيل، شرع يتكلّم على الجائز الذي هو ثاني الأقسام الثلاثة في الإجمال، وإنما أخره في التفصيل لما مرَّ آنفاً من طول الكلام عليه، و (جائز) خبر مقدم، و(ما أمكنا) مبتدأ مؤخَّر. وألف (أَمْكَنَا) للإطلاق. وإِيْجَاداً) و(اعْدَاماً) تمييزان محولان عن المضاف الذي كان مبتدأ في الأصل. والتقدير: وإيجاد ما أمكن وإعدامه جائز كل منهما في حقه تعالى. فإن قيل: إن هذا الإخبار لا فائدة له؛ لأنَّ الجائز هو الممكن، والممكن هو الجائز، فكأنه قال: الجائز جائز أو الممكن ممكن، أجيب بأن التمييز أعني (إيجاداً وإعداماً) يدفع عدم فائدته؛ لأنه تمييز محول عن عن المضاف الذي كان مبتدأ في الأصل، والتقدير وإيجاد الممكن وإعدامه جائز كلّ منهما في حقه تعالى كما تقدم، وقد أشار الشارح إلى هذا بقوله؛ أي: فِعْلُ كلِّ ممكن وتركُهُ، فإنَّه قدر ذلك أخذاً من قوله: إيجاداً) اعداماً وإلا فلا حاجة للتقدير مع التمييز واعترض بأن الفعل والترك كلُّ منهما ممكن، فيعود الإشكال وأجيب بأن المغايرة اللفظيَّةَ القوية كافية، إذ ربَّما
[What is permissible in His right] 44 - And permissible in His right is what we can [conceive of] in terms of bringing into existence and annihilating, like His granting wealth. His saying: (And permissible in His right... etc.) When he finished speaking about the necessary and the impossible, he began to speak about the permissible which is the second of the three divisions in summary, and he only delayed it in detail because of the preceding length of speech on it. And (jā'iz) is a forward predicate, and (mā amkanā) is a delayed subject. The alif in (amkanā) is for unrestrictedness. And (ījādan) and (i'dāman) are two tamyīz (specifications) transformed from the possessed which was originally the subject. The estimation: And bringing into existence and annihilating what is possible are each permissible in His right. If it is said: This report has no benefit because the permissible is the possible, and the possible is the permissible, so it is as if he said: the permissible is permissible, or the possible is possible. The answer is that the tamyīz, namely (ījādan and i'dāman), removes its lack of benefit, because it is a tamyīz transformed from the possessed which was originally the subject, so the estimation is: bringing into existence the possible and annihilating it are both permissible in His right as previously. And the commentator indicated this by his saying: i.e., doing every possible and leaving it. For he estimated that taking from his saying (ījādan and i'dāman), otherwise there would be no need for estimation with the tamyīz. And it was objected that doing and leaving are each possible, so the problem returns. And the answer is that strong verbal differentiation (al-mughāyara al-lafẓiyya al-qawiyya) is sufficient, because perhaps...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage defines the third category: what is permissible (jā'iz) for Allah. The author explains the grammatical structure of the verse: 'jā'iz' is a forward predicate, 'mā amkanā' is the delayed subject, and 'ījādan wa i'dāman' are tamyīz (specifications) transformed from the possessed. The estimation is that bringing the possible into existence and annihilating it are both permissible. An objection is raised: since the permissible and the possible are identical, the statement is redundant. The answer: the tamyīz avoids redundancy because it transforms the phrase to mean bringing into existence the possible and annihilating it, which are both permissible. This is further clarified by the commentator's paraphrase: 'doing every possible and leaving it'. A second objection: doing and leaving are themselves possible, so redundancy remains. The answer: strong verbal differentiation (between the phrases) is sufficient to remove the redundancy.
