إيجاد
إعدام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of ja'iz is a meaningless tautology because ja'iz and mumkin are the same.
Correction: The tamyīz transforms the statement to specify actions (bringing into existence and annihilating), making it informative.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the objection that the description of the permissible is redundant?
A) By saying the permissible is a different category
B) By explaining the tamyīz transforms the meaning to specific actions
C) By stating the objection is invalid
D) By citing a hadith

Key Takeaway
The permissible for Allah is defined by the logical possibility of action or inaction, and grammatical analysis resolves apparent redundancies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson defends the meaningfulness of describing Allah's actions with precise language. Reflect on how you speak about Allah—do you ever describe His actions in vague or careless terms, as if they were merely possibilities? What specific change in your speech will you make today to honor His perfection?
?
If both bringing into existence and annihilating are equally permissible for Allah, then every blessing you have and every loss you face are equally within His wise choice. Which loss have you not accepted as a deliberate act of His wisdom, and how will you acknowledge that today?
?
The objection that the definition is tautological was answered by showing that the grammatical structure adds real meaning. Is your own understanding of Allah's attributes merely a repetition of words, or have you let the precision of this lesson deepen your awe of Him? Commit to one way this insight will change your next moment of du'a.

The Definition of Ja'iz in God’s Right and the Tautology Objection
Al-Ajhari defines ja'iz and handles tautology objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ajhari defines ja'iz and defends it against the charge of tautology through grammatical analysis and the distinction between essence and action.

Core Idea
A good definition is never redundant—it specifies scope.

When the poet defines 'possible in God's right' as 'that which is possible for us to create or annihilate,' a critic might object that this merely says 'the possible is possible.' But by analyzing the grammar—especially the two tamyīz words—the commentator shows that the definition actually concerns actions, not essences, and thereby gains its utility. This lesson unpacks that grammatical insight and the first two rounds of objection and answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Arabic verse line and its literal translation without analyzing yet. Let the wording sink in.
Notice the words 'ījādan' and 'i‘dāman'.
2
Examine the grammatical analysis
The commentator explains that 'jā'iz' is a fronted predicate, 'mā amkanā' is a delayed subject, and 'ījādan' and 'i‘dāman' are tamyīz (discriminatives) that specify the actions. This transforms the meaning from 'the possible is possible' to 'bringing into existence and annihilating the possible are possible in God's right.'
The tamyīz is crucial here.
3
Follow the objection and first answer
The objection claims tautology. The first answer says the tamyīz removes the tautology by making the subject actions. Then a second objection arises: if both doing and leaving are possible, the tautology returns. The second answer invokes the strong verbal difference between words for 'doing' and 'leaving' versus 'possible' and 'contingent.'
This shows the layered logical defense.

٤٤ - وَجَائِزٌ فِي حَقِّهِ مَا أَمْكَنَا إيجاداً إِعْدَاماً كَرَزْقِهِ الغِنَى قوله: (وَجَائِزٌ فِي حَقِّهِ ... إلخ) (۲) لما فرغ من الكلام على الواجب والمستحيل، شرع يتكلم على الجائز الذي هو ثاني الأقسام الثلاثة في الإجمال، وإنما أخره في التفصيل لما مرَّ آنفاً من طول الكلام عليه، و (جائز) خبر مقدم، و(ما أمكنا) مبتدأ مؤخَّر . وألف (أَمْكَنَا) للإطلاق. و(إيجاداً) و(إعداماً) تمييزان محولان عن المضاف الذي كان مبتدأ في الأصل. والتقدير : وإيجاد ما أمكن وإعدامه جائز كل منهما في حقه تعالى. فإن قيل : إن هذا الإخبار لا فائدة له؛ لأنَّ الجائز هو الممكن، والممكن هو الجائز، فكأنه قال : الجائز جائز أو الممكن ممكن ، أجيب بأن التمييز أعني (إيجاداً وإعداماً) يدفع عدم فائدته ؛ لأنه تمييز محول عن المضاف الذي كان مبتدأ في الأصل، والتقدير وإيجاد الممكن وإعدامه جائز كلّ منهما في حقه تعالى كما تقدم، وقد أشار الشارح إلى هذا بقوله؛ أي: فِعْلُ كلِّ ممكن وتركُهُ ، فإنَّه قدر ذلك أخذاً من قوله : إيجاداً إعداماً وإلا فلا حاجة للتقدير مع التمييز واعترض بأن الفعل والترك كلُّ منهما ممكن، فيعود الإشكال وأجيب بأن المغايرة اللفظيَّةَ القوية كافية، إذ ربَّما يتوهم أن صفة الفعل أو الترك الوجوب، بخلاف الجائز والممكن، فإن مغايرتهما غير قوية، ويدفع أصل الإشكال بأن المبتدأ هو الممكن في ذاته، والخبر هو الجائز في حقه تعالى، فهو مقيد بكونه في حقه تعالى فكأنَّه قال : الممكن في ذاته جائز في حقه تعالى(۱)، خلافاً للمعتزلة في قولهم بوجوب بعض الممكنات عليه تعالى، فإنهم قالوا : بوجوب الصلاح والأصلح عليه تعالى(۲)، وخلافاً للبراهمة في قولهم: باستحالة (۳) إرسالِ الرُّسل مع أنَّه من الممكنات وهذه فائدة معتبرة، أفاده العلامة الأمير والعلامة الشَّنواني. قوله : كَرَزْقِهِ الغِنَى) هذا مثال لفعل الممكن، ومثال تركه عدم رزقه إيَّاهُ، والرزقُ بفتح الراء مصدر ; وأما بالكسر فاسمٌ للمرزوق به والضمير عائد على الله، والإضافة في (رزقه) من إضافة المصدر لفاعله، والمفعول الأول محذوف، و(الغنى) مفعوله الثاني، والتقدير : كرزق الله العبد الغنى وهو بالكسر وبالقصر ضد الفقر، فهو كثرة الأموال، وأما بالكسر وبالمد : فهو إنشاد الشعر، وبالمد مع الفتح: النفعُ، وأما بالفتح مع القصر فلم يُسمع .
44. And possible in His right is that which is possible for us, in terms of bringing into existence and annihilating, like His giving wealth. His statement: (And possible in His right ... etc.) (2) When he finished speaking about the necessary and the impossible, he began to speak about the possible, which is the second of the three divisions in summary, and he only postponed it in detail due to the length of the discussion earlier. And (possible) is a predicate preceding, and (that which is possible for us) is a subject delayed. The alif of (amkana) is for absoluteness. And (bringing into existence) and (annihilating) are two discriminatives transformed from the genitive which was the subject originally. The estimation: And the bringing into existence of that which is possible and its annihilation, each of them is possible in His right. For if it is said: This information has no benefit, because the possible is the contingent, and the contingent is the possible, so it is as if he said: The possible is possible or the contingent is contingent. The answer is that the discriminative, namely (bringing into existence and annihilating), removes its lack of benefit, because it is a discriminative transformed from the genitive which was the subject originally, and the estimation is: The bringing into existence of the contingent and its annihilation, each of them is possible in His right as mentioned, and the commentator indicated this by his saying: i.e., the doing of every contingent and its leaving, for he estimated that taken from his saying: bringing into existence and annihilating, otherwise there is no need for estimation with the discriminative. And an objection was made: the doing and leaving, each of them is contingent, so the difficulty returns. And the answer was: The strong verbal difference is sufficient, since perhaps one might imagine that the attribute of doing or leaving is necessity, unlike the possible and the contingent, for their difference is not strong. And the original difficulty is repelled by the fact that the subject is the contingent in itself, and the predicate is the possible in His right, so it is restricted by being in His right, so it is as if he said: The contingent in itself is possible in His right, contrary to the Mu'tazila in their saying that some contingents are necessary upon Him, for they said: It is obligatory to do what is good and best (al-salāh wa al-aslah), and contrary to the Barāhima in their saying that sending messengers is impossible, while it is among contingent things. And this is a significant benefit, conveyed by al-'Allāma al-Amir and al-'Allāma al-Shanwānī. His statement: Like His giving wealth is an example of doing the contingent, and an example of leaving it is not giving it to him. And 'al-rizq' with fatḥa on the rā' is a verbal noun; but with kasra it is a noun for what is provided. The pronoun refers to God, and the genitive in (rizqihi) is from the verbal noun's annexation to its agent, and the first object is omitted, and (al-ghinā) is its second object. The estimation: Like God's giving the servant wealth (al-ghinā), which with kasra and shortening is the opposite of poverty, so it is abundance of wealth. But with kasra and lengthening, it is the chanting of poetry. And with lengthening and fatḥa, it is benefit. As for with fatḥa and shortening, it has not been heard.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition and defence of the concept of ja'iz (possible/permissible) in relation to God. It begins by citing the verse (line 44 of the poem): 'And possible in His right is that which is possible for us, in terms of bringing into existence and annihilating, like His giving wealth.' The commentator then explains the grammar: 'jā’iz' is a predicate in front, and 'mā amkanā' is a delayed subject; the alif of 'amkanā' indicates absoluteness; 'ījādan' and 'i‘dāman' are two discriminatives (tamyīz) transformed from the annexed phrase that was originally the subject. The intended meaning is: bringing into existence and annihilating each possible thing are both possible in God's right. The commentator then raises an objection: this statement seems tautological because 'jā’iz' and 'mumkin' are synonyms, so it is like saying 'the possible is possible'. The answer is that the discriminatives ('ījādan' and 'i‘dāman') prevent tautology by specifying that the subject is the action (doing or leaving) of the contingent, not the contingent itself. The commentator further explains that the strong verbal difference between 'doing' and 'leaving' is enough to clarify the benefit. He then refutes the original difficulty by stating that the subject is 'the contingent in itself' and the predicate is 'possible in His right', so it affirms contingency in essence and permissibility in relation to God, thus denying any necessity or impossibility. This refutes two groups: the Mu'tazila, who claim that some acts (like what is good and best) are obligatory upon God, and the Barāhima (a group of Indian Brahmins), who claim that sending messengers is impossible. Both claims are false because they contradict the principle that all contingents are possible in God's right. The passage ends with an explanation of the example 'like His giving wealth', clarifying the word 'rizq' and 'ghinā' in various forms. This lesson shows the careful logical work in defining a kalam term and defending its usefulness.

Key Terms
الجائز
الممكن
التمييز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition 'ja'iz in God's right is that which is possible for us' is tautological and useless.
Correction: The definition is not tautological because it specifies actions through tamyīz and addresses the scope of God's right, refuting those who claim necessity or impossibility in some actions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what removes the benefitlessness of the definition?
A) The alif of absoluteness.
B) The discriminatives (tamyīz) 'bringing into existence' and 'annihilating'.
C) The example of giving wealth.
D) The distinction between possible and contingent.

Key Takeaway
The definition of ja'iz gains meaning by specifying actions through tamyīz, countering the charge of tautology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a nuanced theological distinction as 'just semantics' without considering the spiritual precision it protects? What would it mean for your faith if you stopped dismissing such distinctions?
?
This passage shows that even the most basic definitions can hide profound meaning when examined carefully. Is your belief in God's attributes built on such careful examination, or on comfortable slogans? What is one attribute of God you have not thought about with this level of precision?
?
The scholars in this passage defended a definition with logical rigor against repeated objections. Are you willing to defend the truths of your faith with that same careful reasoning, even when it feels tedious? What expense might that require of you?

The Benefit of Defining Ja'iz: Refuting Mu'tazila and Barāhima
Al-Ajhari defends the utility of the ja'iz definition by refuting Mu'tazila and Barāhima.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of ja'iz serves to refute the Mu'tazila claim of obligatory acts and the Barāhima claim of impossible acts, thereby establishing God's absolute freedom.

Core Idea
Defining ja'iz protects divine omnipotence from unwarranted restrictions.

After establishing the definition, the commentator reveals its ultimate benefit: it contradicts erroneous schools that impose necessity or impossibility on certain actions of God. The Mu'tazila argue that God must do what is best (al-salāh wa al-aslah), while the Barāhima claim sending messengers is impossible. The definition affirms that God is free to act or not, and nothing is obligatory or impossible in His right.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the ultimate solution
Notice how the commentator resolves the original difficulty by stating that the subject is 'the contingent in itself' and the predicate is 'possible in His right'. This distinction is key.
2
Identify the two refuted groups
The passage mentions the Mu'tazila (who claim some acts are obligatory) and the Barāhima (who claim sending messengers is impossible). Understand their positions.
These are historical theological opponents.
3
Reflect on the benefit
The passage explicitly calls this a 'significant benefit' because it shows how a precise definition can correct false beliefs.
4
Examine the example
The example of God giving wealth illustrates a possible action; the opposite (not giving) is also possible. This concretely shows the range of God's freedom.

Distinguishing the Possible from the Permissible for God
The commentator distinguishes possible from permissible and refutes two sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the crucial distinction between 'mumkin' (possible in itself) and 'ja'iz' (permissible for God) and how this refutes the Mu'tazila and Barahima.

Core Idea
What is possible in itself is not automatically permissible; only God's permission makes it so.

This passage addresses a subtle but important distinction: just because something is logically possible (mumkin) does not mean it is automatically permissible (ja'iz) for God to do. The commentator clarifies that in the creedal statement, the predicate is qualified by 'fi haqqihi ta'ala'—meaning God's permission is the deciding factor. This understanding refutes two groups: the Mu'tazila who claim God must do what is best, and the Barahima who claim God cannot send messengers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to identify the potential confusion about ja'iz and mumkin. Then read the translation.
Notice how the commentator first states the confusion, then resolves it.
2
Understand the distinction
The commentator explains that 'mumkin' describes a thing's essence, while 'ja'iz' is a ruling about God's action regarding it. The predicate is restricted to God's right.
Key: 'al-mumkin fi dhatihi ja'iz fi haqqihi ta'ala'.
3
Recognize the two refutations
The passage explicitly refutes the Mu'tazila (obligation of al-salah wa al-aslah) and the Barahima (impossibility of sending messengers). Understand why each group's claim contradicts the distinction.
Both groups impose obligation or impossibility on God, which is denied by the ja'iz characterization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتوهم أن صفة الفعل أو الترك الوجوب، بخلاف الجائز والممكن، فإن مغايرتهما غير قوية، ويدفع أصل الإشكال بأن المبتدأ هو الممكن في ذاته، والخبر هو الجائز في حقه تعالى، فهو مقيد بكونه في حقه تعالى فكأنَّه قال : الممكن في ذاته جائز في حقه تعالى(۱)، خلافاً للمعتزلة في قولهم بوجوب بعض الممكنات عليه تعالى، فإنهم قالوا : بوجوب الصلاح والأصلح عليه تعالى(۲)، وخلافاً للبراهمة في قولهم: باستحالة (۳) إرسالِ الرُّسل مع أنَّه من الممكنات وهذه فائدة معتبرة، أفاده العلامة الأمير والعلامة الشَّنواني.
قوله : كَرَزْقِهِ الغِنَى) هذا مثال لفعل الممكن، ومثال تركه عدم رزقه إيَّاهُ، والرزقُ بفتح الراء مصدر ؛ وأما بالكسر فاسمٌ للمرزوق به والضمير عائد على الله، والإضافة في (رزقه) من إضافة المصدر لفاعله، والمفعول الأول محذوف، و(الغنى) مفعوله الثاني، والتقدير : كرزق الله العبد الغنى وهو بالكسر وبالقصر ضد الفقر، فهو كثرة الأموال، وأما بالكسر وبالمد : فهو إنشاد الشعر، وبالمد مع الفتح: النفعُ، وأما بالفتح مع القصر فلم يُسمع .
One might imagine that the attribute of doing or leaving is obligation, unlike the permissible and the possible, for their distinctness is not strong. The root of the problem is repelled by [noting] that the subject is the possible in itself, and the predicate is the permissible in His right, so it is restricted by being in His right, as if He said: the possible in itself is permissible in His right (1), contrary to the Mu'tazila in their statement that some possible things are obligatory upon Him, for they said: obligation of what is good and most good upon Him (2), and contrary to the Barahima in their statement: impossibility of sending messengers (3) even though it is among possible things, and this is a weighty benefit, as stated by Allamah al-Amir and Allamah al-Shanwani.
His statement: "like His providing wealth" is an example of a possible act, and its leaving is His not providing it to him. "Providing" (al-rizq) with fatha on the ra' is a masdar; with kasra it is a noun for the provided thing. The pronoun refers to God. The idafa in "His providing" is the idafa of the masdar to its agent, the first object is omitted, and "wealth" is its second object. The تقدير (underlying structure) is: like God providing the servant with wealth. "al-ghina" with kasra and qasr is the opposite of poverty, meaning abundance of wealth; with kasra and madd it means reciting poetry; with fatha and madd it means benefit; with fatha and qasr it is unattested.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. The commentator first addresses a potential misunderstanding: one might think that an act's attribute of being done or left (by God) entails obligation, confusing wajib (obligatory) with ja'iz (permissible) and mumkin (possible). He explains that the distinction between ja'iz and mumkin is subtle but real. The problem is resolved by noting that in the phrase "al-mumkin fi dhatihi ja'iz fi haqqihi ta'ala" (the possible in itself is permissible in God's right), the predicate is qualified by God's right, so the possible in itself is not automatically permissible for God; it is specifically permissible in His right. This refutes two groups: the Mu'tazila, who claim that God must do what is best (obligation of al-salah wa al-aslah), and the Barahima, who claim that sending messengers is impossible, even though it is possible. The commentator then provides a linguistic explanation of the example "ka-rizqihi al-ghina" (like His providing wealth). He parses the grammar: "al-rizq" as a masdar with fatha, the pronoun refers to God, the first object is implicit, and "al-ghina" is the second object. He clarifies the meaning and vocalization of "al-ghina" (wealth vs. psalmody vs. benefit). This lesson combines a key doctrinal point—denying any obligation or impossibility upon God—with careful grammatical analysis of the supporting example.

Key Terms
الجائز
الممكن
الوجوب
المعتزلة
البراهمة
الصلاح والأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'ja'iz' and 'mumkin' are synonyms.
Correction: The commentator shows they are distinct: mumkin refers to the essence of a thing, ja'iz refers to divine permission.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes 'mumkin' (possible) from 'ja'iz' (permissible) in this passage?
A) Mumkin is what God does, ja'iz is what He does not
B) Mumkin pertains to the essence of a thing, ja'iz pertains to its divine permission
C) Mumkin is necessary, ja'iz is contingent
D) They are synonyms

Key Takeaway
An act is not obligatory upon God; the possible in itself becomes permissible only by God's right.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is not obligated to do what is best for you, how does that challenge your expectation that life should always go your way? Will you still trust Him when it doesn't?
?
The Barahima denied that God could send messengers because they limited His power. Where in your life do you subconsciously limit what God can do? What would it take for you to truly believe that nothing is impossible for Him?
?
This lesson says that what is possible in itself becomes permissible only by God's right. Are you living as if your own possibilities are automatically permissible, without seeking His permission and guidance? What is one area where you need to submit your 'possible' to His 'permissible'?

The Grammar of 'His Providing Wealth': A Linguistic Lesson
The commentator parses the grammar of the example 'ka-rizqihi al-ghina' and explains the meanings of 'al-ghina'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical structure of the phrase 'ka-rizqihi al-ghina' and the meaning of 'al-ghina' with different vowellings.

Core Idea
Even theological examples require precise grammatical analysis.

After establishing the doctrinal point, the commentator provides a grammatical breakdown of the example 'ka-rizqihi al-ghina' (like His providing wealth). This lesson examines the parsing: al-rizq as a masdar, the hidden first object, and the second object al-ghina. Additionally, the word al-ghina has multiple meanings depending on its voweling and length.

Guided Path
1
Read the grammatical explanation
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the parsing of 'ka-rizqihi al-ghina'. Note the different vowellings of 'rizq' and 'ghina'.
The commentator explains that 'rizq' with fatha is a masdar, with kasra is a noun.
2
Understand the different forms of 'al-ghina'
The commentator lists four possible vowelling/length combinations for 'ghina' and their meanings. Memorize them.
Only the form with kasra and qasr means 'wealth'.
3
Appreciate the precision required
Reflect on why such grammatical detail matters in theological writing: to avoid ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتوهم أن صفة الفعل أو الترك الوجوب، بخلاف الجائز والممكن، فإن مغايرتهما غير قوية، ويدفع أصل الإشكال بأن المبتدأ هو الممكن في ذاته، والخبر هو الجائز في حقه تعالى، فهو مقيد بكونه في حقه تعالى فكأنَّه قال : الممكن في ذاته جائز في حقه تعالى(۱)، خلافاً للمعتزلة في قولهم بوجوب بعض الممكنات عليه تعالى، فإنهم قالوا : بوجوب الصلاح والأصلح عليه تعالى(۲)، وخلافاً للبراهمة في قولهم: باستحالة (۳) إرسالِ الرُّسل مع أنَّه من الممكنات وهذه فائدة معتبرة، أفاده العلامة الأمير والعلامة الشَّنواني.

?

The Story of Shaykh Afif al-Din: The Principle of Not Questioning God's Decree
A story and argument against objecting to God's decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari theology rejects the idea that God is obligated to reward obedience and punish disobedience, and how the story of Shaykh Afif al-Din illustrates this principle.

Core Idea
Obedience and disobedience are signs, not causes of reward or punishment.

This lesson presents a remarkable story from Ash'ari tradition: a pious shaykh, disturbed by the massacre of innocents, questions God's wisdom – only to be corrected by a dream. Through this narrative, we are introduced to a foundational doctrine: God's actions are not subject to external obligation. Obedience does not automatically necessitate reward, nor disobedience punishment; they are merely indicators of what God may will. This passage challenges us to think deeply about divine sovereignty and human accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the story and notice the shaykh’s objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the shaykh's emotional reaction and his question to God about the suffering of innocents.
The shaykh's objection is natural, but the answer is surprising.
2
Examine the poem’s advice
The dream presents a poem that tells the shaykh to leave objection and not ask God about His actions. Reflect on the meaning of 'whoever dives into the deep sea perishes.'
The poem emphasizes human limitation in understanding divine wisdom.
3
Understand the theological conclusion
The author explains that God is not benefited by obedience nor harmed by disobedience. Obedience and disobedience do not necessitate reward or punishment; they are only signs that indicate what God may will.
This is a key Ash'ari principle: no inherent necessity.
4
Reflect on the hypothetical reversal
The passage adds that even if God reversed the signs—rewarding disobedience and punishing obedience—it would be good from Him. This drives home the point that God is not constrained.
This hypothetical is meant to highlight absolute divine sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكي عن الشيخ عفيف الدين الزاهد أنَّه كان بمصر، فبلغه ما وقع ببغداد من القتل، فإنَّه وقع السيف فيها أربعين يوماً فقُتل ألف ألف، وعَلَّقت النصارى المصاحف في أعناق الكلاب، وجعلوا المساجد كنائس، وألقوا كتب الأئمة في الدجلة، حتى صارت كالجسر يمرُّ الخيلُ عليها ، فأنكر الشيخ عفيف الدين ذلك، وقال: يا رَبِّ ؛ كيف هذا وفيهم الأطفال، ومن لا ذنب له؟ فرأى في النوم رجلاً ومعه كتاب فأخذه فإذا فيه : [من المتقارب] دَعِ الإِعْتِرَاضَ فَمَا الْأَمْرُ لَكْ وَلَا الحُكْمُ فِي حَرَكَاتِ الفَلَكْ وَلَا تَسْأَلِ الله عَنْ فِعْلِهِ فَمَنْ خَاصَ لُجَّةَ بَحْرِ هَلَكْ وبالجملة فهو سبحانه وتعالى لا تنفعه طاعة ولا تضره معصية، والكل بخلقه، فليست الطاعة مستلزمة للثواب وليست المعصية مستلزمة للعقاب، وإنَّما هما أمارتان تدلان على الثواب لمن أطاع والعقاب لمن عصى، حتى لو عكس دلالتهما بأنْ قال : مَنْ أطاعني عذبته ومَنْ عصاني أثبته لكان ذلك منه حسناً، فلا حرج عليه، لا يُسأل عما يفعل.
It was narrated about al-Shaykh Afif al-Din al-Zahid that he was in Egypt, and news reached him of what happened in Baghdad of killing, for the sword fell upon it for forty days and a million were killed, and the Christians hung the Qur'ans around the necks of dogs, and made mosques into churches, and threw the books of the imams into the Tigris, until it became like a bridge over which horses would pass. So al-Shaykh Afif al-Din objected to that and said: 'O Lord, how is this when among them are children and those who have no sin?' Then he saw in a dream a man with a book, and he took it, and in it was: [al-Mutaqarib] 'Leave objection, for the matter is not yours, nor is the judgment in the movements of the sphere. Do not ask God about His action, for whoever dives into the deep sea perishes.' In short, He, glorified and exalted, is not benefited by obedience nor harmed by disobedience, and all is by His creation. So obedience does not necessitate reward, and disobedience does not necessitate punishment. They are only signs that indicate reward for the one who obeys and punishment for the one who disobeys, even if He reversed their indications by saying: 'Whoever obeys Me I will punish, and whoever disobeys Me I will reward,' that would be good from Him. There is no constraint upon Him. He is not questioned about what He does.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage combines an anecdote and a theological argument. It begins with a story about Shaykh Afif al-Din al-Zahid, who was distressed by the massacres and desecration in Baghdad and questioned why God allowed the suffering of innocents. In a dream, he receives a poem advising him to abandon objection, as human understanding cannot grasp the divine wisdom in the movements of the heavens. The author then draws the theological principle: God is not benefited by human obedience nor harmed by disobedience; all actions are created by Him. Obedience and disobedience do not necessitate reward or punishment in themselves; they are merely indicators (amarat) of what God may will. The point is driven home with a hypothetical: even if God said He would punish those who obey and reward those who disobey, that would be entirely fitting and good from Him, because He is not constrained by any external obligation. The passage establishes the Ash'ari principle of divine sovereignty: God acts as He wills, and no one can hold Him accountable.

Key Terms
الطاعة
المعصية
الثواب
العقاب
الأمارة
عكس الدلالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari view makes God arbitrary or unjust.
Correction: In Ash'ari theology, God's actions are not bound by external obligations; His actions are inherently just because He defines justice. The signs indicate but do not necessitate.

Checkpoint
What is the main theological principle illustrated by the story and the poem in this passage?
A) God always rewards the obedient and punishes the disobedient.
B) Obedience and disobedience are merely signs that do not necessitate reward or punishment.
C) God is obligated to do what is best for His creation.
D) Humans cannot understand any of God's actions.

Key Takeaway
God is not constrained by obligation; His actions are beyond human questioning, and obedience/disobedience are signs, not necessary causes of reward/punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see suffering that seems unfair—especially to the innocent—do you ever find yourself questioning God's wisdom or justice? How does this passage's command to 'leave objection' challenge you to submit to His decree even when you cannot understand it?
?
If obedience to God is 'only a sign' and not a guarantee of reward, what does that mean for the motivation behind your acts of worship? Are you willing to obey even if you were told that obedience might not lead to Paradise? What would that reveal about your true love for God?
?
This passage says God could reverse the signs—punishing the obedient and rewarding the disobedient—and it would still be good from Him. Can you sincerely accept that standard of justice? What in your heart resists this, and how can you train yourself to trust God's absolute sovereignty over your own sense of fairness?

Definitions of Good and Evil: Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna Compared
The author presents two terminological definitions of good and evil and endorses a third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna define al-qabih (evil) and al-hasan (good), and why Imam al-Haramayn's view is considered the best.

Core Idea
Good and evil are defined by reward and punishment, but scholars differ on the middle categories.

This passage clarifies two technical terminologies used in kalam: how the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna define moral categories. Understanding these definitions is crucial because they affect how legal and theological rulings are classified. The author presents both views and then endorses the nuanced perspective of Imam al-Haramayn.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice how the author introduces the terms al-qabih and al-hasan.
Pay attention to the phrase 'اصطلحت المعتزلة' — it signals a technical definition.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila definition
Find the sentence defining al-qabih for the Mu'tazila. What are the two aspects? Then find what they include in al-hasan.
Notice that for them, al-qabih is specifically the forbidden (haram).
3
Identify the Ahl al-Sunna definition
Read the sentence about Ahl al-Sunna. What do they say? Notice the phrase 'المنهي مطلقاً قبيح'.
The word 'المنهي' means what is prohibited.
4
Understand Imam al-Haramayn's view
The author says 'الأحسن ما قاله إمام الحرمين'. Read his view. What does it say about the disliked and contrary to the better?
This is the preferred opinion in Ash'ari theology.
5
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on why these distinctions matter. How do they affect the classification of actions beyond the five legal categories?
Think about how Ash'aris avoid calling disliked actions 'evil'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الشَّرِّ وَالخَيْرِ) اعلمُ أنَّهم يعبرون عن الأول بالقبيح، وعن الثاني بالحسن، واصطلحتِ المعتزلة على أنّ القبيح ما يكون متعلق الذم في العاجل؛ أي: الدنيا والعقاب في الآجل ؛ أي: الآخرة، فيكون القبيح هو الحرام بخصوصه، وعلى الحسن ما لا يكون متعلَّق الذم والعقاب، فيشمل الواجب والمندوب والمباح والمكروة وخلاف الأولى إن لم ندخله في المكروه، فهذه الأمور كلُّها حسنةٌ عندهم . واصطلح كثير من أهل السنة على أنّ المنهي * مطلقاً قبيح، والأحسنُ ما قاله إمام الحرمين : أنَّ المكروة ومنه خلافُ الأولى ليس حسناً ولا قبيحاً .
His saying: (evil and good): Know that they refer to the first as 'al-qabih' (ugly/evil) and to the second as 'al-hasan' (beautiful/good). The Mu'tazila have terminologically agreed that al-qabih is that which is the object of blame in the immediate (i.e., the world) and punishment in the future (i.e., the afterlife); thus al-qabih is specifically the forbidden. And al-hasan is that which is not the object of blame and punishment, so it includes the obligatory, recommended, permissible, disliked, and contrary to the better if we do not include it in the disliked; so these things are all good according to them. And many of Ahl al-Sunna have terminologically agreed that what is prohibited absolutely is qabih, and the best is what Imam al-Haramayn said: that the disliked and including contrary to the better is neither good nor evil.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a terminological clarification about the words 'sharr' (evil) and 'khayr' (good), which are often used interchangeably with 'qabih' and 'hasan' respectively. The author explains two different terminologies: that of the Mu'tazila and that of many Ahl al-Sunna, along with the preferred opinion of Imam al-Haramayn. For the Mu'tazila, qabih is defined as an act that incurs blame in this world and punishment in the Hereafter, which means they restrict it to the specifically forbidden (haram). Hasan is anything not subject to blame or punishment, thus including obligatory, recommended, permissible, disliked, and even 'contrary to the better' (khilaf al-awla) if not considered part of the disliked. For many Ahl al-Sunna, any forbidden act is absolutely qabih. But the author endorses Imam al-Haramayn's view: actions like the disliked and 'contrary to the better' are neither hasan nor qabih—they occupy a middle category. This shows the nuanced classification of moral categories in Ash'ari thought.

Key Terms
القبيح
الحسن
المعتزلة
أهل السنة
إمام الحرمين
المكروه
خلاف الأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'qabih' and 'hasan' are purely moral terms, independent of reward and punishment.
Correction: In this passage, these terms are defined by their relation to blame and punishment: qabih is blameworthy/punishable, hasan is not.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what is included in 'hasan'?
A) Only the obligatory and recommended.
B) Only the permissible.
C) Everything that is not subject to blame and punishment, including the recommended, permissible, and even the disliked.
D) Only the obligatory and permissible.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila define qabih as only the forbidden, while Ahl al-Sunna treat all prohibitions as qabih; Imam al-Haramayn places disliked actions in a neutral category.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever condemned someone's action as sinful simply because it felt wrong to you, without checking whether it is actually prohibited? How does this lesson's distinction between disliked and forbidden challenge your attitudes toward others?
?
The Mu'tazila define 'good' as what does not bring blame or punishment. In your daily life, do you secretly define 'good' as what brings you comfort or social approval? Name one action you will do today purely for Allah's sake, even if it earns no praise.
?
Imam al-Haramayn places disliked actions in a neutral category. Can you think of an action you often avoid because you think it is bad, but which this lesson suggests might be neither good nor evil? How does that change your willingness to refrain from judging others?

The Two Stations of Ru'ya: Permissibility and Occurrence
Distinguishes two stations: permissibility and occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct theological stations regarding the vision of Allah in the afterlife: its rational permissibility and its actual occurrence.

Core Idea
The vision of Allah has two separate questions: is it possible? and will it happen?

Before discussing the nature of the vision of Allah (ru'ya), scholars first distinguish between the question of whether it is rationally permissible (jawaz) and the question of whether it will actually occur (wuqu'). This passage, drawing on the commentary of al-Sa'd al-Taftazani, clarifies that the author’s discussion focuses on the first station.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the basic meaning.
Notice the phrase 'two stations'.
2
Identify the two stations
Look for the words 'جواز' (permissibility) and 'وقوع' (occurrence). These are the two stations.
The passage explicitly names them.
3
Note the author's focus
The commentator states that the author's apparent intent is the first station (permissibility). Understand why this distinction matters.
This sets the stage for further discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن هنا مقامين كما يُستفاد من كلام السعد في شرح المقاصد: أحدهما في جواز الرؤية، وثانيهما في وقوعها والمتبادر من كلام المصنف المقام الأول كما هو قضية مرجع الضمير. قوله : (بِالأَبْصَارِ) ظاهره أن الرؤية بالحَدَقِ فقط، وهو أحد أقوال ثلاثة. ثانيها : أنها بجميع الوجوه، لظاهر قوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَهُ الله إِلَى رَبَّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ ) القيامة : 22-23]. ثالثها: أنها بكل جزء من أجزاء البدن كما نُقل عن أبي يزيد البسطامي.
And the conclusion is that there are two stations, as understood from the words of al-Sa'd in Sharh al-Maqasid: one regarding the permissibility of seeing [Allah], and the second regarding its occurrence. The apparent meaning from the author's words is the first station, as indicated by the referent of the pronoun. His saying: 'with the eyes' (bi-l-absar) apparently means that seeing is only with the pupils, which is one of three opinions. The second: that it is with the entire face, due to the apparent meaning of His saying: 'Faces that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord' (75:22-23). The third: that it is with every part of the body, as narrated from Abu Yazid al-Bistami.

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition. It first introduces two stations (maqamayn) concerning the vision of Allah (ru'ya) in the afterlife: one on the possibility (jawaz) and the other on the actual occurrence (wuqu'). The author clarifies that the speaker's (al-musannif) apparent intent is the first station. Then it explains the phrase 'bil-absar' (with the eyes), noting that it appears to mean seeing only with the pupils, but this is just one of three positions. The second position holds that it is with the entire face, citing the Quranic verse about faces looking at their Lord. The third position, attributed to Abu Yazid al-Bistami, holds that it is with every part of the body. This shows the range of interpretation among scholars regarding the nature of the vision.

The Three Interpretations of 'Bill-Absar'
Enumerates three opinions on the meaning of 'bill-absar'.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
The vision of Allah could be with the pupils, the whole face, or the entire body.

After establishing the two stations, the passage turns to the interpretation of the phrase 'bill-absar' found in the author's poem. This lesson explores these three opinions.

Seeing Allah Without How: Refuting the Mu'tazilite Objection
Sanusi explains 'bila kayf' and refutes the Mu'tazilite rational argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'bila kayf' in the context of the beatific vision and how the Sunni response refutes the Mu'tazilite rational argument against seeing Allah.

Core Idea
Vision of Allah is possible without spatial conditions; they are only habitual, not rational.

The author of the Jawhara affirms that believers will see Allah in the afterlife, but immediately qualifies it with 'but without how' to prevent any anthropomorphic misunderstanding. This opens the door to a major theological dispute: the Mu'tazilites argued that vision necessarily requires facing and direction, which are impossible for Allah. In this passage, Sanusi explains the meaning of 'bila kayf' and provides a concise refutation of the Mu'tazilite rational argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'bila kayf'
Read the opening explanation of 'bila kayf' carefully. It means the seen (Allah) is not conditioned by any modality of contingent things like facing, direction, or space.
This is a corrective to a potential misinterpretation of the earlier statement that Allah is seen.
2
Grasp the Mu'tazilite argument
Identify the Mu'tazilite objection summarized: if Allah were seen, He would have to face the seer and thus be in a direction and space—impossible for the transcendent Creator.
The author presents their argument fairly before refuting it.
3
Learn the Sunni response
Notice that the Sunni answer denies the necessity of facing for vision. Vision is a power Allah creates in creatures without requiring the seen to be facing or localized.
The denial is of the claim of necessity, not of the fact of vision.
4
Distinguish habitual vs rational necessity
Understand the crucial point: the Mu'tazilite objection mistakenly treats a habitual condition (facing) as a rational necessity. Many rational thinkers disagree, so it cannot be a self-evident truth.
This distinction refutes the charge of impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : لَكِنْ بِلا كَيْف لما كان قد يُتوهم من قوله : (ومنه أن ينظر بالأبصار) أنه تعالى يُرى بكيف كما في رؤية بعضنا بعضاً، استدرك عليه بقوله (لكن بلا كيف)؛ أي: بلا تكيف للمرئي بكيفية من كيفيات الحوادث؛ من مقابلة وجهة وتحيز وغير ذلك، وغرض المصنف بذلك الجواب عن شبهة المعتزلة العقلية التي تمسكوا بها في قولهم بإحالة الرؤية . و حاصلها : أنَّه تعالى لو كان مرتباً[؟] لكان مقابلاً للرائي بالضرورة، فيكون في جهة وحيز، وحاصل الجواب : أن قولكم : لكان مقابلاً للرائي بالضرورة ممنوع ، فلزوم الجهة والحيز ممنوع إذ الرؤية قوة يجعلها الله في خلقه لا يشترط فيها مقابلة المرئي ولا كونه في جهة وحيز ولا غير ذلك، ودعوى الضرورة فيما نازع فيه الجمُّ الغفير من العقلاء غير مسموعة. غاية الأمر أنَّ هذه الأمور لازمةٌ عادةً لا عقلاً.
His saying: 'but without how' (bila kayf) — because it might be understood from his saying 'and that He is seen with the eyes' that Allah is seen with a 'how' as in the seeing of some of us others, so he corrected it with his saying 'but without how'; that is, without the seen being conditioned by any modality from the modalities of contingent things, such as facing, direction, occupying space, etc. The author's purpose with that is to answer the rational doubt of the Mu'tazila which they cling to in their saying that vision is impossible. And its summary: that if He were seen[?], He would necessarily face the seer, thus be in a direction and space. And the summary of the answer: that your saying 'He would necessarily face the seer' is denied, so the necessity of direction and space is denied, because vision is a power that Allah creates in His creatures, which does not require the seen to be facing nor be in a direction or space, etc. The claim of necessity in something about which the great multitude of rational people have disputed is not heard. At most, these things are necessary habitually ('ādatan) not rationally ('aqlan).
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The author first explains the phrase 'bila kayf' (without how) as a response to a potential misunderstanding: some might think that seeing Allah in the afterlife involves a modality similar to seeing physical objects (e.g., facing, direction). The author clarifies that it means the seen (Allah) is not conditioned by any modality of contingent things like direction or space. Then the Mu'tazilite rational argument is summarized: if Allah were seen, He would have to face the seer, thus be in a direction and space, which is impossible for Allah. The author responds by denying the premise that facing the seer is necessary for vision; rather, vision is a power Allah creates without requiring facing or spatial location. The claim of logical necessity is rejected because many rational thinkers have disagreed; at most, these conditions are habitual (necessary by custom) not rational necessity.

Key Terms
بلا كيف
شبهة
المعتزلة
مقابلة
جهة
حيز
ضرورة
عادة
عقلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision of Allah in the afterlife necessarily requires facing and direction, making it impossible for the transcendent Creator.
Correction: Vision is a power Allah creates in His creatures; it does not require the seen to face or be localized. The alleged necessity is only habitual, not rational.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunni response to the Mu'tazilite rational argument, is facing the seer a rational necessity for vision?
A) Yes
B) No, it is only a habitual necessity
C) No, it is impossible
D) Yes, it is a logical requirement

Key Takeaway
The beatific vision of Allah is affirmed without modality, and the Mu'tazilite rational objection is refuted by denying that spatial conditions are rationally necessary for vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Rational Proof for the Possibility of Ru'ya
Provides a syllogistic proof for the possibility of seeing Allah using the verse of Moses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the syllogistic proof for the possibility of seeing Allah based on the verse of Moses, and the Mu'tazila objection and its refutation.

Core Idea
The vision of Allah is possible because it is conditioned on a possible event.

This lesson examines the rational proof for the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) as presented by the author. The proof uses a conjunctive syllogism derived from Allah's statement to Moses: 'If the mountain remains in its place, then you will see Me.' The Mu'tazila objected by claiming the condition was impossible, but the author refutes their claim as baseless.

Guided Path
1
Understand the grammatical opening
The author begins by parsing the phrase 'idh bi-ja'izin 'ulliqat'. Notice that 'idh' is explanatory and introduces the reason for the judgment. The phrase 'bi-ja'izin' is attached to the verb 'ulliqat', indicating what the vision is conditioned upon.
This grammatical analysis sets up the logical argument.
2
Grasp the syllogistic argument
The author presents a conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani). The minor premise is that the vision of the Creator is conditioned upon a possible matter (the mountain's stability). The major premise (implied) is that whatever is conditioned on the possible is itself possible. Therefore, the vision is possible.
The major premise is omitted because it is considered known.
3
Review the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila deny the minor premise, claiming that the mountain was moving, so the condition was impossible. They argue that if the condition is impossible, the conditioned (vision) is also impossible.
This objection challenges the validity of the minor premise.
4
See the refutation
The author dismisses the Mu'tazila claim as baseless, noting that there is no evidence for it. He compares it to their unfounded interpretation that 'lan' in the verse indicates eternal negation.
The refutation relies on the lack of support for their claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إِذْ بِجَائِز عُلِّقَتْ) بسكون الزاي للوزن، و(إذ تعليليَّة داخلة على (علقت)، و (بجائز) متعلق به، فكأنه قال : حكمنا بجواز الرؤية عقلاً؛ لأنَّ الله تعالى علقها بأمرٍ جائز عقلاً، وهو استقرار الجبل حين سأله موسى على نبينا وعليه أفضل الصلاة والسلام، حيث قال ﴿رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ قَالَ لَن تَرَانِي وَلَكِنِ انظُرْ إِلَى الْجَبَلِ فَإِنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ فَسَوْفَ تَرَانِي﴾ [الأعراف: ١٤٣]، والاستدلال بالآية من وجهين : الأول: ما أشار إليه المصنف وحاصله قياس اقتراني أشار إلى صغراه، وحذف كبراه؛ للعلم بها كالنتيجة . وتقريره أن تقول : رؤية الباري عُلّقت على أمر ممكن، وكلُّ ما عُلِّق على الممكن لا يكون إلا ممكناً فرؤية الباري لا تكون إلا ممكنة ومنعت المعتزلة الصغرى قائلين : إن المراد فإن استقر مكانه حال تحركه، وهو مستحيل، فالرؤية مُعلَّقةً على مستحيل فتكون مستحيلة، وهو تقول لا دليل عليه ولا داعي يدعو إليه، كقولهم: إنَّ (لن) في قوله تعالى : لَن تَرَانِي [الأعراف : ١٤٣] للتأبيد.
His saying: 'Since it was attached to a permissible thing' with sukoon on the zay for meter, and 'idh' is explanatory entering upon 'attached', and 'bi-ja'izin' is attached to it, as if he said: We judged the permissibility of vision rationally because Allah Almighty conditioned it upon a rationally permissible matter, namely the stability of the mountain when Moses, may the best prayers and peace be upon our Prophet and him, asked Him, when He said: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You. He said: You will not see Me, but look at the mountain; if it remains in its place, then you will see Me' [Al-A'raf: 143]. And the inference from the verse is from two aspects: The first is what the author referred to, and its gist is a conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) that signaled its minor premise and omitted its major premise due to knowledge of it like the conclusion. Its formulation is: The vision of the Creator is conditioned upon a possible matter, and everything conditioned upon the possible is only possible, so the vision of the Creator is only possible. The Mu'tazila denied the minor premise, saying: What is meant is 'if it remains in its place while it is moving', which is impossible, so vision is conditioned upon an impossible thing and thus becomes impossible. And this is a claim with no evidence and no reason to advocate it, like their saying that 'lan' in His statement 'You will not see Me' [Al-A'raf: 143] denotes eternal negation.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the possibility of seeing Allah, presented as a commentary on the line 'idh bi-ja'izin 'ulliqat'. It begins by parsing the phrase: 'idh' is explanatory, attached to the condition of a permissible thing. The author then states the substantive argument: Allah conditioned the vision of Moses on the stability of the mountain, which is a possible event (jā'iz 'aqlan). This forms the minor premise of a conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani): the vision of the Creator is conditioned on a possible matter. The major premise, taken as known, is that whatever is conditioned on the possible is itself possible. Therefore, the vision of the Creator is possible. The Mu'tazila object by claiming that the mountain was actually moving (at the moment of asking), so the condition was impossible, thus vision is impossible. The author dismisses this as an unsubstantiated claim, likening it to their baseless interpretation that 'lan' in the verse indicates eternal negation. The passage follows the order: grammatical note, statement of the argument, syllogistic formulation, Mu'tazila objection, and refutation.

Key Terms
جواز الرؤية
القياس الاقتراني
الممكن
المستحيل
المعتزلة
استقرار الجبل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila claim that the condition in the verse (the mountain's stability) was actually impossible because the mountain was moving at the time.
Correction: The verse does not indicate that the mountain was moving; it presents a hypothetical condition. The condition of stability is a rationally possible event, and the Mu'tazila claim is an unsupported assertion.

Checkpoint
What is the minor premise of the syllogism as presented?
A) Everything conditional on the possible is possible.
B) The vision of Allah is conditional on a possible event.
C) The vision of Allah is impossible.
D) The mountain was moving.

Key Takeaway
The rational proof for the possibility of seeing Allah is grounded in the conditional in the verse of Moses, and the Mu'tazila objection lacks evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just understood that the vision of Allah is possible because He conditioned it on a possible event. If you truly internalized that seeing your Lord is a real hope, would your life look any different tonight? What would you do tomorrow to prepare for that encounter?
?
The Mu'tazila rejected this possibility based on an assumption they could not prove. Are there any truths about Allah's mercy, His promise, or His attributes that you have pushed away because of a baseless assumption you inherited or invented? Will you examine that assumption today and ask for guidance?
?
The proof hinges on a verse where Allah says to Moses, 'You will not see Me, but look at the mountain...' — a conditional that leaves the door open. Does your heart feel the weight of this: that seeing Allah is not just a theological possibility but a personal hope? If it is possible, then the greatest joy is real. What are you doing right now that shows you are living for that joy?

The First Proof for the Possibility of Seeing Allah: The Syllogism from Moses' Verse
Author presents a categorical syllogism from Quran 7:143 proving ru'ya is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse of Moses (7:143) provides a rational proof that seeing Allah is possible, through a conjunctive syllogism, and how the Mu'tazila objection is refuted.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is possible because it is tied to a possible condition.

This lesson unpacks the first proof for the rational possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) based on the Quranic dialogue between Moses and Allah. The author presents a logical syllogism where the minor premise is explicit and the major premise is implied, then addresses the Mu'tazila counterargument. By working through this passage, you will see how scriptural evidence is structured into a formal rational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic and English translation to get a sense of the author's argument. Notice the key terms like 'قياس اقتراني' (conjunctive syllogism) and 'صغرى' (minor premise).
2
Identify the syllogism's components
The author says the syllogism has a minor premise (صغرى) that is stated and a major premise (كبرى) that is omitted because it is well-known. Identify both. The conclusion is that vision of Allah is possible.
The minor premise is: 'Ru'ya is tied to a possible matter.' The major premise is: 'Everything tied to a possible matter is possible.'
3
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila denied the minor premise by reinterpreting the condition. They claimed 'if it remains in its place' means 'while it is moving', which is impossible. Therefore, the condition is impossible, making ru'ya impossible.
The author calls this a 'mere claim with no evidence' (تَقَول لا دليل عليه).
4
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why the author dismisses the Mu'tazila interpretation as baseless. Compare it to their other claim about 'lan' indicating eternal negation. The author asserts that the plain meaning is clear and that the condition is indeed possible.
This shows the importance of taking the apparent meaning of the Quran unless there is strong evidence for reinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إِذْ بِجَائِز عُلِّقَتْ) بسكون الزاي للوزن، و(إذ تعليليَّة داخلة على (علقت)، و (بجائز) متعلق به، فكأنه قال : حكمنا بجواز الرؤية عقلاً؛ لأنَّ الله تعالى علقها بأمرٍ جائز عقلاً، وهو استقرار الجبل حين سأله موسى على نبينا وعليه أفضل الصلاة والسلام، حيث قال رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ قَالَ لَن تَرَانِي وَلَكِنِ انظُرْ إِلَى الْجَبَلِ فَإِنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ فَسَوْفَ تَرَانِي [الأعراف: ١٤٣]، والاستدلال بالآية من وجهين : الأول: ما أشار إليه المصنف وحاصله قياس اقتراني أشار إلى صغراه، وحذف كبراه؛ للعلم بها كالنتيجة . وتقريره أن تقول : رؤية الباري عُلّقت على أمر ممكن، وكلُّ ما عُلِّق على الممكن لا يكون إلا ممكناً فرؤية الباري لا تكون إلا ممكنة ومنعت المعتزلة الصغرى قائلين : إن المراد فإن استقر مكانه حال تحركه، وهو مستحيل، فالرؤية مُعلَّقةً على مستحيل فتكون مستحيلة، وهو تَقَول لا دليل عليه ولا داعي يدعو إليه، كقولهم: إنَّ (لن) في قوله تعالى : لَن تَرَانِي [الأعراف : ١٤٣] للتأبيد
His saying: (إِذْ بِجَائِز عُلِّقَتْ) with sukun on the zā' for meter, and 'إذ' is explanatory entering upon 'عُلِّقَتْ', and 'بجائز' is attached to it. So it is as if he said: We judged the permissibility of seeing (Allah) rationally because Allah, exalted, tied it to a rationally permissible matter, which is the settling of the mountain when Moses, upon our Prophet and him be the best prayer and peace, asked Him when he said: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You.' He said: 'You will not see Me, but look at the mountain; if it remains in its place, then you will see Me' [Al-A'raf 7:143]. The deduction from the verse is from two aspects: The first is what the author indicated, and its essence is a conjunctive syllogism in which he indicated its minor premise and omitted its major premise due to its being known like the conclusion. Its formulation is: Seeing the Creator is tied to a possible matter, and everything tied to the possible is not except possible, so seeing the Creator is not except possible. The Mu'tazila denied the minor premise, saying: The meaning is 'if it remains in its place while it is moving,' which is impossible, so seeing is tied to an impossible matter, thus it is impossible. But that is a mere claim with no evidence and no cause to call for it, like their saying that 'lan' in His statement 'You will not see Me' [Al-A'raf 7:143] indicates eternal negation.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the first proof for the rational possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya), based on the verse of Moses (Quran 7:143). The author begins by commenting on the poem's line 'إِذْ بِجَائِز عُلِّقَتْ' (since it is tied to a permissible thing). He explains that the word 'إذ' (when/since) is explanatory, and 'بجائز' (by a permissible thing) is the object of the verb 'عُلِّقَتْ' (was tied). The meaning is that we judge the permissibility of seeing Allah rationally because Allah tied it to a rationally permissible thing, namely the settling of the mountain. The author then quotes the verse. He explains that the deductive argument from the verse has two aspects. The first aspect is a conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani), which the poet alluded to by mentioning only the minor premise, omitting the major premise because it is well-known like the conclusion. The syllogism is: (1) Seeing the Creator is tied to a possible matter (the mountain settling). (2) Everything tied to a possible matter is itself possible. (3) Therefore, seeing the Creator is possible. The Mu'tazila objected by denying the minor premise. They interpreted 'if it remains in its place' as referring to its settling while it is (in a state of) moving, which is impossible, so the condition is impossible and thus the conditional (seeing) is impossible. The author dismisses this as a mere claim (taqawwul) with no evidence or compelling reason. He compares it to their other claim that the word 'lan' in the verse indicates eternal negation (i.e., that Moses will never see Allah even in the afterlife). Thus, the author upholds the validity of the proof and refutes the Mu'tazila objection as baseless.

صغرى
كبرى
ممكن
محال
جائز
تأبيد
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila claim that the condition in the verse is impossible, making ru'ya impossible.
Correction: The author argues that the condition (mountain settling) is inherently possible, and the Mu'tazila reinterpretation is an arbitrary claim with no evidence.

Checkpoint
What is the major premise that the author says is omitted in the conjunctive syllogism?
A) Seeing the Creator is possible.
B) Everything tied to a possible matter is possible.
C) The mountain can settle.
D) Moses asked to see Allah.

Key Takeaway
The verse of Moses provides a clear rational syllogism: since ru'ya is tied to a possible condition (mountain settling), it must itself be possible, and the Mu'tazila reinterpretation is a baseless claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If seeing Allah is rationally possible, why do you not yearn for it with all your heart? What worldly attachments hold you back from longing for the vision of your Lord?
?
The Mu'tazila rejected a clear syllogism based on a baseless assumption. Where in your own life do you resist a truth because it conflicts with your preconceived notions? Are you willing to submit your intellect to revelation?
?
Moses asked to see Allah, and Allah responded with a condition. How does your own desire to 'see' Allah manifest in your worship? Do you seek Him sincerely, or are you content with the mere formality of faith?

The Proof from Moses' Request and Refutation of the Mu'tazila
Conditional syllogism from Quran 7:143 with Mu'tazila refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hypothetical syllogism from Moses' request proves the permissibility of seeing Allah, and why the Mu'tazila's objection fails.

Core Idea
A prophet would not ask for the impossible; Moses asked, so seeing Allah is possible.

Sanusi's second proof for the possibility of ru'yat Allah uses a logical argument from the Quran. He argues that since Moses, a prophet who knows what is possible for Allah, asked to see Him, it cannot be impossible. This passage also refutes the Mu'tazila claim that Moses asked only for his people, showing the verse is about his own desire.

Guided Path
1
Understand the syllogism structure
Identify the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion of the conditional syllogism presented.
The major premise is a conditional statement: if impossible, then Moses would not ask.
2
Recognize prophets' knowledge
Note the key claim that prophets know what is necessary, impossible, and permissible regarding Allah, so they would not ask for the impossible.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila objection and its refutation
Understand the Mu'tazila argument that Moses asked for his people, and see how Sanusi refutes it by citing the verse's explicit self-reference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: سكت عنه المصنف . وحاصله قياس استثنائي، وتقريره هكذا : لو كانت الرؤية ممتنعة في الدنيا ما سألها موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام؛ لأنَّه نبيٌّ يَعلمُ ما يجب في حق الله وما يستحيلُ وما يجوز، إذ لا يجوز على أحد من الأنبياء الجهل بشيء من أحكام الألوهية، لكنه سألها موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام فدلَّ على أنَّها جائزة، وقول المعتزلة : سألها لأجل جهلة قومه، مردودٌ بأن سياق الآية حيث قال: ﴿رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ﴾ [الأعراف: ١٤٣] صريح في حال نفسه.
And the second: the author remained silent about it. Its essence is a hypothetical syllogism, and its formulation is as follows: If seeing (Allah) were impossible in this world, Moses, peace be upon him, would not have asked for it; because he is a prophet who knows what is necessary, impossible, and permissible with respect to Allah, since it is not permissible for any of the prophets to be ignorant of anything from the rulings of divinity. But Moses, peace be upon him, asked for it, so that indicates that it is permissible. The statement of the Mu'tazila that he asked for it due to the ignorance of his people is refuted by the fact that the context of the verse where he said: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You' [Al-A'raf 7:143] is explicit about his own self.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents the second deductive proof (al-thani) that the author (al-Sawi) did not include in the poem but explains here. It is a hypothetical syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). The argument is: (1) If seeing Allah were impossible in this world, then Moses (as a prophet) would not have asked for it, because prophets know what is necessary, impossible, and permissible regarding Allah. (2) But Moses did ask to see Allah. (3) Therefore, seeing Allah is permissible (ja'iz). The author refutes the Mu'tazila's objection that Moses asked only to satisfy the ignorance of his people (i.e., he himself knew it was impossible but asked to show them). The refutation is that the verse itself is explicit: Moses said 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You' – this is a request for himself (sarih fi hali nafsihi), not on behalf of others. Thus, the Mu'tazila's claim is invalid.

Key Terms
قياس استثنائي
ممتنع
جائز
أحكام الألوهية
سياق الآية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses asked to see Allah only for his people's sake, not because he himself thought it possible.
Correction: The verse shows Moses asked for himself: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You' is explicit about his own desire.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why would Moses not have asked for seeing Allah if it were impossible?
A) Because he would fear punishment.
B) Because prophets know what is possible and impossible for Allah.
C) Because he already saw Allah in a vision.
D) Because his people would not believe him.

Key Takeaway
The argument from Moses' request is a strong proof that seeing Allah is permissible, and the Mu'tazila objection fails because the verse explicitly refers to Moses himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that seeing Allah is possible? Have you ever yearned to see Him with the same intensity as Moses, or have you settled for a distant faith that never expects such intimacy?
?
Moses, a prophet who knew Allah's attributes, still asked to see Him. When was the last time you, in full knowledge of Allah's greatness, dared to ask Him for something seemingly impossible? What holds you back?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed Moses asked for others, not himself — an excuse that softens the request. Are there areas in your worship where you ask on behalf of others to avoid the raw personal vulnerability of asking for yourself? What would it look like to make your next du'a solely about your own longing for Allah?

Proving the Possibility of Seeing Allah: Moses' Request
Sanusi presents a hypothetical syllogism from Moses' request.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a hypothetical syllogism based on Moses' request demonstrates the logical possibility of seeing Allah, and how the Mu'tazila objection is refuted.

Core Idea
Moses' request proves vision is possible because prophets know what is impossible.

This lesson unpacks a key rational proof for the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) in this world. Scholars such as Sanusi present a hypothetical syllogism rooted in the story of Moses. By following the logical steps, you will see how the proof works and why a common objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hypothetical syllogism
Read the syllogism carefully: if vision were impossible, Moses would not ask; Moses did ask; therefore it is possible. Note the logical structure.
This is a conditional proof.
2
Examine the premise about prophets' knowledge
The argument relies on the premise that prophets know what is obligatory, possible, and impossible for Allah. Understand why this premise is crucial.
Without this, the syllogism would not hold.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila objection and its refutation
The Mu'tazila claim Moses asked on behalf of his ignorant people. The author refutes this by citing the verse context. See how the refutation strengthens the proof.
This shows the importance of scriptural context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: سكت عنه المصنف . و حاصله قياس استثنائي، وتقريره هكذا : لو كانت الرؤية ممتنعة في الدنيا ما سألها موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام؛ لأنَّه نبيٌّ يَعلمُ ما يجب في حق الله وما يستحيلُ وما يجوز، إذ لا يجوز على أحد من الأنبياء الجهل بشيء من أحكام الألوهية، لكنه سألها موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام فدلَّ على أنَّها جائزة، وقول المعتزلة : سألها لأجل جهلة قومه، مردودٌ بأن سياق الآية حيث قال: ﴿رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ﴾ [الأعراف: ١٤٣] صريح في حال نفسه.
And the second: the author remained silent about it. Its essence is a hypothetical syllogism, and its formulation is thus: If vision were impossible in this world, Moses (peace be upon him) would not have asked for it; because he is a prophet who knows what is obligatory for Allah, what is impossible, and what is permissible, for it is not permitted for any prophet to be ignorant of any of the rulings of divinity. But Moses (peace be upon him) asked for it, so it indicates that it is permissible. And the statement of the Mu'tazila: 'He asked it because of the ignorance of his people' is refuted by the context of the verse where He said: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You' [al-A'raf: 143], which is explicit concerning his own state.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof (the second of two proofs the author omits to detail) in the form of a hypothetical syllogism (قياس استثنائي). The argument runs: if seeing Allah in this world were impossible, Prophet Moses would not have asked for it—because prophets know what is permissible, obligatory, and impossible regarding Allah. Since Moses did ask, this shows vision is permissible. The Mu'tazila object that Moses asked on behalf of his ignorant people, not for himself. The author refutes this by citing the verse itself, which states 'My Lord, show me that I may look at You'—clear that Moses spoke for himself. The proof thus establishes the logical possibility (جواز) of vision, not its actual occurrence.

Misconception: The proof depends on Moses being ignorant of Allah's attributes.
Correction: The proof actually relies on Moses' knowledge as a prophet; he knew vision was not impossible.

Key Takeaway
The logical possibility of seeing Allah is established by Moses' request, which a prophet would not make if it were impossible.

Deriving 41 Beliefs from Self-Sufficiency and Need
Author calculates 23 beliefs from istighna' and 18 from iftiqar, totaling 41.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concepts of divine self-sufficiency (al-istighna') and need (al-iftiqar) logically entail 41 specific beliefs about God, covering the three rational judgments: necessary, impossible, and possible.

Core Idea
Self-sufficiency and need yield 41 beliefs: 20 necessary, 20 impossible, 1 possible.

In this passage, the author systematically enumerates the beliefs (aqa'id) that are logically derived from two fundamental concepts: God's absolute self-sufficiency (al-istighna') and His being the one to whom all things are in need (al-iftiqar). By carefully tracing the implications of each concept, we arrive at a total of 41 beliefs that cover the three categories of rational judgment—necessary, impossible, and possible—regarding Allah. This enumeration forms a cornerstone for understanding the divine attributes according to Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two foundational concepts
First, grasp the meaning of al-istighna' (divine self-sufficiency) and al-iftiqar (the world's need for God). These are the two principles from which all 41 beliefs are derived.
Self-sufficiency means God has no need for anything; need means everything depends on Him.
2
Trace the implications of self-sufficiency
Follow the author's reasoning: if God is self-sufficient, He must possess certain attributes (necessary), cannot have their opposites (impossible), and is not compelled to do or leave any possible act (possible). Count the beliefs: 11 necessary, 11 impossible, 1 possible = 23.
3
Trace the implications of need
From the concept of need, the author derives nine necessary attributes (including life, power, will, knowledge, oneness) and nine impossible opposites. That gives 18 beliefs.
Note the reference to the doctrine of states (al-ahwal).
4
Combine and classify the total beliefs
Add the 23 from self-sufficiency to the 18 from need to get 41. Then classify them: 20 necessary, 20 impossible, 1 possible. See how this covers all three rational judgments.
5
Reflect on the rational judgments
Understand that this enumeration is not arbitrary but follows strict logical entailment. It demonstrates the structure of i'tiqad (creed) regarding Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالأحوال (۱) إذ لو لم تجب له هذه الصفات، لكان محتاجاً إلى المحدث أو المحل أو مَنْ يدفع عنه النقائص، فهذه إحدى عشرة عقيدة من الواجبات، وإذا وجبت هذه الصفات استحالت أضدادها، فهذه إحدى عشرة عقيدة من المستحيلات. ويستلزم أيضاً نفي وجوب فعل شيء من الممكنات أو تركه، وإلا لزم افتقاره إلى فعل ذلك الشيء أو تركه ليتكمل به فهذه عقيدة الجائز، فجملة ما يستلزمه الاستغناء ثلاثة وعشرون عقيدة. وأما الافتقار فيستلزم الحياة والقدرة والإرادة والعلم ولوازمها وهي كونه حيا وقادراً ومريداً وعالماً بناء على القول بالأحوال، ويستلزم أيضاً الوحدانية، فهذه تسعة العقائد الواجبات ومتى وجبت هذه الصفات استحالت أضدادها، فهذه تسعةٌ من العقائد المستحيلات، فجملة ما استلزمه الافتقار ثمانية عشرة عقيدة، فإذا ضُمَّت للثلاثة والعشرين السابقة كان المجموع واحداً وأربعين، الواجب له تعالى منها عشرون والمستحيل عليه عشرون والجائز عليه واحدٌ، فقد اشتملت الجملة الأولى على أقسام الحكم العقلى الثلاثة الراجعة له تعالى.
By way of states (1): for if these attributes were not necessarily true of Him, He would be in need of an originator, a locus, or one who repels deficiencies from Him. So these are eleven beliefs from the necessary. And when these attributes are necessarily true, their opposites are impossible, so these are eleven beliefs from the impossible. And it also entails the negation of the necessity of doing any possible thing or leaving it, otherwise the neediness to do that thing or leave it to become perfect would be necessary. So this is the belief of the possible. So the total of what self-sufficiency entails is twenty-three beliefs. As for need, it entails life, power, will, knowledge, and their concomitants, which are that He is living, powerful, willing, knowing, based on the doctrine of states. And it also entails oneness. So these are nine beliefs from the necessary. And when these attributes are necessarily true, their opposites are impossible, so these are nine from the impossible beliefs. So the total of what need entails is eighteen beliefs. So when they are added to the previous twenty-three, the total is forty-one: the necessary for Him, exalted, from them is twenty, and the impossible for Him is twenty, and the possible for Him is one. So the first summary has included the three divisions of rational judgment relating to Him, exalted.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the beliefs (aqa'id) derived from the two fundamental concepts of divine self-sufficiency (al-istighna') and need (al-iftiqar). The author first explains the implications of self-sufficiency: if God were not necessarily characterized by certain attributes (like life, power, etc.), He would be dependent. Therefore, eleven attributes are necessary (wajib) for Him, and their opposites are impossible (mustahil), giving eleven impossible beliefs. Additionally, self-sufficiency entails that no possible action or omission is necessary for God, leading to one possible (ja'iz) belief. The total from self-sufficiency is 23 beliefs. Then the author turns to the concept of need (al-iftiqar) which implies that God must have life, power, will, knowledge, and their concomitants (being living, powerful, willing, knowing) based on the doctrine of states (al-ahwal), plus oneness, totaling nine necessary beliefs. Their opposites are nine impossible beliefs. This gives 18 beliefs from need. Adding the two sets yields 41 beliefs: 20 necessary, 20 impossible, and 1 possible. The passage concludes that this first summary (al-jumla al-ula) encompasses the three rational judgments regarding God.

Key Terms

الافتقار
الأحوال
الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز
العقائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The numbers 23, 18, and 41 are arbitrary or merely conventional.
Correction: The numbers are strictly derived through logical entailment from the concepts of self-sufficiency and need, as explained in the passage.

Checkpoint
How many beliefs are derived from al-istighna' (self-sufficiency)?
A) 23
B) 18
C) 20
D) 41

Key Takeaway
The 41 beliefs about Allah are not arbitrary but are logically necessitated by His self-sufficiency and the world's need for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is absolutely self-sufficient, why do you still worry about your provision? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized that He needs nothing from you, yet you need everything from Him? Commit to one specific worry you will surrender to Him today.
?
This lesson shows that 20 things are necessary about Allah. Which of these—like His being living, powerful, or one—do you most often forget in your prayer? How would your khushu' increase if you reminded yourself of one of these attributes before each sujood? Choose one to focus on this week.
?
The possible belief is that no action or omission is necessary for Allah's perfection. Are there any 'obligations' you have imposed on Allah—expectations of how He should treat you? Surrender one specific expectation today and acknowledge that His actions are purely by His will, not by your merit.
